Nicomachean Ethics

The original inquiry into the good life: happiness, virtue, friendship, and the mean

by Aristotle

c. 340 BC 2026

This is an AI modernization of Nicomachean Ethics into contemporary English. The original is available from Standard Ebooks. Also available as an epub.

Book I

The End

Chapter 1

Every art and every inquiry, and likewise every action and every choice, seems to aim at some good; and so it has been well said that the good is that at which everything aims.

But notice a difference among these aims, or ends. Sometimes what we are after is the activity itself; sometimes it is a result over and above the activity. And wherever there is an end beyond the act, the result is better than the activity that produces it.

Now since there are many kinds of action and many arts and sciences, there are many ends too: health is the end of medicine, a ship of shipbuilding, victory of the art of war, wealth of household management.

But often several of these are grouped under one master art or science — bridle-making and the other equipment trades under horsemanship; horsemanship, along with everything else a soldier does, under the art of war; and so on. In every such case the end of the master art is more desirable than the ends of the arts beneath it, since those are pursued for its sake. And this holds equally whether the end in question is the activity itself or something beyond it, as in the cases just mentioned.

Chapter 2

Suppose, then, that there is some end of our actions that we want for its own sake, choosing everything else as a means to it — and suppose we do not choose everything for the sake of something further, since then the chain would run on forever and desire would be empty and pointless. Clearly this end would be the good, the best of all things. And surely, from a practical standpoint, knowing it matters enormously: like archers with a definite mark to shoot at, we will be far more likely to hit what we want.

If this is right, we must try to sketch, at least roughly, what this good is — and first of all, which of the arts or sciences it belongs to.

It would seem to belong to the supreme science, the one that most of all deserves to be called the master science.

Politics looks like the answer. It is Politics that prescribes which sciences a state needs, which ones each citizen is to study, and how far; and we see even the most prestigious arts — household management, rhetoric, the art of war — placed under its authority.

Since Politics makes use of the other practical sciences, and since it further lays down what people are to do and what they are to refrain from doing, its end must embrace the ends of all the rest; so its end must be the proper good of man.

For even though this good is the same for a single person and for a state, the good of the state seems the grander and more complete thing, both to achieve and to preserve. It is a fine thing to secure it for one person; but to secure it for a whole people, and for whole states, is nobler and more divine.

This, then, is what the present inquiry aims at — and it is a kind of political inquiry.

Chapter 3

We must be satisfied with as much precision as our subject allows; the same exactness should no more be expected in every kind of reasoning than in every kind of craftsmanship.

Now the noble and the just — the things Politics deals with — vary so much, and are so uncertain, that some people conclude they exist only by convention, not by nature.

There is a similar uncertainty about good things, because good things often do people harm: men have been ruined by wealth before now, and have lost their lives through courage.

Since our subject and our starting materials are like this, we must be content to indicate the truth roughly and in outline. When we are dealing with things that admit of no fixed and unchanging rules, and reasoning from premises that are only probable, we must settle for conclusions that are only probable too.

The reader, for his part, should take each of my statements in the same spirit. It is the mark of an educated mind to expect, in each kind of inquiry, only as much exactness as the subject allows: it is just as absurd to accept probable reasoning from a mathematician as to demand rigorous proof from an orator.

Now each of us judges well what he knows, and that is what earns the name "a good judge" — a good judge of some special field when he has been educated in that field, a good judge without qualification when he has had a broad education. Hence a young man is not a fit student of Politics: he lacks experience of the business of life, and that experience is precisely the data and subject matter of Politics.

Besides, since he tends to be swept along by his feelings, the study will do him no good; for its aim is not knowledge but practice.

And here it makes no difference whether a man is young in years or merely young in character. The young man's disqualification is not a matter of time; it comes from living by feeling and chasing every desire as it arises. For people like that, knowledge turns out useless in practice — just as it does for those we call weak-willed. But for those who order their desires and their actions by reason, knowledge of these matters will be a great benefit.

So much, then, by way of preface: about the student, about the spirit in which what we say must be received, and about what we propose to do.

Chapter 4

To resume: since all knowledge and every choice aims at some good, what is this good that we say Politics aims at — what is the highest of all the goods that action can achieve?

As far as its name goes, nearly everyone agrees. Ordinary people and cultivated people alike call it happiness, and they take "living well" and "doing well" to mean the same thing as "being happy."

But they disagree about what happiness actually is, and the many do not give the same account of it as the wise.

The many take it to be something obvious and tangible — pleasure, or wealth, or fame. One man says this, another says that; often the same man says different things at different times: when he is sick, it is health; when he is poor, it is wealth. And when people feel the sting of their own ignorance, they admire most whoever says something grand that sails over their heads.

Some philosophers, on the other hand, have held that beyond all these particular good things there is an "absolute" good, which is the cause of the goodness in all the rest.

It would hardly be worthwhile to review every opinion ever held; we will confine ourselves to those that are most widespread, or that seem to have some reason on their side.

But we must not overlook the distinction between arguments that proceed from first principles and arguments that work up toward them. Plato was right to keep raising this question and asking which way the road runs — from the starting points or toward them — just as on a racecourse you may run from the judges to the far post, or back again.

Well: we must start from what is known.

But "what is known" means two things: what is known to us, which is one thing, and what is known absolutely, which is another.

I think it is safe to say that we must start from what is known to us.

And this is why nothing but a good moral upbringing can prepare a man to study the noble and the just — in a word, to study Politics. For here the undemonstrated fact is the starting point, and if the fact is sufficiently clear to a man, he will not need the reason why on top of it. Now the man with a good upbringing either already has the starting points of action or will easily accept them when they are pointed out. As for the man who neither has them nor will accept them, let him hear Hesiod:

  Best of all is the man who sees the truth himself;
  good, too, is the one who listens to wise advice;
  but the man who neither sees it for himself nor takes
  another's words to heart — he is of no use at all.

Chapter 5

Let us pick up the discussion at the point where we digressed.

It seems that people take their notions of the good, or happiness, from the lives actually lived — and not unreasonably. The many, the least refined, suppose it to be pleasure, which is why the life they aim at is the life of enjoyment.

For the most conspicuous kinds of life are three: this life of enjoyment, the life of the statesman, and, third, the life of contemplation.

The mass of men show themselves utterly slavish by preferring a life fit for cattle; but their view gets a hearing, because many of the people in high places share the tastes of Sardanapalus.

Cultivated men with a practical bent prefer honor; for honor, I suppose we may say, is the aim of the statesman's life.

But honor seems too shallow to be the good we are seeking. It appears to depend on the people who give it rather than on the man who receives it, while we have a hunch that the good is something peculiarly a man's own — something that can scarcely be taken away from him.

Moreover, men seem to pursue honor in order to reassure themselves of their own worth: at any rate, they want to be honored by sensible people, by people who know them, and on the ground of their virtue — their excellence. So plainly, in their own view at least, virtue is better than honor; and perhaps we should take virtue, rather than honor, to be the end of the statesman's life.

But even virtue looks too incomplete to be our answer. A man could possess virtue and yet be asleep, or inactive his whole life long; worse, he could meet with the greatest disasters and misfortunes. And no one would call such a man happy — except to defend a thesis for argument's sake. But we need not dwell on this now; it is discussed well enough in the popular writings.

The third kind of life is the life of contemplation. We will deal with it further on.

As for the moneymaking life, it is something forced and unnatural; and wealth is obviously not the good we are searching for, since it is merely useful — a means to something else. One would sooner take the earlier candidates — pleasure, honor, virtue — for ends, since they are prized for their own sake. But not even they are the end, though a great deal of breath has been wasted trying to show that they are.

Chapter 6

Setting these views aside, we now have to examine the "universal good" and state the difficulties it raises — not a pleasant task, given our friendship for the authors of the doctrine of Ideas. But we venture to think this is the right course: for the sake of truth we ought to sacrifice even what is nearest to us, especially since we call ourselves philosophers. Both are dear to us, but it is a sacred duty to give the preference to truth.

First, then: the very men who put forward this theory did not posit a common Idea for things of which one is prior to the other — which is why they held no common Idea of numbers. But the predicate "good" is applied to substances, and also to qualities and relations. Now what exists independently — what we call substance — is logically prior to what is relative; for the relative is a kind of offshoot, an accident of a thing or substance. So by their own principles there cannot be one common Idea covering all these goods.

Second, "good" is used in as many different senses as "is" or "being." We apply it to substances, or independent existences — God, reason; to qualities — the virtues; to quantity — the moderate or due amount; to relatives — the useful; to time — the opportune moment; to place — the right habitation; and so on. Clearly, then, the word "good" cannot stand for one and the same notion in all these applications; if it did, it could not be applied in all the categories, but in one only.

Third, if the notion were one, then — since there is one science for all the things that come under one Idea — there would be one science of all goods. But in fact there are many sciences even for goods within a single category. The science of the opportune moment in war is strategy; in disease, medicine. The science of the due amount in food is medicine; in exercise, gymnastics.

Fourth, one might ask what they even mean by this "absolute." In "absolute man" and "man," the word "man" has one and the same sense; as far as manhood goes, there is no difference between them. And if that is so, neither will there be any difference in goodness between "absolute good" and "good."

Fifth, they do not make the good any more good by making it eternal: a white thing that lasts a long while is no whiter than one that lasts a day.

There seems to be more plausibility in the doctrine of the Pythagoreans, who place the One in their column of goods rather than reducing all goods to unity; and even Speusippus seems to follow them here.

But these points can wait for another occasion. To what I have said, however, someone may object that the Platonists do not talk this way about all goods indiscriminately. Goods that are pursued and welcomed for their own sake, they hold, are called good by reference to one common Form, while things that tend to produce or preserve these goods, or to ward off their opposites, are called good only as means to them, in a different sense.

Evidently, then, goods will fall into two classes: things good in themselves, and things good as means to the former. So let us separate the goods-in-themselves from the merely useful, and ask whether the first class is called good by reference to one common Idea.

Now what kind of things would one call "good in themselves"?

Surely the things we pursue even apart from their consequences — wisdom, say, and sight, and certain pleasures and certain honors. For although we sometimes pursue these as means, no one could refuse to rank them among the things that are good in themselves.

If these are excluded, nothing is good in itself except the Idea — and then the Form will be empty and meaningless.

If, however, these things do belong among the goods-in-themselves, then it will have to be shown that the goodness of all of them answers to one and the same account, the way "white" means the same thing applied to snow and applied to white lead.

But in fact we have to give a separate and different account of the goodness of honor and wisdom and pleasure.

"Good," then, is not a term applied to all these things alike, in one sense, by reference to one common Idea or Form.

But then how do they all come to be called good? Surely not by a mere accident of naming. Perhaps because they all proceed from one source, or all contribute to one end; or perhaps rather by some analogy — just as we call reason the eye of the soul, because reason stands to the soul as the eye stands to the body, and so on.

But we may drop these questions for now; working them out in detail belongs more properly to another branch of philosophy.

And for the same reason we may drop any further consideration of the Idea. For even granting that this term "good," applied to so many different things, has one and the same meaning throughout — or that there exists an absolute good apart from all the particulars — it is evident that this good would not be anything a man can act on or attain. And it is a good of exactly that kind we are now seeking.

It might be thought, perhaps, that the universal good would still be worth knowing with a view to the goods that are attainable and achievable: with it as a pattern, the argument runs, we shall more readily discern our own good, and discerning it, achieve it.

There is certainly some plausibility in this argument, but it seems at odds with how the sciences actually work. All of them aim at some good and strive to fill their gaps, yet they leave the knowledge of this universal good alone. And it is hardly likely that every practitioner of every art and science would fail to know — would not even look for — something that would help them so much.

Indeed, I am at a loss to see how the weaver or the carpenter would be furthered in his own craft by knowing this absolute good, or how contemplating the pure Form would make anyone better at healing the sick or commanding an army. For it seems to me the doctor does not even seek health in this abstract way: he seeks the health of man — or rather of some particular man. For it is individuals he has to heal.

Chapter 7

Leaving these matters, then, let us return once more to the question: what can this good be that we are searching for?

It seems to be different in different actions and different arts — one thing in medicine, another in war, and so on. What, then, is the good in each of these? Surely that for the sake of which everything else is done. In medicine that is health; in war, victory; in building, a house — a different thing in each case, but always, in whatever we do and whatever we choose, it is the end. For it is always for the sake of the end that everything else is done.

If, then, there is one end of everything man does, this end will be the good achievable by action — or if there are several, then these.

By this route the argument comes around to the same point as before; and this point we must now try to state more clearly.

We see that there are many ends. But some of them are chosen only as means — wealth, flutes, instruments generally. So plainly not all ends are final.

But the best of all things must, we conceive, be something final.

If there is only one final end, then, this will be what we are seeking; if there are several, the most final of them.

Now what is pursued as an end in itself is more final than what is pursued as a means to something else; and what is never chosen as a means is more final than the things chosen both as ends in themselves and as means; in short, that is strictly final which is always chosen as an end in itself and never as a means.

Happiness, more than anything else, seems to answer to this description. Happiness we always choose for itself and never for the sake of anything else; while honor and pleasure and reason and every virtue we choose partly for themselves — for apart from any result, we would still choose each of them — but partly for the sake of happiness, on the supposition that they will help make us happy. Whereas no one chooses happiness for the sake of these things, or as a means to anything else at all.

We seem to be led to the same conclusion if we start from the notion of self-sufficiency.

The final good is thought to be self-sufficient. And by self-sufficient we do not mean sufficient for a man on his own, leading a solitary life; we take account of parents, children, wife — in short, friends and fellow citizens generally, since man is by nature a social being. Some limit has to be set here, of course; extend the circle to ancestors and descendants and friends of friends, and it never comes to a stop. But that question can wait. For now, let us take "self-sufficient" to mean: what by itself makes life desirable and lacking in nothing. And happiness is believed to be exactly that.

Further, happiness is believed to be the most desirable thing in the world — and not merely as one good thing among others. If it were merely one among others, then plainly the addition of even the least of other goods would make it more desirable; for the addition makes a surplus of good, and of two goods the greater is always the more desirable.

Happiness, then, appears to be something final and self-sufficient — the end of everything man does.

But perhaps the reader will say that while no one disputes that happiness is the best thing in the world, a still more precise definition of it is wanted.

The best way to get one, I think, is to ask: what is the function of man? For just as the goodness and excellence of a piper or a sculptor, or the practitioner of any art — of anyone, in general, who has a function or business to perform — lies in that function, so man's good would seem to lie in his function, if he has one.

But can we really suppose that while a carpenter and a cobbler each have a function and a business of their own, man himself has no business, no function assigned him by nature? Surely not. Just as his separate parts — eye, hand, foot — plainly each have their own function, so we must suppose that man too has some function over and above all these.

What, then, is it?

Merely being alive he evidently shares even with the plants, and we are looking for what is peculiar to him. So we must set aside the life of mere nutrition and growth.

Next comes the life of the senses; but this too he plainly shares with horses and cattle and animals of every kind.

There remains, then, the life of action — the life of his rational nature, which has two sides: one rational in the sense of obeying reason, the other rational in the sense of having and exercising reason.

But since this phrase is still ambiguous, let us be clear that we mean the life that consists in the active exercise of these powers; for that seems the truer sense of the term.

The function of man, then, is an activity of his soul: on one side obeying reason, on the other having and using it.

Now the function of a man in any line of work and the function of a good man in that line are generically the same — a harper and a good harper, for instance — and this holds in every case without exception; the good man simply adds his superior excellence at the work. A harper's function is to play the harp; a good harper's, to play it well.

(Man's function, then, being as we say a kind of life — namely an activity of the soul, and actions of various kinds carried out with reason — the good man's function is to do all this well and nobly. And the function of anything is done well when it is done in accordance with that thing's proper excellence.)

If this is so, the result is that the good of man is an activity of the soul in accordance with virtue — or, if there is more than one virtue, in accordance with the best and most complete of them.

But we must add: over a full span of years. One swallow does not make a spring, nor does one fine day; and in the same way one day, or any short stretch of time, does not make a man blessed or happy.

Let this stand, then, as our rough outline of the good. For this, I think, is the proper method: first sketch the outline, then fill in the details. And once the outline is fairly drawn, it would seem that anyone can carry on the work and fit in the particulars that time reveals to us or helps us find. That, indeed, is how the arts and sciences have grown; filling in the gaps takes no extraordinary genius.

We must remember, however, what was said above, and not demand the same degree of accuracy in every branch of study, but in each case only as much as the subject matter allows and as suits that kind of inquiry. The carpenter and the geometer both look for the right angle, but in different ways: the carpenter wants only as close an approximation as his work requires, while the geometer wants to know what a right angle is and what its special character is — his aim is the truth. We must follow the same course everywhere else, so that side issues do not end up taking more of our time than the real business at hand.

Nor must we demand the reason why in every case alike. Sometimes it is enough that the undemonstrated fact be fairly pointed out, as with the starting points, or first principles, of a science. The undemonstrated fact always forms the first step of a science; and these starting points are reached in different ways — some by induction, some by perception, some by a kind of training. In each case we must try to grasp them in the proper way, and take pains to define them clearly; for they carry great weight in everything that follows. The start, I think, is more than half the race; and once our starting point is found, it clears up a good number of our difficulties.

Chapter 8

We should not be content, then, to test this starting point of ours only against our premises and our conclusion; we must also test it against what people actually say about happiness. For everything in experience chimes with a true principle, while a false one soon collides with the facts.

Now, good things have long been sorted into three classes: external goods on one side, and on the other goods of the soul and goods of the body. And goods of the soul are commonly held to be goods in the fullest sense — more truly good than any of the others.

But "actions and activities of the soul" surely count as goods "of the soul." So our account is confirmed by this opinion — an old one, and endorsed by everyone who occupies himself with philosophy.

Indeed, our account secures the support of this opinion simply by saying that certain actions and activities are the end; for that ranks the end among the goods of the soul, not among external goods.

Our account also agrees with the common saying that the happy man "lives well" and "does well"; for happiness, as we have described it, just is a kind of living well and doing well.

More than that: everything people look for in happiness seems to be contained in happiness as we define it.

Some hold that happiness is virtue, or some particular virtue; some say practical wisdom; others a kind of wisdom; others again say all or some of these, with pleasure added, either as an ingredient or as a necessary accompaniment; and some even work external prosperity into their account.

Now, some of these views are held by many people and have age on their side; others by a few, but those few carry weight. And it is likely that neither camp is entirely wrong — that each is right on at least one point, if not on most.

First, then, the view that happiness is virtue, or some kind of virtue, harmonizes with our account; for "activity in accordance with virtue" surely belongs to virtue.

But I think it makes no small difference whether we conceive the good as mere possession or as use — as a habit or as the active exercise of that habit. For the habit can be present and yet produce nothing good, as when a man is asleep or otherwise kept from his function; the activity cannot. It must show itself in acts, and in good acts. Just as at the Olympic games it is not the finest and strongest bodies that are crowned but those who compete — for the victors come from among these — so in life too, the prizes go to those who not only have the virtues but put them into action.

Further, the life of such men is pleasant in itself. Pleasure is a state of the soul, and each man finds his pleasure in whatever he is said to love: the lover of horses in horses, the lover of sights in sightseeing — and in the same way, the lover of justice in just acts, and generally the lover of virtue in virtuous acts.

Now with most men, the things they take pleasure in are perpetually at war with one another, because those things are not pleasant by nature. But those who love the noble take pleasure in what is naturally pleasant; and virtuous acts are naturally pleasant, so that such acts are pleasant both to these men and in themselves.

Their life, then, has no need of pleasure fastened onto it as an accessory; it carries its pleasure within itself.

Indeed — to add one more point — a man is not even good unless he takes pleasure in noble deeds. No one would call a man just who did not enjoy acting justly, or generous who took no pleasure in acts of generosity; and so on down the list.

If this is so, then virtuous acts are pleasant in themselves. But they are also good and noble, and each of these in the highest degree — if the good man's judgment about them is right, and his judgment is as we have said.

Happiness, then, is at once the best and noblest and pleasantest thing in the world; and these three do not come apart, as the inscription at Delos would have it:

  Noblest is what is most just; best of all is health;
  but pleasantest is to win your heart's desire.

For all of them together belong to the best activities; and it is these activities — or the one among them that is best of all — that we identify with happiness.

And yet happiness plainly needs external goods too, as we said. For it is impossible, or at least not easy, to act nobly without some equipment of fortune. Many things can be done only with instruments, so to speak — friends, wealth, political influence. And there are some things whose absence takes the bloom off our happiness: good birth, good children, personal beauty. A man is not very likely to be happy if he is downright ugly, or of low birth, or alone in the world and childless — and perhaps less likely still if his children or friends are worthless, or if he had good ones and lost them.

As we said, then, happiness seems to require this kind of prosperity as well; which is why some identify happiness with good fortune, just as others identify it with virtue.

Chapter 9

This raises a further question: is happiness attained by learning, or by forming habits, or by some other kind of training? Or does it come by some divine dispensation — or even by chance?

Well, if the Gods give any gifts to men, happiness is likely to be among them — likelier, indeed, than anything else, in proportion as it is better than everything else that is human.

But this belongs more properly to another inquiry. Even if happiness is not heaven-sent, though — even if it comes through virtue or some kind of learning or training — it still seems to be one of the most divine things there is; for the prize and end of virtue is evidently something supremely good, something divine and blessed.

Again, if it is acquired in this way, it will be open to many; for then everyone not crippled for virtue will be able to win it by study and care.

And if it is better that men come to happiness this way rather than by chance, it is reasonable to suppose things really are so — since in nature everything is arranged as well as it can possibly be, and likewise in art, and in every kind of causation, above all in the highest kind. To hand over the greatest and noblest of things to chance would be far too jarring a note.

But our definition itself clears up the difficulty. Happiness was defined as an activity of the soul in accordance with virtue — or, if there is more than one virtue, in accordance with the best and most complete of them. Of the remaining goods — the goods other than happiness itself — some must be present as necessary conditions, while others are aids, useful instruments of happiness. And this agrees with what we said at the start. We laid it down that the end of Politics is the best of ends; but the chief business of Politics is to make the citizens people of a certain character — good, and apt to do what is noble. It is no accident, then, that we call no ox or horse or any other animal happy; for none of them can share in activity of this kind.

For the same reason, no child is happy either: his age keeps him, as yet, from doing such things. When we do call a child happy, it is because we hope he will do them. For, as we said, happiness requires not only complete virtue but also a full term of years for its exercise. Our circumstances are subject to many turns and to every sort of chance, and it is possible that the most prosperous man alive will meet with great disasters in his old age, as the tales of Troy tell of Priam; and a man who has been dealt fortune like that, and has come to a miserable end, no one calls happy.

Chapter 10

Are we, then, to call no man happy while he lives? Must we look to the end, as Solon said, and call no man happy until he is dead?

And if we do grant that, do we mean that a man actually is happy once he is dead? Surely that is absurd — especially for us, who say that happiness is a kind of activity, a kind of life.

But suppose we do not call the dead man happy, and suppose Solon meant not that, but only that when a man is dead you can at last pronounce him happy safely, as being now out of reach of evil and misfortune. Even this can be disputed. For people think that good and evil can, in some fashion, touch a dead man — just as they can touch a living man without his being aware of them — honors paid to him, for instance, and disgraces, and the successes and misfortunes of his children and descendants generally.

But this raises difficulties of its own. A man may have lived happily into old age and ended as he lived, and yet all sorts of reversals may come upon his descendants — some of them may be good people who meet the fortune they deserve, others the opposite. And clearly the descendants may stand at any degree of remove from their ancestor. It would be strange if the dead man changed along with all this, becoming happy and miserable by turns; but it would also be strange if the fortunes of his line did not reach the ancestors at all, even for some limited time.

But let us go back to our earlier question; its solution may clear up this difficulty too.

Suppose Solon's saying means that we should look to the end and only then call a man happy — not as being happy now, but as having been happy once.

Surely it is strange that at the very time when he is happy we should refuse to say of him what is true. We decline to call the living happy because their fortunes may change, and because we take happiness to be something enduring and hard to shake, while the wheel of fortune keeps turning for one and the same man. Obviously, the argument runs, if we track a man's fortunes, we shall call him happy and then miserable over and over, making the happy man "a sort of chameleon, one who rests on no sound foundation."

Our reply is that tracking fortune like this cannot be right. For our good and ill do not lie in fortune. Human life needs good fortune added to it, as we said; but it is virtuous activity that constitutes a man's happiness, as the contrary activity constitutes his misery.

In fact, the difficulty we have been discussing turns into further confirmation of our account. For nothing human is as stable as activity in accordance with virtue; such activities seem more lasting even than the sciences. And the highest of them are the most lasting of all, because the happy spend their lives in them most fully and most continuously — which seems to be exactly why we do not forget how to do them.

The happy man, then, as we define him, will have the permanence we are looking for, and will keep his character all through life. For he will always, or with the least possible interruption, be occupied in virtuous deeds and virtuous thinking; and whatever his fortune, he will bear it in the noblest fashion and carry himself fittingly always and in everything, since he is truly good — "foursquare, without a flaw."

But fortune deals out many things, some great, some small. The small ones, good or bad, plainly carry no weight in the scale. The great ones, when they come in numbers, will make life happier if they are good — for they add a grace of their own to life, and the use a man makes of them is itself noble and good; but if they are evil, they crush and maim happiness, for they bring pain and get in the way of many activities.

And yet even here nobility shines through — when a man bears many great misfortunes with calm, not because he feels nothing, but out of nobility and greatness of soul. And if it is a man's activities that set the character of his life, as we said, then no happy man can become miserable; for he will never do what is hateful and base. The truly good and sensible man, we hold, bears whatever fortune sends with dignity, and always makes the best of his circumstances — as a good general makes the most warlike use of the army he has, and a good shoemaker makes the best shoe that can be made from the leather he is given, and so on through all the crafts.

If this is so, the happy man will never become miserable — though neither will he be truly happy if he meets the fate of Priam.

Nor, on the other hand, is he shifting and changeable. He will not be dislodged from his happiness easily, or by ordinary misfortunes, but only by many heavy ones; and after such blows he will not recover his happiness quickly, but — if at all — only over a long and complete stretch of time, in which he wins his way to great and noble things.

We shall meet every objection, then, if we say that the happy man is "one whose activity accords with complete virtue, and who is adequately furnished with external goods, not for some chance stretch of time but for a full term of years" — adding, perhaps, "and who will go on living so, and will die as he lived." For the future is hidden from us, and happiness we take to be an end, something in every way final and complete.

If this is so, then those among the living who have these things, and will keep them, we shall call blessed — but blessed as men are blessed.

Chapter 11

So much for that question. Turning now to the fortunes of a man's descendants, and of his friends generally: the doctrine that none of it touches the dead at all seems too cold, and too much at odds with what people believe. But the things that happen to descendants and friends are many and various — some come home more, some less — and to work through them one by one would be a long business, indeed an endless one. It will be enough to speak in general terms, in outline only.

Now, just as some of a man's own misfortunes have a certain weight and pull on his life while others sit more lightly, so it is with what happens to any of his friends. And it always makes far more difference whether those whom an event touches are alive or dead — far more than it matters, in a tragedy, whether the terrible crime is committed on the stage or is merely presupposed to have happened before the play begins. So we must take this difference into our reckoning — and still more, perhaps, the standing doubt whether the dead share in any good or evil at all. For it appears that even if something does get through to them, good or bad, it is something faint and slight, either in itself or to them; or if not that, then at least of a size and kind that cannot make happy those who are not, nor take happiness from those who are.

The conclusion, then, seems to be this: the good fortune of friends, and likewise their misfortune, does affect the dead in some measure — but not in such a way, or to such a degree, as to make the happy unhappy, or anything of that kind.

Chapter 12

These points settled, we may next ask whether happiness belongs among the goods we praise, or rather among those we revere; for it is plainly not a mere capacity, but a good actually possessed.

Whatever we praise, we seem to praise as being of a certain quality and as standing in a certain relation to something. We praise the just man and the courageous man, and the good man generally, and virtue itself, because of the actions and the works they produce; and we praise the strong man and the swift runner and the rest because each has a certain natural gift, standing in a certain relation to something good and admirable.

This becomes obvious when we consider the praises offered to the Gods: the Gods are made ridiculous by being measured against our standard — and that happens because, as we said, praise always involves reference to something else.

If praise, then, belongs to things of that sort, it is clear that for the best things what is due is not praise but something greater and better — as, indeed, our usage shows. The Gods we call blessed and happy, and "blessed" is what we call the most godlike men.

So too with good things: no one praises happiness the way he praises justice; we call it blessed, as something better and more divine than the goods we praise.

Eudoxus is thought to have argued well on these lines when he pleaded the case for pleasure's claim to first prize. The fact that pleasure, though a good, is not praised shows — he maintained — that it stands above the goods that are praised, as God and the good stand above them; for these are the standards by which we judge everything else. Praise we give to virtue, since virtue makes us apt to do what is noble; encomiums we compose for its works, whether of body or of soul.

But refining these distinctions belongs more properly to those who have made a study of encomiums. For us it is plain from what has been said: happiness belongs among the goods that we revere and count as final.

And this seems to follow, further, from the fact that happiness is a first principle or starting point. It is for its sake that we do everything else we do; and the principle and cause of all goods we hold to be something divine and worthy of reverence.

Chapter 13

Since happiness is an activity of the soul in accordance with virtue — or, if there is more than one virtue, in accordance with the best and most complete of them — we should now examine virtue; for that will probably throw light on our inquiry about happiness.

And indeed the true statesman seems to be occupied with virtue above all; for he wants to make the citizens good and obedient to the laws. We have examples in the lawgivers of Crete and Sparta, and any others who have been like them. And if this inquiry belongs to Politics, then plainly pursuing it fits the plan we laid down at the start.

The virtue we are to examine is, of course, human virtue; for it was the good of man and the happiness of man that we set out to find. And by human virtue we mean virtue not of the body but of the soul; for happiness, on our account, is an activity of the soul.

If this is so, then evidently the statesman must know something about the soul, just as the man who is to heal the eye, or the whole body, must know something about them — all the more so as Politics is a higher and better science than medicine. And the better sort of physicians take great pains to know about the body.

As statesmen, then — as students of Politics — we must inquire into the soul; but we must inquire with our own purpose in view, and go only as far as that purpose requires. To push into finer detail would be more labor than our undertaking needs.

Now, certain doctrines about the soul are stated well enough in our published discussions, and we shall use them.

Two parts of the soul are distinguished: an irrational part and a rational part.

Whether these are separated the way the parts of the body are, or the parts of anything divisible — or whether they are two only in thought, and in fact inseparable, like the concave and the convex in the curve of a circle — makes no difference for our present purpose.

Of the irrational part, one division seems to be common to everything that lives, plants included: I mean the part that causes nutrition and growth. For we must credit everything that takes nourishment with a faculty of this kind — in the embryo as in the full-grown creature, since it is more reasonable to suppose the faculty is the same than that it changes.

The virtue of this faculty, then, plainly belongs to man in common with other living things; it is not specifically human.

This is confirmed by the fact that in sleep this part of the soul is thought to be most active, while the good man and the bad are indistinguishable when they are asleep — whence the saying that for half their lives the happy differ not at all from the miserable. And this is just what we should expect; for sleep is the soul's holiday from the very functions that make it called good or bad — except insofar as some of what goes on in the body filters through, which is why the dreams of good men are better than other people's.

But enough of this. We may dismiss the nutritive part, since by nature it has no share in human virtue.

There seems, however, to be another principle in the soul that is irrational and yet in some way partakes of reason. In the self-restrained man and the weak-willed man alike, we praise the reason — the rational part — for it exhorts them rightly and urges them toward what is best. But there is plainly something else in them besides reason, something that fights against reason and pulls the other way. Just as a paralyzed limb, when you will to move it to the right, swings instead to the left, so it is with the soul: the weak-willed man's impulses run counter to his reason. The difference is only that in the body we see the disobedient member, while in the soul we do not. But we must hold, I think, that in the soul too there is something besides reason, which opposes it and runs against it. (In what sense it is distinct from reason does not matter here.)

Yet this something partakes of reason too, as we said. In the self-restrained man, at least, it obeys reason; and in the self-controlled and courageous man it is more obedient still, for in him it is in complete harmony with reason.

The irrational part, then, turns out to be twofold. There is the vegetative faculty, which has no share of reason at all; and there is the faculty of appetite — of desire in general — which does share in reason after a fashion, in that it listens to reason and submits to its rule. It is "rational" in the sense in which we say a man listens to reason when he heeds his father or his friends — not in the sense in which we speak of reasoning out mathematical truths. And that the irrational part is somehow open to reason is attested by all our advising, all our rebuking and exhorting.

If we choose to say that this part, too, has reason, then the rational part will likewise have two divisions: one that has reason in the strict sense, in itself; and one that listens to reason as a man listens to his father.

Now, on this division of the soul's faculties the division of virtue is based. For we speak of intellectual virtues and moral virtues: wisdom, understanding, and practical wisdom we call intellectual; generosity and self-control we call moral. When we are describing a man's moral character we do not say that he is wise or intelligent, but that he is gentle or self-controlled. Yet we praise the wise man too for his habit of mind; and habits that deserve praise are what we call virtues.

Book II

Moral Virtue

Chapter 1

Virtue, then, is of two kinds, intellectual and moral. Intellectual virtue owes its birth and growth mainly to teaching, and so it needs time and experience. Moral virtue is the product of habit — which is why its Greek name, êthikê, is a slight variation on ethos, the word for habit.

From this one thing is immediately plain: none of the moral virtues is implanted in us by nature, for nothing that exists by nature can be trained to behave otherwise. A stone naturally falls downward, and you could not train it to rise — not if you threw it up ten thousand times. You could not train fire to burn downward. Nothing that naturally behaves one way can be habituated into behaving another.

The virtues, then, come neither by nature nor against nature. Nature gives us the capacity to acquire them, and training develops that capacity.

Again, wherever nature gives us something, we get the power first and put it to use afterward. The senses show this clearly: we did not acquire sight by repeatedly seeing, or hearing by repeatedly hearing. On the contrary, we had the powers first and then used them; we did not get them by using them. But the virtues we acquire by first doing the acts — just as with the crafts. We learn a craft by doing the very thing we will do once we have learned it: we become builders by building, and lyre-players by playing the lyre. And so by doing just acts we become just, and by doing self-controlled and courageous acts we become self-controlled and courageous.

What happens in cities attests to this. Legislators make their citizens good by training them — at least, that is every legislator's wish; those who fail at it miss their mark, and this is what separates a good constitution from a bad one.

Again, every virtue is produced, and every vice too, by the same acts — and here also the crafts run parallel. Playing the lyre produces good lyre-players and bad ones alike; so with builders and all the rest: build well and you become a good builder, build badly and you become a bad one. If it were otherwise, nobody would need a teacher; we would all simply be born good or bad at our trades. And it is exactly the same with the virtues. By how we act in our dealings with other people, we become just or unjust; by how we act in the face of danger, training ourselves to feel fear or confidence, we become courageous or cowardly. So too with our appetites and with anger: by behaving one way or the other in the situations that engage these feelings, some of us become self-controlled and even-tempered, others self-indulgent and hot-tempered. In a word: acts of a certain kind produce habits of the same kind.

Hence we must make sure our acts are of the right kind, for as the acts vary, so does the character that results. It makes no small difference, then, whether a person is trained from youth in this way or in that — it makes a great difference; or rather, it makes all the difference.

Chapter 2

But our present inquiry does not aim, as our others do, at theory alone. We are not asking what virtue is merely in order to know; we are asking in order to become good — otherwise the inquiry would profit us nothing. So we must examine acts and ask what kind they should be; for, as we said, it is the acts that determine the character that results.

First, then, that our acts must accord with right reason is common ground, and we shall take it for granted here, postponing for later the question of what right reason is and how it relates to the other virtues.

But let one thing be understood before we go on: all reasoning about practical matters can be given in outline only, not with scientific exactness. As we said at the start, the precision to demand depends on the subject in hand, and in matters of conduct and questions of what is useful there are no fixed laws — any more than in questions of health.

And if our general account is inexact, our reasoning about particular cases is more inexact still. Particular cases fall under no science and no book of rules; the agent must always look at the occasion itself and judge what it requires, just as in medicine or in navigation.

Still, though this is so, we must try to give what help we can.

First, then, observe that in matters of this kind, falling short and going too far are equally fatal. This is plain — to illustrate what we cannot see by what we can — in the case of strength and health. Too much exercise destroys strength, and so does too little; too much food and drink ruins health, and so does too little — while the fitting amount produces, increases, and preserves them. It is just the same with self-control and courage and the other virtues. The man who runs from everything, fears everything, and never makes a stand becomes a coward; the man who fears nothing at all and will walk into anything becomes foolhardy. Likewise the man who takes his fill of every pleasure and abstains from none becomes self-indulgent, while the man who shuns every pleasure — a "boor," as we say — ends up insensible, dead to enjoyment. Self-control and courage, then, are destroyed by excess and by deficiency, and preserved by the mean.

But habits are not only produced, preserved, and destroyed by the same occasions and the same means; they also show themselves in the same circumstances. This is how it works with something as palpable as strength. Strength is produced by taking plenty of nourishment and doing plenty of hard work — and it is the strong man who is most capable of doing exactly that. So it is with the virtues: by abstaining from pleasures we become self-controlled, and once we have become self-controlled we are best able to abstain. And so with courage: by habituating ourselves to make light of danger and to stand up to it, we become courageous; and once we have become courageous, we are best able to face danger.

Chapter 3

The pleasure or pain that accompanies a man's acts should be taken as the test of his formed character.

The man who abstains from bodily pleasures and enjoys the abstaining is self-controlled; the man who abstains but resents it is self-indulgent. The man who faces danger with pleasure — or at least without pain — is courageous; the man to whom it is painful is a coward.

For moral virtue is deeply concerned with pleasures and pains. It is pleasure that moves us to do what is base, and pain that moves us to shrink from what is noble. That is why, as Plato says, we need to be trained from childhood to feel pleasure and pain at the right objects. This is what sound education means.

Another reason virtue has to do with pleasure and pain is that it has to do with actions and feelings — and every feeling and every action is accompanied by pleasure or pain.

Punishment attests to it as well, for punishment works through pleasure and pain. It is a kind of cure, and a cure works by administering the opposite of the disease.

Again, as we said before, every state of character is essentially bound up with the very things that form it, for better or for worse; and it is through pleasures and pains that characters go bad — through pursuing and avoiding the wrong ones, or at the wrong time, or in the wrong manner, or going wrong in whatever other way can be distinguished.

Hence some people go so far as to define the virtues as states of impassivity — a kind of calm freedom from feeling. But they go wrong in stating it absolutely, without adding "in the right or wrong manner, at the right or wrong time," and so on.

We may lay it down, then, that moral virtue makes us do what is best in matters of pleasure and pain, and vice the contrary. The following considerations will throw further light on the point.

There are three kinds of things that move us to choose, and three that move us to avoid: on one side the noble, the useful, and the pleasant; on the other their opposites — the shameful, the harmful, and the painful. About all of these the good man tends to go right and the bad man to go wrong, but especially about pleasure. For pleasure we share with the animals, and it accompanies everything we pursue or choose; even the noble and the useful appear pleasant to us.

Again, the feeling of pleasure has been bred into all of us from infancy by our upbringing; it is so ingrained in our life that it can scarcely be scrubbed out. And we all, more or less, make pleasure our measuring stick in judging actions. For this reason too, then, our whole inquiry must deal with these matters; for feeling pleasure and pain in the right way or the wrong way has no small influence on our acts.

Again, it is harder to fight pleasure than to fight anger — and Heraclitus says fighting anger is hard. But virtue, like craft, is always concerned with the harder case, for success is worth more when the task is harder. For this reason also, both virtue and the science of the state must always concern themselves with pleasures and pains; for the man who handles them rightly will be good, and the man who handles them badly will be bad.

Let this stand as established, then: virtue has to do with pleasures and pains; the acts that produce it also develop it — and, done differently, destroy it; and it shows itself in the very acts that produced it.

Chapter 4

But here someone may ask what we mean by saying that men can become just and self-controlled only by doing just and self-controlled acts. Surely, the objection runs, if their acts are just and self-controlled, they are already just and self-controlled — just as people who do what is grammatical and musical are already grammarians and musicians.

We may answer, first, that this is not quite true even of the crafts. A man may write something correctly by chance, or because someone else prompted him; he is not a grammarian until he not only writes correctly but writes as a grammarian would — that is, from his own knowledge of grammar.

But second, the virtues are not parallel to the crafts on this point anyway. What a craft produces has its excellence in itself, so it is enough if the product comes out with a certain quality. But with the virtues, an act does not count as done justly or with self-control merely because the act itself is of a certain sort. The doer must also be in a certain state of mind when he does it: first, he must know what he is doing; second, he must choose the act, and choose it for its own sake; and third, the act must flow from a formed and stable character. Of these conditions, only one — the knowledge — matters for possessing a craft. But for possessing the virtues, knowledge counts for little or nothing, while the other conditions, which come only from repeatedly doing what is just and self-controlled, count not a little but for everything.

An act, then, is called just or self-controlled when it is the sort of act the just or self-controlled man would do. But the man who does it is not thereby just or self-controlled — not unless he also does it in the spirit of the just or self-controlled man.

It is right, then, to say that a man becomes just by doing what is just, and self-controlled by doing what is self-controlled; and without doing these things, no one has any chance of ever becoming good.

But most men, instead of doing them, take refuge in theory. They imagine they are philosophizing, and that philosophizing will make them good — like a sick man who listens attentively to everything his doctor says and then follows none of his orders. That kind of philosophizing will no more produce a healthy soul than that kind of treatment will produce a healthy body.

Chapter 5

We must ask next what virtue actually is.

A quality of the soul is one of three things: (1) a feeling, (2) a capacity, or (3) a habit — a settled state of character. Virtue, then, must be one of these three. By feelings I mean appetite, anger, fear, confidence, envy, joy, love, hate, longing, emulation, pity — in general, whatever is accompanied by pleasure or pain. By a capacity I mean that in virtue of which we are said to be capable of these feelings — capable, for instance, of being angered or pained or moved to pity. And by a habit I mean that in virtue of which we are well or badly disposed in the matter of our feelings: in the matter of anger, for instance, we are badly disposed if we run too violent or too slack, and well disposed if we keep to the moderate course. And so with the rest.

Now, the virtues are not feelings, and neither are the vices. (1) We are not called good or bad on account of our feelings, but we are called so on account of our virtues and vices. (2) We are neither praised nor blamed for our feelings — a man is not praised for being afraid, nor blamed simply for being angry, but only for being angry in a particular way — whereas we are praised and blamed for our virtues and vices. (3) We can be angered or frightened without any deliberate choice, but the virtues are a kind of deliberate choice, or at least are impossible without it. And (4) with respect to our feelings we are said to be moved, but with respect to our virtues and vices we are not said to be moved; we are said to be disposed this way or that.

For these same reasons they are not capacities either. We are not called good or bad merely for being capable of feeling, nor are we praised or blamed for it. And besides, nature gives us our capacities, and nature does not make us good or bad — but that point we have already dealt with.

If, then, the virtues are neither feelings nor capacities, only one possibility remains: they are habits.

Chapter 6

We have found, then, the genus to which virtue belongs. But we want to know not merely that virtue is a habit, but what kind of habit it is.

We may safely say this much: the virtue of a thing both puts the thing itself in good condition and makes it perform its function well. The virtue of the eye, for instance, makes both the eye and its work good; it is by the eye's virtue that we see well. In the same way the proper virtue of a horse makes it what a horse should be — good at running, at carrying its rider, at standing firm against a charge.

If this holds in every case, then the proper virtue of man will be the habit that makes a man good and makes him perform his function well.

How this comes about we have already said; but the same conclusion can be reached another way, by asking what the nature of this virtue is.

Now take any quantity, continuous or discrete: it is possible to take a larger amount, a smaller amount, or an equal amount — and each either in relation to the thing itself or relative to our own needs.

By an equal or fair amount I mean a mean amount — one that lies between excess and deficiency.

By the absolute mean — the mean of the thing itself — I mean the point equally distant from both extremes; and that is one and the same for everybody.

By the mean relative to us I mean the amount that is neither too much nor too little for us; and that is not one and the same for everybody.

For instance, if ten is too large and two is too small, then six is the mean of the thing itself — the arithmetic mean — for six exceeds one extreme by exactly as much as it is exceeded by the other. That is the mean by arithmetical proportion.

But the mean relative to us cannot be found that way. If ten pounds of food is too much for a given man to eat, and two pounds too little, it does not follow that the trainer will order him six; for six may itself be too much for this particular man, or too little — too little for Milo the wrestler, too much for the beginner. The same holds in running and wrestling.

So we may say generally that a master of any art avoids what is too much and what is too little; he searches out the mean and chooses it — not the absolute mean, but the mean relative to us.

Every art or science, then, perfects its work in this way: it looks to the mean and brings its work up to that standard. That is why people say of a good piece of work that nothing could be added to it and nothing taken away — meaning that excess or deficiency destroys its goodness, while the mean preserves it. And good artists, as we say, do in fact keep their eyes fixed on the mean in everything they do. Now if this is so, and if virtue, like nature, is more exact and better than any art, it follows that virtue too must aim at the mean. I mean moral virtue; for it is moral virtue that deals with feelings and actions, and it is these that admit of excess, deficiency, and the mean. It is possible to feel fear, confidence, desire, anger, pity — pleasure and pain generally — too much or too little, and both are wrong. But to feel them at the right times, about the right things, toward the right people, for the right end, and in the right way — that is the mean and the best course, and that is the mark of virtue. In the same way our outward actions also admit of excess, deficiency, and the due amount.

Virtue, then, deals with feelings and with actions, where excess is wrong and deficiency is blamed, while the mean amount is praised and hits the mark — and both being praised and being right belong to virtue.

Virtue, then, is a kind of mean state, since it aims at the mean.

Again, there are many ways of going wrong — for evil belongs to the unlimited, as the Pythagoreans pictured it, and good to the limited — but only one way of going right. That is why the one is easy and the other hard: easy to miss the target, hard to hit it. And this is one more reason why excess and deficiency belong to vice, and hitting the mean belongs to virtue:

  Good men are good in one way; bad men are bad in every way.

Virtue, then, is a habit of choice, lying in the mean relative to us, determined by reason — that is, by the reason by which the man of practical wisdom would determine it. And it is a mean, first, because it lies in the middle between two vices, one of excess and one of deficiency; and second, because while the vices fall short of or overshoot the due amount in feeling and in action, virtue finds the mean and chooses it.

In its essence, therefore — by the definition of what it is — virtue is a mean; but measured by what is best and right, it is an extreme.

Not every action or feeling admits of a mean, however. Some have names that already imply badness: among feelings, spite, shamelessness, envy; among actions, adultery, theft, murder. All of these are blamed because they are bad in themselves, not because of their excess or deficiency. With these it is impossible ever to go right; they are always wrong. Right and wrong in such things does not depend on doing them with the right person, at the right time, in the right manner — with adultery, say — the mere doing of any of them is wrong.

It would be equally absurd to look for a mean, an excess, and a deficiency in unjust or cowardly or self-indulgent conduct; for then there would be a mean of excess and a mean of deficiency, an excess of excess and a deficiency of deficiency.

The fact is this: just as there is no excess or deficiency of self-control or courage — because the mean is there, in a sense, an extreme — so in these kinds of conduct there is no mean, no excess, no deficiency: however they are done, they are wrong. For, to put it generally, there is no mean of excess or deficiency, and no excess or deficiency of the mean.

Chapter 7

But it is not enough to state all this in general terms; we must go on and apply it to the particulars — the several virtues and vices. For in reasoning about conduct, general statements are too vague and carry less truth than particular ones. Conduct deals with particular cases, and it is against these that our statements must be found to hold good.

Let us take the particulars, then, from the following table.

In the feelings of fear and confidence, the mean is courage. Of those who exceed, the man who exceeds in fearlessness has no name — as happens often in this table — while the man who exceeds in confidence is foolhardy, and the man who exceeds in fear and falls short in confidence is a coward.

In certain pleasures, and to a lesser extent in certain pains, the mean is self-control, and the excess is self-indulgence. People who fall short in the matter of these pleasures hardly exist, so they too have received no name; let us put them down as "insensible."

In giving and taking money, the mean is generosity; the excess and deficiency are extravagance and stinginess. These two vices exceed and fall short in opposite ways: the extravagant man exceeds in spending but falls short in taking, while the stingy man exceeds in taking but falls short in spending. (For the present we are only giving an outline or summary — nothing more; these points will be treated in detail later.)

Besides these there are other dispositions in the matter of money. There is a mean called magnificence — for the magnificent man is not the same as the generous man: he deals with large sums, the generous man with small — an excess called vulgarity or bad taste, and a deficiency called cheapness. These vices differ from the ones opposed to generosity; how they differ will be explained later.

With respect to honor and disgrace, the mean is greatness of soul; the excess may be called vanity, and the deficiency smallness of soul.

And just as we said that generosity is related to magnificence, differing only in that it deals with small sums, so here there is a virtue related to greatness of soul, differing only in that it is concerned with small honors instead of great ones. A man may desire honor as much as he ought, or more, or less: the man who carries the desire to excess is called ambitious, the man who lacks it unambitious, while the man with the due amount has no name. Nor are there abstract names for the characters, except "ambition" for the ambitious man's. Because of this, the men at the extremes both lay claim to the middle ground. Indeed, in everyday speech the man in the middle is sometimes called ambitious and sometimes unambitious; and sometimes the ambitious man is praised, sometimes the unambitious. Why this is so we will explain later; for now let us carry out our plan and run through the remaining types of character.

In the matter of anger, too, there is excess, deficiency, and the mean. The characters here scarcely have recognized names; but since we call the man in the middle gentle, let us call the mean gentleness. Of those at the extremes, let the man who exceeds be called wrathful and his vice wrathfulness, and the man who falls short wrathless and his character wrathlessness.

Besides these there are three other means, resembling one another and yet different. All of them have to do with our dealings with one another in word and action, but they differ in this: one concerns the truthfulness of those dealings, the other two their pleasantness — one of the two, pleasantness in amusement, the other, pleasantness in all the business of life. We must speak of these as well, so that we may see more plainly how in every case the mean is praiseworthy, while the extremes are neither right nor praiseworthy, but blamable.

Here too names are mostly lacking, and we must try, as elsewhere, to coin them ourselves, to keep the argument clear and easy to follow.

In the matter of truth, then, let us call the man who observes the mean truthful, and the mean truthfulness. Pretence that exaggerates is boasting, and the man a boaster; pretence that understates is irony, and the man ironical.

With regard to pleasantness in amusement, the man who observes the mean is witty, and his character wittiness; the excess is buffoonery, and the man a buffoon; the man who falls short is a boor, and his character boorishness.

With regard to pleasantness in the other affairs of life, the man who makes himself pleasant in the right way is friendly, and his mean state is friendliness; the man who overdoes it is obsequious if he has no ulterior motive, and a flatterer if he is angling for his own advantage; the man who falls short and makes himself disagreeable at every turn is a quarrelsome and sour sort of fellow.

There are also ways of observing the mean in mere emotions, and in our conduct with regard to them. Shame, for instance, is not a virtue, and yet the modest man is praised. For here too we speak of one man as observing the mean and another as exceeding it — like the bashful man, whom the least thing makes shy — while the man who is deficient in the feeling, or lacks it altogether, is shameless; the man who observes the mean is modest.

Righteous indignation, again, hits the mean between envy and spite. These have to do with the pleasure and pain we feel at what happens to our neighbors. The righteously indignant man is pained by the sight of undeserved prosperity; the envious man goes beyond him and is pained by the sight of anyone prospering; while the spiteful man, so far from being pained, actually rejoices in his neighbors' misfortunes.

But there will be another opportunity to discuss these matters.

As for justice, the term is used in more senses than one; so after settling the questions above, we will distinguish its various senses and show how each kind of justice is a mean.

And then we will treat the intellectual virtues in the same way.

Chapter 8

There are, then, as we said, three kinds of disposition: two kinds of vice, one marked by excess and the other by deficiency, and one kind of virtue, the observance of the mean. Now each is in a way opposed to each; for the extreme dispositions are opposed both to the mean and to one another, while the mean is opposed to both extremes. Just as the equal is greater when compared with the less, and less when compared with the greater, so the mean dispositions are excessive compared with the deficient ones, and deficient compared with the excessive ones, in feeling and in action alike. The brave man looks foolhardy next to the coward, and cowardly next to the foolhardy man; the self-controlled man looks self-indulgent next to the insensible man, and insensible next to the self-indulgent man; the generous man looks extravagant beside the stingy man, and stingy beside the extravagant man.

And so each extreme character tries to shove the middle character away from the middle, representing him as belonging to the opposite extreme: the coward calls the brave man foolhardy, the foolhardy man calls him a coward, and so on in the other cases.

But though mean and extremes are opposed to one another in this way, the extremes are strictly contrary to each other rather than to the mean; for they lie farther from each other than from the mean, just as the greater lies farther from the less, and the less from the greater, than either lies from the equal.

Sometimes, moreover, an extreme bears a certain resemblance to the mean — foolhardiness to courage, extravagance to generosity — whereas between the extremes there is the greatest possible unlikeness.

And "things as far removed from each other as possible" is the accepted definition of contraries; so the farther apart things are, the more contrary they are.

Compared with the mean, however, it is sometimes the deficiency that is more opposed and sometimes the excess. Foolhardiness, an excess, is not so opposed to courage as cowardice, a deficiency, is; but insensibility, a deficiency of feeling, is not so opposed to self-control as self-indulgence, an excess, is.

There are two reasons for this. One is drawn from the nature of the thing itself: since one extreme is in fact nearer to the mean and more like it, we naturally do not set it against the mean so strongly as the other. Foolhardiness seems nearer to courage and more like it, cowardice more unlike; so it is cowardice we call the opposite of courage — for what lies farther from the mean seems more contrary to it.

That, then, is the one reason, drawn from the thing itself. The other lies in ourselves, and it is this: whatever we are more prone to by nature appears more opposed to the mean. Our natural inclination runs toward indulgence in pleasure, and so we slip more easily into self-indulgence than into disciplined habits. The directions in which we tend to run to lengths, then, are the ones we speak of as more opposed to the mean; and thus self-indulgence, the excess, is more opposed to self-control than the deficiency is.

Chapter 9

We have now explained sufficiently that moral virtue is a mean, and in what sense: first, that it lies in the middle between two vices, one of excess and one of deficiency; and second, that it aims at the mean in feeling and in action.

And this is why it is a hard thing to be good; for finding the middle — the mean — in each case is hard, just as finding the center of a circle is not something everyone can do, but only the man who knows how.

So too anyone can get angry — that is easy — and anyone can give money away or spend it. But to do these things to the right person, to the right extent, at the right time, for the right end, and in the right way — that is not something everyone can do, and it is by no means easy. That is why doing it well is rare and praiseworthy and noble.

The man who aims at the mean, then, should first of all steer away from the extreme that is more opposed to it, as Calypso advises Odysseus:

  Keep your ship clear of that smoke and surf.

For of the two extremes one is more dangerous, the other less. Since hitting the mean precisely is hard, we must, as the proverb has it, take to the oars when we cannot sail, and choose the lesser of two evils; and we shall do that best in the way just described.

Second, we must each consider what we ourselves are most prone to — for different natures incline toward different things — and we can learn this from the pleasure and pain we feel. Then we must drag ourselves in the opposite direction; for by pulling well away from the wrong side we shall arrive at the middle, just as we straighten a bent stick by bending it back the other way.

But in everything we must be most on our guard against pleasant things — against pleasure — for we can scarcely judge her impartially. We should treat her, then, as the old counselors of Troy proposed to treat Helen, and repeat their saying in every case: if we send her away, we shall be less likely to go wrong.

This, in outline, is the course by which we shall best be able to hit the mean.

It is a hard task, we must admit, above all in the particular case. It is not easy to determine, for instance, how one ought to be angry, and with whom, and on what grounds, and for how long; for public opinion sometimes praises those who fall short and calls them gentle, and sometimes calls a harsh temper manly.

In fact a slight slip from the mean, whether toward excess or toward deficiency, is not blamed — only a considerable one; for that no one can mistake. But how far and to what extent a man must go wrong to earn blame is hardly possible to fix by reasoning; nothing that falls within the scope of perception can be so fixed. Such things lie in the region of particulars, and the judgment lies with perception.

This much, then, is plain: the middle character is in all cases to be praised, but we ought to lean sometimes toward excess and sometimes toward deficiency; for that is how we shall most easily hit the mean and get things right.

Book III

The Will

Chapter 1

Virtue, as we have seen, has to do with feelings and actions. Now, praise and blame attach only to what is voluntary; what is involuntary gets pardon, and sometimes even pity.

So anyone investigating the nature of virtue needs to draw a clear line between the voluntary and the involuntary — and lawmakers will find the distinction useful too, when they hand out rewards and punishments.

The general view is that an act is involuntary when it is done under compulsion or through ignorance.

"Done under compulsion" means the cause is outside the person, who contributes nothing to it — as when someone is carried off by a whirlwind, or by men too strong to resist.

But there is a real question about acts done to avoid a greater evil, or to gain some noble end. Suppose a tyrant orders you to do something disgraceful while he holds your parents or your children in his power: do it and they live, refuse and they die. Whether such acts are voluntary or involuntary is a matter of genuine dispute.

Throwing cargo overboard in a storm is a similar case. No one dumps his property over the side for nothing; but any sensible person will do it to save his own life and the lives of the crew.

Acts of this kind, then, are mixed — but they come closer to being voluntary. For they are wanted and chosen at the moment they are done, and the end of an act is whatever the agent has in view at the time. So in applying the terms voluntary and involuntary, we must look at the agent's state of mind at the moment of action. And at that moment he does will the act; for in cases like these the cause that sets his limbs moving lies inside him — and where the cause lies in the agent, it is up to him to act or not to act.

Such acts, then, are voluntary — though in themselves, apart from the circumstances, we may grant they are involuntary, since no one would choose anything of the kind for its own sake.

In fact, people are sometimes praised for actions of this sort — for enduring something disgraceful or painful in order to secure some great and noble result. In the opposite case they are blamed; for no decent person would endure utter disgrace with no noble result in view, or only a trivial one.

In some cases we do not praise, but we do pardon: when someone is driven to do wrong by pressure too strong for human nature, pressure no one could withstand.
