Though there are some cases, I think, where the plea of compulsion cannot be allowed — where a man ought to accept death in its most painful form rather than do the deed. The circumstances that supposedly "compelled" Alcmaeon, in Euripides' play, to kill his mother look simply absurd.

It is sometimes hard to decide whether we should do this deed to avoid that evil, or endure that evil rather than do this deed; and it is harder still to stand by our decision. For usually the evil we want to avoid is painful, while the deed we are being pushed toward is disgraceful — and that is why we are blamed or praised, according to whether we let ourselves be compelled or not.

What kinds of acts, then, should be called compulsory?

Our answer, I think, must be this. First: when the cause lies outside and the agent contributes nothing, the act is compulsory without qualification — and therefore involuntary. Second: when an act that would never be done voluntarily for its own sake is chosen now, in preference to a given alternative, and the cause lies in the agent — such an act is "involuntary in itself," in the abstract, but "voluntary here and now, in preference to this alternative." And an act of this second kind is closer to being voluntary; for acts belong to the realm of particulars, and the particular thing done here is done voluntarily.

It is hardly possible, though, to lay down rules for which of two alternatives should be preferred; the particular cases differ too much.

Someone might argue that acts whose motive is something pleasant or something noble are compulsory too — for here also, the claim goes, something outside us constrains us.

But if that were true, every act would be compulsory; for pleasure and the noble are the motives of every act of every human being.

Again, acting under compulsion, against one's will, is painful; but acting for the sake of something pleasant or noble brings pleasure. And it is absurd to blame things outside us instead of our own readiness to be caught by their lures — absurd to claim our noble acts as our own while charging our disgraceful ones to "the pleasant things outside us."

Compulsory, then, it appears, means this: the cause is external, and the person compelled contributes nothing.

What is done through ignorance is always "not-voluntary," but it is "involuntary" only when the agent is pained afterwards — sorry when he discovers what he has done. For when a man who acted through ignorance feels no distress at the deed, you cannot say he did it voluntarily, since he did not know what he was doing; but you cannot say he did it involuntarily or unwillingly either, since he is not sorry.

So a man who has acted through ignorance and is sorry afterwards is held to have acted involuntarily, unwillingly; if he is not sorry, we may say — to mark the difference — that he acted "not-voluntarily." Since the case is different, it is better to have a different name for it.

Acting through ignorance, moreover, seems to be a different thing from acting in ignorance. A man who is drunk or in a rage is not thought to act through ignorance but through drunkenness or rage — yet he acts not knowingly, but in ignorance.

Every vicious man, to be sure, is ignorant of what he ought to do and ought not to do, and it is exactly this kind of error that makes men unjust and bad in general. But the word "involuntary" does not properly apply where a man is merely ignorant of what is right. The ignorance that makes an act involuntary is not ignorance of the principles that should guide our choices — that is vice; not, I say, ignorance of the universal, for that we blame — but ignorance of the particulars: the persons and things the act affects. These are the grounds for pity and pardon; a man who is ignorant of any of these particulars acts involuntarily.

It may be worth spelling out, then, what these particulars are and how many. They are: first, who is acting; second, what he is doing; third, the object or person the act affects; sometimes also the instrument — what he is doing it with; the purpose — what he is doing it for, say, to protect someone; and the manner — gently, for instance, or violently.

Now, no one short of a madman could be ignorant of all of these. Obviously a man cannot be ignorant of who is acting — how could he not know himself?

But a man can be ignorant of what he is doing. Someone who has said something will plead that the words slipped out before he knew it, or that he did not know the subject was forbidden — as Aeschylus pleaded about the Mysteries. Or a man might plead that in discharging the weapon he only meant to demonstrate how it worked, as the defendant did in the catapult case. Again, a man might mistake his own son for an enemy, as Merope does; or a sharp spear for a blunted practice one; or a heavy stone for a pumice-stone. One might kill a man with a drug meant to save him, or land a hard blow when one only meant to touch — as boxers do when they spar with open hands.

Since ignorance is possible about all these circumstances, a man who is ignorant of any of them is held to have acted involuntarily — above all when he is ignorant of the most important ones; and the most important seem to be the persons affected and the result.

Beyond this, though, the agent must be grieved and sorry for what he has done, if an act committed in this kind of ignorance is to count as involuntary and not merely not-voluntary.

But now, having found that an act is involuntary when done under compulsion or through ignorance, we may conclude that a voluntary act is one the doer originates himself, with knowledge of the particular circumstances.

For I venture to say it is wrong to call acts done from anger or desire involuntary.

In the first place, if that were right, we could no longer say that any of the other animals act voluntarily — nor even children.

Again, what does the claim mean? That none of the acts we do from desire or anger is voluntary? Or that the noble ones are voluntary and the disgraceful ones involuntary? Taken the second way, it is surely ridiculous, since the cause of both is one and the same. Taken the first way, it is absurd too, I think: absurd to say that we ought to desire a thing and, in the same breath, that pursuing it is involuntary. And in fact there are things we ought to be angry at, and things we ought to desire — health, for instance, and learning.

Again, what is done unwillingly seems to be painful, while what is done from desire is pleasant.

Again, as far as involuntariness goes, what is the difference between wrong done by calculation and wrong done in anger? Both are to be avoided; but the unreasoning passions seem to belong to a man just as much as his reason does, so acts done from the impulse of anger or desire are the man's own acts too. To call them involuntary, then, would be too absurd.

Chapter 2

Now that we have distinguished the voluntary from the involuntary, our next task is to discuss choice. For choice seems to be bound up with virtue most intimately of all, and to be a surer test of character than actions themselves.

Choosing is evidently a kind of willing, but the two terms are not identical: willing is the wider one. Children and the other animals have will, but not choice; and acts done on the spur of the moment are called voluntary, but not chosen.

Those who say that choice is appetite, or anger, or wish, or some sort of opinion do not seem to describe it correctly.

In the first place, choice is not shared by irrational creatures; appetite and anger are.

Again, the weak-willed man acts from appetite and not from choice; the self-restrained man acts from choice and not from appetite.

Again, appetite can run contrary to choice; but one appetite cannot run contrary to another appetite.

Again, the object of appetite — or of aversion — is the pleasant or the painful; but the object of choice, as such, is neither.

Still less can choice be anger; for acts done in anger seem to be, of all acts, the least chosen.

Nor is choice the same as wish, though the two look very alike. We cannot choose the impossible — a man who said he did would be thought a fool — but we can wish for the impossible, to escape death, for example.

Again, we may wish for things that could never come about through our own doing — that a particular actor or athlete should win, say — but we never choose such things; we choose only what we think our own action can bring about.

Again, strictly speaking we wish for the end and choose the means. We wish to be healthy, but we choose the things that will make us healthy; we wish to be happy, and we admit the wish, but it would not be correct to say we choose to be happy. For, in a word, choice deals with what is in our power.

Nor can choice be opinion. For one thing, anything at all can be a matter of opinion — the unalterable and the impossible no less than what is in our power. For another, we sort opinions by whether they are true or false, not by whether they are good or bad, which is how we sort choices.

Choice, then, is not the same as opinion in general — and no one, in fact, claims it is.

But neither is it identical with some particular kind of opinion. For it is our choosing good or evil that makes us morally good or bad; holding certain opinions does not.

Again, we choose to take or avoid something good or evil; we opine what a thing is, or what it is good for, or in what way — but we cannot opine to take or to avoid.

Again, we commend a choice for being right or correct, an opinion for being true.

Again, we choose a thing when we know well that it is good; we can hold an opinion about a thing we know nothing about.

Again, it seems the people best at choosing are not always the best at forming opinions: some have excellent judgment but, through depravity, fail to choose what they ought.

It may be said that choice must be preceded or accompanied by an opinion or judgment. But that makes no difference; our question is not that, but whether the two are identical.

What, then, is choice, since it is none of these?

It seems, as we said, that what is chosen is willed, but what is willed is not always chosen.

The missing mark, I think, is "after previous deliberation." For choice implies calculation and reasoning. The name itself seems to point the same way: something is chosen before other things — picked out in preference to them.

Chapter 3

Now for deliberation. Do we deliberate about everything — is anything whatever a possible subject of deliberation — or are there some things about which deliberation is impossible?

By "a matter for deliberation" we should mean, I think, not what a fool or a madman might deliberate about, but what a rational being would.

Now, no one deliberates about things eternal and unalterable — the system of the heavenly bodies, say, or the incommensurability of the side and the diagonal of a square.

Again, no one deliberates about things that change but always change in the same way, whether the cause is necessity or nature or anything else — the solstices, for instance, and the sunrise; nor about things wholly irregular, like drought and rain; nor about matters of chance, like finding a treasure.

Again, even human affairs are not all matters for deliberation: what constitution would be best for Scythia is a question no Spartan would deliberate about.

The reason we deliberate about none of these things is that none of them can be brought about by us.

What we deliberate about is conduct — the things within our own control. And these are all that is left; for besides nature, necessity, and chance, the only remaining cause of change is reason and human agency in general. Though we should add that each person deliberates about what he himself can do.

One further limit: where knowledge is exact and complete, there is no room for deliberation. Take writing: there is no doubt about how the letters should be formed.

We deliberate, then, about things our own agency brings about — but not about all of them equally. We deliberate about medicine and moneymaking; about navigation more than about gymnastics, since navigation has not yet been worked up into so exact a system; and so on — more about matters of art than matters of science, because there is more doubt about them.

Matters of deliberation, then, are matters where there are rules that generally hold good but where the outcome cannot be predicted — where there is an element of uncertainty. In important matters we call in advisers, distrusting our own judgment.

We deliberate not about ends but about means. A doctor does not deliberate whether he shall heal, nor an orator whether he shall persuade, nor a statesman whether he shall produce a good system of laws — no one in any profession deliberates about his end. Rather, having set the end before us, we consider how and by what means it can be reached. If it can be reached by several means, we consider which is easiest and best; if by only one, we consider how it is to be reached by that means, and how that means itself is to be secured, and so on — until we come to the first link in the chain of causes, which is the last thing we discover.

For in deliberating we seem to inquire and analyze in just this way, as we analyze a geometrical figure in order to learn how to construct it. (Not all inquiry is deliberation — mathematical inquiry is not — but all deliberation is inquiry.) And what comes last in the analysis comes first in the construction.

If we run into an impossibility, we drop the plan — if the scheme needs money, say, and no money can be raised. But if the thing looks possible, we set to work. By "possible" I mean what can be done by us; and what our friends can do, we can in a sense do ourselves, since it is we who set our friends to work.

Sometimes we are working out what instruments we need, sometimes how to use them; and so throughout: sometimes what agency will produce the effect, sometimes how, or through whom, that agency is to be set going.

It appears, then — as we said before — that a man originates his own acts; that he deliberates about what he can do himself; and that what he does, he does for the sake of something else. For he cannot deliberate about the end, only about the means to it; nor, again, can he deliberate about particular facts — whether this is a loaf, or whether it is properly baked. Those are matters of immediate perception. And if a man goes on deliberating forever, he will never reach a conclusion.

The object of deliberation and the object of choice are the same, except that the object of choice is already fixed and settled: when we say "this is chosen," we mean it has been selected after deliberation. For we always stop the inquiry into how to do a thing once we have traced the chain of causes back to ourselves — to the commanding part of ourselves; for that is the part that chooses.

The old constitutions Homer describes illustrate the point: there the kings announce to the people what they have chosen.

Since, then, a thing is said to be chosen when, being in our power, it is desired after deliberation, we may define choice as deliberate desire for something in our own power. For we first deliberate, then make our decision, and then desire in accordance with the deliberation.

Let this stand, then, as our outline account of choice — of what it is, and of what it deals with, namely means to ends.

Chapter 4

Wish, we have already said, is for the end. But here opinion splits: some hold that what we wish for is the good; others, that it is whatever seems good.

Each side runs into trouble. Those who say the object of wish is the good must admit that when a man chooses badly, what he wishes for is not an object of wish at all — for if it were, it would have to be good, and in fact it may be bad. Those who say the object of wish is whatever seems good must admit that nothing is an object of wish by nature; each man simply wishes for what seems good to him — and different things, even contrary things, seem good to different people.

Since neither answer quite satisfies us, perhaps we had better say this: the good, plainly and without qualification, is the real object of wish; but for each man, the object of wish is what seems good to him. The good man, then, wishes for what is really worth wishing for; what the bad man wishes for might be anything at all. It is just the same with the body: people in sound condition find healthy the things that really are healthy, while the sick find other things healthy — and likewise with what tastes bitter or sweet, what feels hot or heavy, and so on. For the good man judges each case correctly: in each case, what is true is what seems true to him.

Every trained capacity has its own special form of the noble and the pleasant, and perhaps nothing marks the good man so much as this: his power to discern these in each case, being himself, so to speak, their standard and measure.

What misleads most people is pleasure. It seems to be a good thing even when it is not. So they choose the pleasant as if it were good, and shun pain as if it were evil.

Chapter 5

We have seen that we wish for the end, while we deliberate about and choose the means to it.

Actions concerned with means, then, will be guided by choice, and so will be voluntary.

But the acts in which the virtues show themselves are concerned with means.

Therefore virtue is in our power — and so, equally, is vice. For where it is in our power to act, it is in our power not to act; where we can say no, we can say yes. If doing a deed is noble and lies with us, then not doing it, which is disgraceful, also lies with us; and if refraining is noble and lies with us, then doing it, which is disgraceful, lies with us too. But if doing and not doing noble or base deeds lies with us, and if this, as we found, is exactly what it is to be good or bad, then it follows that it lies with us to be decent men or worthless ones.

So the saying

  "No one is wicked by his own will, and no one is blessed against it"

is partly false and partly true. No one, certainly, is blessed against his will; but vice is voluntary.

If we deny this, we must quarrel with what we just established, and contend that a man is not the source and parent of his actions as he is of his children.

But if those statements stand, and we cannot trace our acts back to any sources other than ones within ourselves, then whatever has its source in us depends on us and is voluntary.

Everyday life bears witness to this, and so do the lawgivers. They correct and punish those who do evil — except where the act was done under compulsion, or through ignorance the agent was not responsible for — and they honor those who do noble deeds, plainly meaning to encourage the one and discourage the other. But no one encourages us to do what is not in our power and not voluntary: there would be no point in persuading a man not to feel heat or pain or hunger, since he will feel them all the same.

I say "ignorance the agent was not responsible for," because the law punishes the ignorance itself when the agent appears responsible for it. For an offense committed while drunk, the penalty is doubled: the source of the offense lies in the man himself, for he could have avoided getting drunk, and the drunkenness was the cause of his ignorance. Again, ignorance of any point of law that a man ought to know, and easily could know, does not save him from punishment. And so in other cases: where the ignorance seems to come from negligence, the offender is punished, since it lay with him to remove that ignorance — he could have taken the trouble.

But, someone may object, it was the man's character not to take the trouble.

We reply that men make themselves responsible for acquiring such a character, by living carelessly — for becoming unjust through repeated acts of wrongdoing, or self-indulgent through spending their days drinking and the like. It is repeated acts of a particular kind that produce a particular character.

You can see this in the way men train for any contest or performance: they practice, continually.

Not to know, then, that repeated acts of a given kind produce the corresponding character or habit is to show a complete want of sense.

Moreover, it is absurd to say that a man who acts unjustly does not wish to be unjust, or that a man who behaves self-indulgently does not wish to be self-indulgent.

If a man knowingly does the acts that must make him unjust, he is unjust voluntarily. It does not follow that if he wishes it, he can stop being unjust and become just — any more than a sick man can become well just by wishing it. He may be voluntarily sick, through living without restraint and disobeying his doctors. There was a time when it was open to him not to be sick; but once he has thrown his health away, that option is gone. When you have thrown a stone, you can no longer call it back — yet the throwing was in your power, for the start of its flight depended on you.

Just so, the unjust or the self-indulgent man was free at the beginning not to acquire this character, and therefore he is unjust or self-indulgent voluntarily; but now that he has acquired it, he is no longer free to put it off.

And it is not only vices of soul that are voluntary; bodily defects are sometimes voluntary too, and then we censure them. Nobody censures natural ugliness, but we do censure the ugliness that comes from negligence and want of exercise. So too with weakness and disability: no one would reproach a man born blind, or blinded by illness or a blow — we would sooner pity him; but everyone would censure a man who had blinded himself with heavy drinking or some other excess.

So among the defects of the body, it is those that depend on us that are censured, and those that do not depend on us that are not. And if that is so, then in other fields as well, the vices that get blamed must be the ones that depend on us.

Some people may still object.

"All men," they may say, "desire what appears good to them, but they have no control over how things appear. A man's character, whatever it is, decides what the end will look like to him."

I answer: if each man is in some way responsible for his own habits and character, then he must in some way be responsible for how things appear to him as well.

But suppose he is not. Then no man is responsible for, or the cause of, his own wrongdoing; he does evil through ignorance of the end, imagining that this is the way to secure the greatest good. On this view, aiming at the true end is not something we choose: a man must be born with a kind of eyesight, so to speak, if he is to judge rightly and choose what is really good. The truly wellborn man is the one whom nature has richly endowed with this gift — for it is the greatest and finest of gifts, one we cannot acquire or learn from anyone else but must keep all our lives just as nature gave it, and to be born well and nobly endowed in this respect would be wellborn in the truest and fullest sense.

Now, granting all that — how will virtue be any more voluntary than vice?

For whether it is nature or something else that fixes how the end appears, it is fixed the same way for both alike, for the good man and the bad; and both alike refer everything they do to it.

So take either horn. Say that it is not nature alone that decides how the end appears to each man, but that the man himself contributes something; or say that the end is fixed by nature, but virtue is voluntary because the good man voluntarily takes the steps toward it. Either way, vice will be exactly as voluntary as virtue; for the bad man's own self is just as active as the good man's — in choosing the particular acts, at least, if not in setting the end.

If, then, the virtues are voluntary, as everyone allows (for we do in fact help to make our own character, and by being of a certain character we give a certain cast to the end as we see it), then the vices must be voluntary too. Everything we have said applies equally to them.

We have now described in outline the nature of the virtues in general: they are modes of observing the mean; they are habits — trained states of character; they show themselves in the very acts that produce them; they depend on us and are voluntary; and they follow the guidance of right reason. But our particular acts are not voluntary in the same sense our habits are. We are masters of our acts from beginning to end, so long as we know the particular circumstances; but of our habits we are masters only of the beginnings — their growth creeps forward step by step, imperceptibly, like the growth of a disease. Still, since it lay with us to use our powers this way or not, the characters that result are on that account voluntary.

Now let us take up the virtues one by one and say what each is, what its field is, and how it operates there; in doing so we shall also see how many there are. And first, courage.

The Several Moral Virtues and Vices

Chapter 6

We have already said that courage is observance of the mean with respect to feelings of fear and confidence.

Now, fear is plainly excited by fearful things, and these are, roughly speaking, evils; which is why fear is sometimes defined as "expectation of evil."

Fear, then, is excited by evil of every kind — disgrace, poverty, disease, friendlessness, death — but not every kind gives scope for courage. Some things we actually ought to fear; fearing them is noble and not fearing them is base. Disgrace is one: the man who fears disgrace is honorable, a man with a proper sense of shame, while the man who does not fear it is shameless (though some stretch the word "courageous" to cover him, since he does bear a certain resemblance to the courageous man — courage also being a kind of fearlessness). Poverty, on the other hand, we probably ought not to fear, nor disease, nor in general the things that do not come from vice and do not depend on us. But being fearless about these is still not courage in the strict sense — though here too the term gets applied by resemblance. There are men, for instance, who are cowards in the dangers of war and yet free and bold in spending money.

Nor, on the other side, is a man a coward for fearing violence to his children or his wife, or for dreading envy and things of that kind; nor is he courageous for staying calm at the prospect of a whipping.

In what terrors, then, does the courageous man show his quality? Surely in the greatest, for no one is better able to endure what is terrible. And of all things the most terrible is death; for death is our limit, and once a man is dead it seems there is no longer anything, good or evil, for him.

Yet even death, it seems, does not give scope for courage on every occasion — not death by drowning, say, or by disease.

On what occasions, then? Surely the noblest; and those are the occasions of war, for they carry the greatest and the noblest danger.

The honors paid to courage in free states and by monarchs confirm this.

Strictly speaking, then, the courageous man is the one who fearlessly faces an honorable death, and all the sudden emergencies that threaten death — and such emergencies come mostly in war.

Of course the courageous man is fearless in illness too, and at sea — but not in the sailors' way. The sailors, because of their experience, stay hopeful while the landsmen have already despaired of their lives and shudder at the thought of such a death.

Besides, the occasions that especially call out courage are those where a man can show his prowess, or where death is noble; and in these deaths there is neither nobility nor any room for prowess.

Chapter 7

The same things do not frighten all men alike; yet we do speak of some terrors as beyond human power to face. Such things frighten every man in his senses. But the terrors a man can face differ in size — some are greater, some less fearful — and the same goes for the things that inspire confidence. Now, the courageous man keeps his presence of mind, so far as a man can. He will fear these fearful things, yes; but he will endure them as he ought and as reason bids, for the sake of the noble — for that is the end and aim of virtue.

But it is possible to fear these things too much or too little, and possible, too, to take as fearful what really is not. So men go wrong sometimes by fearing the wrong things, sometimes by fearing in the wrong manner or at the wrong time, and so on.

And all of this applies equally to the things that inspire confidence.

The man, then, who endures and fears what he ought, from the right motive, in the right manner, and at the right time — and who feels confidence the same way — is courageous.

For the courageous man governs both his feeling and his action by the merits of each case, as reason bids.

Now, the end of every exercise of a habit is the same as the end of the habit itself.

To the courageous man, courage is essentially a noble thing.

Therefore its end is noble too; for everything takes its character from its end.

It is for the sake of the noble, then, that the courageous man endures and acts courageously in each particular case.

As for the characters that run to excess: the man who exceeds in fearlessness has no name (this often happens, as we said before) — but a man would have to be a maniac, or numb to pain, to fear nothing at all, not even earthquakes and breaking waves, as they say is true of the Celts.

The man who is overconfident in the face of fearful things is called foolhardy. But the foolhardy man is generally thought to be really a braggart, putting on a courage he does not have. What he wants is to seem, in the presence of danger, what the courageous man actually is; so he imitates him where he can. That is why your foolhardy man is usually a coward underneath: he blusters as long as it is safe, and turns tail when real danger comes.

The man who fears too much is a coward: he fears what he ought not, and as he ought not, and so through the whole list.

He is deficient in confidence as well; but what shows most is his excess of fear in the presence of pain.

The coward is also despondent, for he is frightened of everything. The courageous man is just the opposite: confidence implies hopefulness.

So the coward, the foolhardy man, and the courageous man all show their characters in the same circumstances, but behave differently in them. The first two exceed or fall short, while the courageous man holds the mean and behaves as he ought; and the foolhardy are headlong and eager before the danger comes but fall away in its presence, while the courageous are keen in the act and quiet beforehand.

Courage, then, as we have said, is observance of the mean with regard to things that excite confidence or fear, in the circumstances we have specified; and it chooses its course and holds its post because doing so is noble, or because failing to is disgraceful.

But to seek death as a refuge from poverty, or from love, or from anything painful, is not the act of a brave man but of a coward. It is softness to run from hardship; and a man who dies this way accepts death not because it is noble, but simply to escape an evil.

Chapter 8

Courage proper, then, is something of this sort.

But besides it there are five other things that go by the name of courage.

First, "civic courage" — the kind that most resembles the real thing.

Citizens often seem to face danger because of the legal pains and penalties on one side and the honors on the other; and this is why the most courageous peoples seem to be found in those states where cowards are disgraced and brave men honored.

This, too, is the courage that inspires Homer's characters — Diomede, for instance, and Hector.

  Polydamas will be the first to taunt me,

says Hector; and Diomede likewise:

  Hector one day will speak among his people
  and say, "The son of Tydeus, at my hand—"

This courage is most like the one we described above because its impulse is a virtuous one: a sense of honor, a desire for something noble (glory), and an aversion to reproach, which is disgraceful.

We might, perhaps, put in the same class men who are forced to fight by their officers; but they are a grade lower, because what drives them is not a sense of honor but fear, and what they are running from is not disgrace but pain. For those in authority compel them in Hector's own fashion:

  Whoever I catch skulking away from the fight
  will have no hope of saving his carcass from the dogs.

Commanders do the same thing when they order their men to stand and flog them if they run, or when they draw them up with a ditch at their backs: all of these, I mean, are using compulsion.

But a man ought to be courageous not because he is compelled to be, but because it is noble.

Second, experience of some particular business is sometimes taken for a kind of courage; this, in fact, is the basis of Socrates' notion that courage is knowledge.

Different people show this sort of courage in different fields, but professional soldiers show it above all in war. War is full of false alarms, and the regulars have seen through them many times; so they look courageous simply because the other troops do not understand the real state of the case.

Again, their experience makes the regulars more effective both in attack and in defense: they are skilled in handling their weapons, and they carry the kind of arms best suited for both purposes. So they fight with the advantage of armed men over unarmed, of trained men over untrained; for in athletic contests too it is not the bravest men who fight best, but the strongest and best conditioned.

But these regulars turn coward whenever the danger climbs too high and they find themselves outmatched in numbers and equipment. Then they are the first to run, while the citizen troops stand their ground and are cut to pieces — as happened at the temple of Hermes. For the citizens held it shameful to run, and preferred death to saving their lives on those terms; but the regulars had faced the danger in the first place only because they fancied themselves the stronger side, and when they learned the truth they ran, fearing death more than disgrace. And that is not what we mean by courageous.

Third, people sometimes count rage as part of what "courage" means.

Men who in sheer rage turn like wild beasts on those who have wounded them are taken for courageous, because the courageous man is also full of rage; for rage, more than anything, is eager to rush at danger. Hence Homer's phrases: "put might into his rage," "roused his wrath and rage," "fierce wrath breathed through his nostrils," "his blood boiled." All these expressions seem to signal rage waking up and bursting out.

Now the truly courageous man acts because it is noble to act, with rage backing him up. Beasts act from pain — from blows, or from fear; in a wood or a marsh they do not attack a man. So beasts are not courageous: it is pain and rage that drive them to rush at danger, without foreseeing any of the terrors involved. If that counted as courage, then hungry donkeys would have to be called courageous — beat them all you like, they will not stop eating. (Adulterers, for that matter, are driven by lust to do plenty of daring things.)

Being driven at danger by pain or rage, then, is not courage proper. Still, this kind — the kind whose impulse is rage — seems to be the most natural, and when deliberate purpose and the right motive are added to it, it becomes real courage.

Again, anger is a painful state, and revenge is pleasant; but those who fight from these motives — to escape the pain or to win the pleasure — may fight well, yet they are not courageous. They do not act because it is noble to act so, or as reason directs; they are driven by their feelings. Still, they bear some resemblance to the courageous man.

Fourth, the confident optimist is not properly called courageous either. He is confident in danger because he has won often and beaten many opponents. He and the courageous man resemble each other, since both are confident; but the courageous man is confident for the reasons already given, while the optimist is confident because he thinks he is the better man and will win without taking a scratch. (People behave the same way when they are drunk — drink makes optimists.) But when he finds out otherwise, he runs; whereas the mark of the courageous man, as we saw, is to face what is terrible to a human being, seeing the danger plainly, because facing it is noble and flinching from it is base.

And so it seems to take greater courage — this is the common view — to be fearless and cool in sudden danger than in danger that has been foreseen; for there the response must come more directly from formed character, since it depends less on preparation. When we can see what is coming, we can choose to meet it by calculation and reasoning; when it is upon us suddenly, we choose according to what we are.

Fifth, those who are unaware of their danger sometimes appear courageous. They are not far removed from the optimists just described, but they are inferior in that they need have no opinion of themselves at all, which the optimist must have. So while the optimist holds his ground for a time, these people — whose boldness rested on a false belief — run the moment they see or suspect that the case is otherwise; as the Argives did when they engaged the Spartans thinking they were Sicyonians.

We have now described what the courageous man is like, and what those who pass for courageous are like.

But there is one more point to notice.

Chapter 9

Courage, as we said, is concerned with feelings both of confidence and of fear; but not equally with both — more with occasions of fear. It is the man who keeps cool and behaves as he ought when things are frightening who is called courageous, rather than the man who does so when things look encouraging.

That is why, as we said, men are called courageous for enduring what is painful.

Courage therefore brings pain, and is justly praised; for it is harder to endure what is painful than to hold back from what is pleasant.

Not that the end of courage is unpleasant — I do not mean that. But it seems to be hidden from view by the circumstances that surround it, as happens in athletic contests too. Boxers have a pleasant end in view, the thing they are striving for — the crown and the honors; but the blows they take are grievous to flesh and blood, and painful, and so is all the training they endure. And since the painful parts are many while the end is small, the pleasantness of the end is hardly visible.

If courage is like this, then death and wounds will be painful to the courageous man, and against his will; but he endures them because enduring is noble and refusing is base.

And the more completely a man possesses every virtue, and the happier he is, the more his death will cost him. Life is worth more to a man of his sort than to anyone else, and he knows he is giving up the very best of things — and that is painful. But he is no less courageous for feeling the pain; we may even say he is more courageous, because in spite of it he chooses noble conduct in battle over all those goods.

So we see that the rule that exercising a virtue is pleasant does not hold for every virtue — except so far as the end is attained.

Still, there is perhaps no reason why men of this character should not make less efficient soldiers than men who are not courageous at all but have nothing good to lose; for such men are reckless of risk, and will sell their lives for a small price.

Here let us close our account of courage. From what we have said, it will not be hard to gather an outline of its nature.

Chapter 10

After courage, let us speak of self-control; for these two seem to be the virtues of the irrational parts of our nature.

We have already said that self-control is the observance of the mean with regard to pleasures (it is not concerned with pains so much, nor in the same way); and self-indulgence shows itself in the same field.

Let us now determine what kind of pleasures these are.

First, let us accept as established the distinction between pleasures of the body and pleasures of the soul — the pleasure of gratified ambition, say, or of the love of learning. When a lover of honor or of learning delights in the thing he loves, it is not his body that is affected but his mind. Now men are not called either self-controlled or self-indulgent for their behavior toward these pleasures, nor toward any other pleasures that are not bodily. People who are fond of gossip and storytelling, and fritter their days away on trifles, we call idle chatterers, but not self-indulgent; nor do we apply that term to those who take the loss of money or friends harder than they should.

Self-control, then, will be concerned with the pleasures of the body — but not even with all of these. Those who delight in what the eye takes in — colors, shapes, painting — are not called either self-controlled or self-indulgent; and yet it would seem possible to delight in these things as one ought, or more or less than one ought.

The same holds for hearing: no one is ever called self-indulgent for taking excessive delight in music or the theater, or self-controlled for enjoying them properly.

Nor do we apply these terms to those who delight in smells, except incidentally. We do not call people self-indulgent for delighting in the smell of fruit or roses or incense, but rather for delighting in the smell of perfumes and rich dishes; for the self-indulgent man delights in these smells because they remind him of the things he craves.

You may see other people, too, enjoying the smell of food when they are hungry; but delighting in such smells constantly is the mark of the self-indulgent man alone, since he alone is constantly craving what they promise.

The lower animals, moreover, get no pleasure through these senses, except incidentally. It is not the scent of the hare that delights a dog, but the eating of it; the scent only made the announcement. The lion rejoices not in the lowing of the ox but in devouring him; the lowing merely told him the ox was near, and so he appears to delight in the sound itself. Likewise, it is not the sight of a stag or a wild goat that pleases him, but the prospect of a meal.

Self-control and self-indulgence, then, have to do with the kinds of pleasure that we share with the lower animals — which is why these pleasures look slavish and brutish. I mean the pleasures of touch and taste.

Even taste seems to play only a small part here, or perhaps no part at all. The function of taste is to discriminate flavors, as wine-tasters do, and cooks seasoning their dishes; but it is by no means this fine discrimination that gives the delight — not to the self-indulgent, at any rate — but the actual enjoyment, and the medium of that is always the sense of touch, in eating and drinking alike, and in sex.

Hence the gourmand who wished his throat were longer than a crane's: what he meant was that his pleasure came through the touch.

The sense involved in self-indulgence, then, is the most common and widespread of all the senses; and so self-indulgence would seem to deserve its place as the most censured of the vices, since it attaches not to our human nature but to our animal nature.

To fix one's delight on things of this kind, and to love them above everything else, is brutish.

And further, even the more manly pleasures of touch lie outside the sphere of self-indulgence — the pleasure a gymnast takes in a rubdown and a warm bath; for the self-indulgent man does not cultivate the sense of touch over his whole body, but only in certain parts.

Chapter 11

Now, some of our desires or appetites appear to be common to the race, others individual and acquired.

The desire for food is natural, or common to the race: every man in want of it desires food or drink, or sometimes both — and sexual intercourse too, as Homer says, when he is young and vigorous.

But not everyone desires to satisfy these wants in this or that particular way, and not everyone desires the same things; so desire of that kind appears to be peculiarly our own — individual.

Of course it is also partly natural: different things please different people, and yet there are some things all men like better than others.

First, then, in the matter of the natural or common desires few people go wrong, and only in one direction — excess. To eat or drink whatever is set in front of you until you can hold no more is to exceed the natural measure in point of quantity, since natural appetite is simply for the filling of the want. That is why such people are called "belly-mad": they fill their bellies past the mark.

It is only thoroughly slavish natures that acquire this vice.

Second, with regard to the individual pleasures — those that attend the gratification of our individual desires — many people go wrong, and in many ways.

People are called "fond of" this or that because they delight in the wrong things, or to an unusual degree, or in the wrong fashion; and the self-indulgent exceed in all these ways at once. They delight in some things they ought not to delight in, since those things are hateful; and where delight is legitimate, they delight more than is right and more than most people do.

Plainly, then, excess in these pleasures is self-indulgence, and a thing to be blamed.

But with the corresponding pains the case is not the same as it was with courage. A man is not called self-controlled for bearing them and self-indulgent for failing to. Rather, the self-indulgent man is called self-indulgent because he is pained more than he ought to be at not getting certain pleasant things — his pleasure causing his pain — and the self-controlled man is called self-controlled because going without these pleasant things, or abstaining from them, does not pain him.

The self-indulgent man, then, desires all pleasant things, or the most intensely pleasant of them, and is led by his desire to choose them ahead of everything else. And so he is constantly in pain — pained at missing them, pained by craving them; for all appetite involves pain. It seems a strange arrangement, being pained for the sake of pleasure.

People who fall short in the matter of pleasure, and delight in these things less than they ought, are hardly found at all; such insensibility is scarcely human. Even the lower animals discriminate among kinds of food, and delight in some and not in others; and a being to whom nothing was pleasant, and to whom one thing was no different from another, would be a long way from being a man. He has no name, because he does not exist.

But the self-controlled man holds the mean in these things. He takes no pleasure in what the self-indulgent man most delights in — he is rather disgusted by it — nor, in general, in the wrong things, nor very much in any of them. When they are absent he is not pained, and he does not desire them — or desires them only moderately: not more than he ought, not at the wrong time, and so on. But things that are pleasant and at the same time conduce to health and good condition he will desire moderately and in the right way; and other pleasant things too, provided they are not harmful, or incompatible with what is noble, or beyond his means. A man who cares for such pleasures on any other terms cares for them more than they are worth; and the self-controlled man is not like that — he is guided by right reason.

Chapter 12

Self-indulgence seems to be more voluntary than cowardice.

For self-indulgence is driven by pleasure, cowardice by pain; and pleasure is something we choose, pain something we avoid. Moreover, pain unhinges us — it upsets the nature of the sufferer — while pleasure does nothing of the sort. Self-indulgence, therefore, is the more voluntary of the two.

For these reasons it is also the more blameworthy, and for another reason besides: it is easier to train oneself to behave rightly toward pleasure, since the occasions for it come up constantly in our lives and the training carries no risk; with occasions of fear the reverse is true.

Again, the settled character we call cowardice would seem to be more voluntary than the particular acts that display it. Being a coward is not itself painful; but the occasions that expose cowardice drive men out of their senses with the fear of pain, so that they throw away their weapons and disgrace themselves completely — which is why the particular acts are even thought to be compulsory. With the self-indulgent man it is the other way around: the particular acts are voluntary, for they are done with appetite and desire, but the character itself is less so; for no one desires to be self-indulgent.

The name we give to self-indulgence we apply also to the faults of children, for the two have a certain resemblance. Which of them is named after the other makes no difference for our present purpose; but plainly the later is named after the earlier.

And the metaphor, I think, is not a bad one. What needs "chastening," or correction, is whatever inclines toward base things and has great powers of growth; and nowhere are those two marks so strong as in appetite and in childhood. Children, like the self-indulgent, live by their appetites, and the desire for pleasant things is most pronounced in them. If this element is not made submissive and obedient to the governing principle, it will run to great lengths; for in an irrational being the desire for pleasant things is insatiable and will feed itself from any source, and every gratification of appetite strengthens the natural tendency — and if the gratifications are great and intense, they push reason out altogether. The gratifications of appetite, therefore, should be moderate and few; and appetite should never be set against reason — this is what we mean by calling it submissive and "chastened" — but should be ruled by reason, as a child should be ruled by his tutor.

And so the self-controlled man's appetites should be in harmony with his reason; for the aim of both is the noble. The self-controlled man desires what he ought, as he ought, and when he ought — and this, again, is exactly what reason prescribes.

This, then, may stand as our account of self-control.

Book IV

The Several Moral Virtues and Vices—Continued

Chapter 1

Generosity, which we will take up next, seems to be moderation in the matter of wealth. What we praise in a generous man is not his conduct in war, nor in the situations where self-control is praised, nor in passing judgment, but his conduct in the giving and taking of wealth — and above all in the giving. By "wealth" we mean everything whose value can be measured in money.

Extravagance and stinginess are the excess and the deficiency in the matter of wealth.

Stinginess always means caring about money more than one should. Extravagance, on the other hand, sometimes stands for a whole bundle of vices at once. Weak-willed people who blow their money on wild living get called extravagant, and so the extravagant are held to be thoroughly worthless characters, since they combine several vices in one.

But we should note that this is not the proper use of the term. The word itself — asotos, literally "a lost man" — is meant to pick out a man with one particular vice: wasting his own substance. He is "lost" because he is being destroyed by his own fault, and wasting what you live on is a kind of self-destruction, since your life depends on it. This, then, is what we will take "extravagance" properly to mean.

Now, anything that has a use can be used well or badly.

Riches are simply an abundance of useful things.

But each thing is best used by the man who has the virtue that deals with that thing.

Therefore riches will be best used by the man who has the virtue that deals with wealth — that is, the generous man.

The ways of using wealth are spending and giving; taking and keeping are rather ways of acquiring it. So it is more distinctive of the generous man to give to the right people than to take from the right sources and refuse the wrong ones. For it is more distinctive of virtue to do good than to have good done to you, and to act nobly than merely to avoid acting basely; and clearly doing good and acting nobly go with the giving, while receiving good and not acting basely go with the taking.

Again, our gratitude goes to the man who gives, not to the man who declines to take — and so does our praise.

Again, it is easier not to take than to give; we are more reluctant to part with what is our own than to take what belongs to someone else.

Again, it is the givers who are commonly called generous. Those who merely abstain from taking are praised not so much for generosity as for justice, and those who take are not praised at all.

Again, of all virtuous characters the generous man is perhaps the best loved, because he is useful — and his usefulness lies in his giving.

Now, virtuous acts, as we said, are noble and are done for the sake of the noble. The generous man, then, like the others, will give for the sake of the noble, and give rightly: to the right people, to the right extent, and at the right time — in short, his giving will have every mark of right giving.

Moreover, he will give with pleasure, or at least without pain; for virtuous acts are always pleasant or painless — certainly they are never painful.

The man who gives to the wrong people, or gives from some motive other than love of the noble, is not generous; he must be called something else.

Nor is the man generous who gives with a pained heart; that shows he would rather have the money than the noble act — which is not how the generous man feels.

Nor will the generous man take from the wrong sources; that kind of taking does not fit a man who sets no store by wealth.

Nor is he quick to ask favors; a man in the habit of doing good to others is not usually in a hurry to have good done to him.

But from the right sources he will take — from his own property, for instance — not because there is anything noble in taking, but simply because he cannot give without it. For the same reason he will not neglect his property, since he means to use it to help people. And he will refuse to give to just anyone who comes along, precisely so that he has something left to give to the right people, at the right times, and where giving is noble.

It is very characteristic of the generous man to overshoot in giving, leaving too little for himself; disregard of self is part of the character.

In calling a man generous we must take his means into account. What makes a gift generous is not the size of the sum but the settled character of the giver, and that character scales the gift to the giver's fortune. So it is entirely possible that the man who gives the smaller amount is the more generous man, if he gives it out of less.

Those who inherit a fortune seem to be more generous than those who made one. For one thing, they have never known want; for another, everyone is especially fond of what they made themselves — look at parents, or poets.

It is not easy for a generous man to be rich. He is not inclined to take or to keep, but very inclined to spend, and he values money not for its own sake but only for the sake of giving it away.

Hence the complaint people bring against fortune, that the men who most deserve wealth have the least of it. But this is natural enough: you cannot have wealth without taking trouble over it, any more than you can have anything else.

Still, the generous man will not give to the wrong people or at the wrong times. If he did, he would no longer be practicing generosity, and having spent that way he would not have enough left for the occasions that matter. For, as we said, the generous man is the one who spends in proportion to his fortune and on the proper objects; the man who overshoots that measure is extravagant. This is why kings are not called extravagant: it is not easy for them to outrun their possessions with their gifts and expenses.

Generosity, then, being moderation in the giving and taking of wealth, the generous man will give and spend the proper amounts on the proper objects, in small matters and great alike, and do it with pleasure; and he will also take the proper amounts from the proper sources. For since the virtue is moderation in both giving and taking, the man who has it will do both rightly. Right taking goes together with right giving; any other kind of taking is contrary to it. The givings and takings that go together are found in the same person; those that are contrary to each other obviously are not.

If it happens that the generous man spends something in a way that is contrary to what is right and noble, he will be pained — but moderately and in due measure; for it is characteristic of virtue to feel pleasure and pain on the right occasions and in due measure.

The generous man is also easy to deal with in money matters. He is not hard to cheat, since he does not value wealth, and he is more likely to be annoyed at having failed to spend where he ought than pained at having spent where he ought not — the sort of man Simonides would not approve of.

The extravagant man goes wrong on these points too: he is neither pleased nor pained on the right occasions or in the right way. But this will become clearer as we go on.

We have already said that extravagance and stinginess are each an excess and a deficiency at once, and in two things: giving and taking (spending counts under giving). Extravagance exceeds in giving and in not taking, and falls short in taking; stinginess falls short in giving and exceeds in taking — in small things, we should add.

Now, the two sides of extravagance are not often combined in one person. It is not easy to keep giving forever while never taking: private citizens soon run through their means, and it is private citizens who generally get the name.

The extravagant man of this pure type — the one who combines both elements — should be considered a good deal better than a stingy man. He is easily cured, both by age and by running out of money, and he can be brought round to the middle course. For he has the essentials of the generous character: he gives and he does not take; he simply does neither well nor as he ought. If he can be trained to that, or changed in any other way, he will be generous — he will give to the right people and refuse to take from the wrong sources. This is why he is generally felt to be no bad character: going too far in giving and in not taking is the mark not of a vicious or ignoble nature, but of a foolish one.

The extravagant man of this sort, then, seems much better than the stingy man — for the reasons just given, and also because he does good to many people, while the stingy man does good to no one, not even himself.

But most extravagant men, as we said, not only give wrongly but take from the wrong sources, and in that respect they are stingy. They become grasping because they want to spend and cannot easily do it — their supplies run out fast — and so they are forced to draw from elsewhere. And since they care nothing for the noble, they take quite recklessly, from any source at all; they long to give, and it does not matter a whit to them how the money goes or where it comes from.

That is why their gifts are not generous. They are not noble, not given for the sake of the noble, not given in the right way. Sometimes such men make rich the very people who ought to be poor: they will give nothing to a man of sober character, and a great deal to flatterers or to anyone who supplies them some other pleasure. Hence most of them are dissolute as well: being quick to part with money, they lavish it on wild living, and since they do not shape their lives toward the noble, they slide easily into the pursuit of pleasure.

The extravagant man, then, if he finds no guidance, ends up here; but with training he can be brought to the moderate and right course.

Stinginess, by contrast, is incurable — old age, and every kind of decline, seems to make men stingy.

It also runs deeper in human nature than extravagance: most men are fonder of money than of giving.

And it reaches far and comes in many forms, for there are, it seems, many ways of being stingy.

It has two parts — falling short in giving and going too far in taking — but it does not always appear whole; sometimes the parts come separately, one man exceeding in taking while another falls short in giving. All those who go by names like the tightwad, the skinflint, the miser fall short in giving, but they do not covet other people's goods or want to take them.

Some are driven to this by a kind of honesty — a wish to steer clear of disgrace. Some of them seem to save (or at least say they save) precisely so that they will never be forced to do anything disgraceful; the "cheeseparer" belongs here, along with his kind, so named for the extreme lengths to which he carries his refusal to give.

Others keep their hands off their neighbors' goods only out of fear, reckoning that it is no easy thing to take other people's property without having your own taken in turn; so they settle for neither taking nor giving.

Others again go to such excess in taking that they will make any gain they can, from anywhere at all — those who ply degrading trades, brothel-keepers and the like, and loan sharks who lend small sums at steep rates. All of these make money from the wrong sources and beyond the right amount.

What these last have in common is plainly the pursuit of base gain: every one of them puts up with disgrace for the sake of gain, and a small gain at that. For the men who make improper gains improperly on a grand scale are not called stingy — tyrants who sack cities and strip temples are called wicked, impious, unjust instead. But the dice-sharper, the man who steals clothes at the baths, the common thief — these count among the stingy, for they all make base gains. Both the thief and the sharper ply their trade for gain and endure disgrace for it: the thief runs the gravest dangers for his haul, and the sharper makes his profit off his friends — the very people he ought to be giving to. Both, then, wanting to make gain in the wrong ways, are seekers of base gain; and all money-making of this sort is stingy.

Stinginess is rightly named as the opposite of generosity. It is a worse evil than extravagance, and men more often go wrong in this direction than in the extravagance we described.

Let this stand, then, as our account of generosity and of the vices opposed to it.

Chapter 2

Our next task, it seems, is to examine magnificence, for this too appears to be a virtue concerned with wealth.

But it does not, like generosity, cover the whole field of money dealings — only those involving large outlays; and there it goes beyond generosity in sheer scale. For as the name itself suggests (megaloprepeia — "greatness that fits"), magnificence is suitable expenditure on a grand scale. But grandeur is relative: what it is suitable to spend when you are fitting out a warship is not what it is suitable to spend when you are heading a sacred embassy.

What is suitable, then, is relative to the person, the occasion, and the business at hand. And the man who spends fittingly on trivial or middling occasions is not called magnificent — the man who can say, with the poet,

  To many a wandering beggar did I give —

but rather the man who spends fittingly on great ones. The magnificent man is generous; but a man may be generous without being magnificent.

The deficiency of this quality is called cheapness; the excess is called vulgarity, bad taste, and so on. The mark of the excess is not spending too much on the proper objects, but making a show — spending on the wrong objects and in the wrong style. We will come to these presently.

The magnificent man is like a skilled artist: he can see what an occasion calls for, and he can spend great sums with taste. For as we said at the outset, a habit — a settled state of character — takes its stamp from the acts it issues in and the things it produces. The magnificent man's outlays, then, must be great and suitable.

And what he produces must be of the same kind, for only so will the expense be at once great and suited to the result.

The result, then, must be worthy of the expenditure, and the expenditure worthy of the result — or even beyond it.

Moreover, the magnificent man's motive in spending this way will be love of the noble; for that is common to all the virtues.

Further, he will spend gladly and with a free hand, since minute reckoning of costs is cheap. He will ask how the work can be made most beautiful and most fitting, not what it will cost and how it can be done most cheaply.

So the magnificent man must be generous as well. The generous man, too, will spend the right amount in the right manner; but it is in the "great" of great and right that magnificence goes beyond generosity, which deals with the same field — I mean in the actual size of the sum spent. And further, where the sum spent is the same, the magnificent man's outlay will produce the more magnificent result.

For the excellence of a possession is not the same as the excellence of a work. As a possession, the most prized thing is what is worth most — gold, say; as a work, what is great and beautiful, for the sight of such a work stirs admiration, and what is magnificent is always admirable. Excellence of work on a great scale — that is magnificence.

Now, there is a class of expenditures we call honorable in a special sense: expenditure on the worship of the gods — offerings, temples, sacrifices — and likewise everything spent on the worship of heroes, and again every public service undertaken from noble ambition, as when a man decides to equip a chorus or a warship, or to give a public feast, in handsome style.

But in every case, as we have said, we must look at the spender — who he is and what he has — for the outlay should be proportionate to the circumstances, and should suit not only the result but the man behind it.

Hence a poor man cannot be magnificent. He lacks the means to spend great sums suitably, and if he tries he is a fool, for he spends out of proportion and in the wrong way, and an act must be done in the right way to be virtuous. Such spending befits those who have the means — earned by their own efforts, or through ancestors or connections — and who have birth, reputation, and the rest; for all these things carry a certain greatness and standing with them.

The magnificent man, then, is properly a man of this sort, and magnificence shows itself most properly in expenditures of the kind we named, for these are the greatest and most honorable. But it can also appear on private occasions — the kind that come once in a lifetime, like a wedding; or the kind that concern the whole city or its leading men, like receiving foreign guests and sending them on their way, or giving them presents and returning theirs. For the magnificent man lavishes money not on himself but on public objects, and gifts to strangers have something in common with offerings to the gods.

A magnificent man will also build his house in a style that matches his wealth, for even a fine house is a kind of public ornament. And he will spend more readily on things that last, since these are the noblest. And on each occasion he will spend what suits it — for what suits the gods is not what suits men, nor a temple what suits a tomb.

And since every expenditure can be great of its kind — great outlay on a great occasion being the most magnificent, and after that what is great for the particular occasion, whatever it is — and since the greatness of the result is not the same as the greatness of the expense (the most beautiful ball or the most beautiful flask that can be had is a magnificent present for a child, though its price is small and cheap), it follows that it is the mark of the magnificent man to do magnificently whatever he does, of whatever kind — for work of that sort is not easily surpassed — and to produce a result worthy of the expense.

Such, then, is the magnificent man. The man of excess — the vulgar man, as we call him — exceeds, as we said, by spending improperly. He spends great sums on small occasions and makes an unseemly show of it: entertaining his club members with a wedding feast; bringing his chorus onstage in purple, when he is producing a comedy, as they did at Mesara. And he will do all this not from any love of the noble and beautiful, but to parade his wealth, hoping to be admired for it — spending little where he ought to spend much, and much where he ought to spend little.

The cheap man, on the other hand, will fall short on every occasion. Even when he is spending very large sums, he will ruin the beauty of the whole by penny-pinching over a trifle — never doing anything without hesitating over it, forever calculating how it can be done for the least possible cost, groaning even at that, and convinced that everything he does is on a needlessly grand scale.

Both these characters, then, are vicious; but they do not bring disgrace, since they neither harm anyone else nor are especially offensive in themselves.

Chapter 3

Greatness of soul, as its very name says, has to do with great things. Let us first pin down what those things are.

It will make no difference whether we examine the quality itself or the man who has it.

By a great-souled man we mean one who claims much and deserves much. The man who claims much without deserving it is a fool, and a man who possesses virtue is never a fool. So the man we have just described is the great-souled man.

The man who deserves little and claims little is modest and sensible, but not great-souled; for greatness of soul implies greatness, just as beauty implies stature. Small men may be trim and well proportioned, but they cannot be called beautiful.

The man who claims much without deserving it is vain — though not everyone who claims more than he deserves is vain.

The man who claims less than he deserves shows smallness of soul, whether his deserts are great or moderate, or small and he claims still less. And the fault seems greatest in the man whose deserts are great; for what would he do if he deserved less than he does?

The great-souled man, then, stands at an extreme in the greatness of what he deserves, but at the mean in how he carries himself; for he claims exactly what he deserves, while all the others claim too much or too little.

Now if he deserves much and claims much — and most of all deserves and claims the greatest things — there will be one thing above all that concerns him. For desert is measured in external goods. And the greatest of external goods, we may fairly say, is the one we render to the gods as their due, the one that people in high positions want most, and the one set as the prize for the noblest deeds. The thing that fits this description is honor — safely the greatest of all external goods. Honor and dishonor, therefore, are the field in which the great-souled man behaves as he ought.

Indeed we can see, without any argument, that honor is what great-souled men care about; honor is what they claim, above everything else, and what they deserve.

The small-souled man falls short, whether you measure his claims against his own deserts or against what the great-souled man claims for himself.

The vain or conceited man exceeds what is due to himself, though he does not out-claim the great-souled man.

But the great-souled man, since he deserves the greatest things, must be a perfectly good man; for the better man always deserves the greater things, and the best man the greatest. The truly great-souled man, therefore, must be good. In fact, greatness in every virtue would seem to be built into being great-souled.

It would be equally out of character for the great-souled man to run away swinging his arms and for him to commit an injustice. For what could he love enough to do something shameful for it, when nothing counts for much in his eyes?

Survey him point by point and you will find that the idea of a great-souled man who is not a good man is completely absurd. If he were not good, he could not even be worthy of honor; for honor is the prize of virtue, and it is paid to the good as their due.

Greatness of soul, then, seems to be a kind of crown of the virtues: it makes them greater, and it cannot exist without them. That is why true greatness of soul is a hard thing; it is impossible without the union of all the virtues.

The great-souled man, then, shows his character above all in the matter of honor and dishonor. At great honor from good men he will be moderately pleased, as merely getting his due — or even less than his due, since no honor can measure up to complete virtue. Still, he will accept it, since they have nothing greater to give him. But honor from ordinary people, on trivial grounds, he will flatly despise; that is not what he deserves. Dishonor he will likewise shrug off; he will never have earned it.

But though it is chiefly in the matter of honor, as we have said, that the great-souled man shows himself, he will also hold the mean in his feelings about wealth and power and every kind of good and bad fortune, whatever happens to him. He will be neither much elated by prosperity nor much cast down by adversity — after all, not even honor strikes him as a very great thing. Power and wealth are desirable for honor's sake (at least, those who have them want to be honored for them); so the man who thinks lightly of honor must think lightly of these too.

And so great-souled men seem to look down on everything.

Now the gifts of fortune are also commonly thought to contribute to greatness of soul. The well born are thought worthy of honor, and so are the powerful and the wealthy; they are in a position of superiority, and whatever is superior in some good thing is always held in greater honor. So these things do, in a sense, make people more great-souled, since such people are honored by some. But strictly, only the good man is worthy of honor — though the man who has goodness and good fortune together is commonly thought worthier still. Those who have these goods without virtue have no just claim to great things and are not properly called great-souled; neither is possible without complete virtue.

Instead, people who have these goods easily become arrogant and insolent. Without virtue it is not easy to carry the gifts of fortune gracefully; and so, unable to carry them, and thinking themselves better than everyone else, such people look down on others while doing whatever happens to please them. They imitate the great-souled man without really being like him, and they imitate him where they can: they do not show virtue in their actions, but they do look down on people. The great-souled man never looks down on anyone unjustly — his estimates are accurate — while most people who do it have no relevant grounds at all.

The great-souled man is not one to run into petty dangers, and he does not love danger, since there are few things he values much. But he is ready to face a great danger, and when he does, he does not spare his life; life is not worth keeping at every cost.

It is his nature to confer benefits, but he is ashamed to receive them; conferring is what a superior does, receiving is what an inferior does. And when he has received a favor, he tends to repay it with a greater one — that way his creditor becomes his debtor, and ends up on the receiving side.

Such men also seem to remember the benefits they have given better than those they have received (for the recipient of a benefit is the inferior of the benefactor, and this man wants to be the superior); they like being reminded of the one, and dislike being reminded of the other. That is why we read that Thetis would not mention to Zeus the services she had done him, and why the Spartans, negotiating with the Athenians, brought up the benefits Sparta had received rather than the ones she had conferred.

It is characteristic of him, again, never to ask favors, or only reluctantly, but to be ready to grant them; to be lofty toward the powerful and the fortunate, but unassuming toward people of middle rank. To assert superiority over the great is difficult and dignified; to assert it over the humble is easy. A haughty manner among the great is perfectly good breeding; among the lowly it is brutal — like showing off your strength on a cripple.

Another mark of him: he does not rush in wherever honor is on offer, or go where others hold first place. He stays aloof and holds back, except where there is great honor to be won or a great work to be done — not many things, but great and notable ones.

Again, he must be open in his hatreds and open in his loves (concealing your feelings — caring less for truth than for what people will think — is cowardly), and open in word and deed. His sense of his own superiority makes him outspoken, and he is truthful, except insofar as he takes an ironic tone with the masses. And he cannot shape his life to suit someone else, unless it is a friend; that is servile — which is why all flatterers and hangers-on of great men are slavish by nature, and why men of low nature become flatterers.

He is not easily moved to admiration; nothing is great to him.

He readily forgets injuries. Brooding on the past, especially on past wrongs, is not in his character; he prefers to overlook them.

He is no gossip. He will not talk about himself or about others, for he does not care to be praised, or to have others blamed (though he is not quick to hand out praise either). So he is not given to speaking ill of people, not even his enemies — except deliberately, to insult them.

When something happens that cannot be helped, or that matters little, he is the last man alive to complain or beg for help; that is how a man behaves who thinks such things very important.

He would rather own beautiful things that bring no profit than useful things that pay; that is the mark of a man whose resources are within himself.

Further, the character of the great-souled man seems to call for a slow step, a deep voice, and measured speech. A man is not likely to hurry when few things deeply interest him, or to get worked up when nothing strikes him as very important — and hurry and excitement are what produce a shrill voice and rapid movements.

This, then, is the great-souled man.

The man who falls short of this quality is small-souled; the man who overshoots it is vain or conceited.

Now these two do not seem to be bad men — they do no harm — but they are in error.

The small-souled man deserves good things and robs himself of what he deserves. He seems the worse for not claiming them, and for misjudging himself; if he judged rightly, he would desire what he deserves, since it is good. Not that such people seem to be fools — rather, too retiring. But a misjudgment of this kind really does seem to make them worse; for men strive for what they think they deserve, and these men shrink back from noble deeds and undertakings, thinking themselves unworthy of them, just as they shrink from external goods.

Vain men, on the other hand, are fools as well as ignorant of themselves, and they make it obvious. They take on honorable offices they are unfit for, and are promptly exposed as incapable.
