They dress in fine clothes and put on fine airs and all the rest of it; they want the whole world to know their good fortune; they talk about themselves, as if that were the road to honor.

But smallness of soul is more opposed to greatness of soul than vanity is; it is both commoner and worse.

Greatness of soul, then, as we have said, has to do with honor on the grand scale.

Chapter 4

But it appears (as we said at the outset) that there is also a virtue concerned with honor which stands to greatness of soul as generosity stands to magnificence. Both this virtue and generosity have nothing to do with great things; they make us behave properly in matters of moderate or trifling importance. Just as in the taking and giving of money it is possible to hit the mean, and also to exceed it or fall short of it, so in the desire for honor it is possible to go too far or not far enough — or, again, to desire honor from the right source and in the right manner.

We call a man ambitious as a term of reproach, meaning that he desires honor more than he ought and from the wrong sources; and we call a man unambitious as a term of reproach, meaning that he does not want to be honored even for noble deeds.

But sometimes we call a man ambitious as a term of praise, meaning that he is manly and loves noble things; and sometimes we call a man unambitious as a term of praise, meaning that he is moderate and sensible (as we said at the outset).

Plainly, then, "fond of" a thing is said in several senses, and the term "fond of honor" does not always mean the same: as praise it means fonder than most men are, as reproach it means fonder than is right. And since there is no recognized name for the observance of the mean, the extremes fight, so to speak, over what looks like an empty seat. But wherever there is excess and deficiency there is also a mean; and honor is in fact desired more than is right, and less; therefore it can also be desired to the right degree. This character, then, is praised — it is observance of the mean in the matter of honor — though it has no recognized name. Compared with ambition it looks like lack of ambition; compared with lack of ambition it looks like ambition; compared with both at once it looks, in a way, like both at once. We can see the same thing happening with the other virtues. But in this case the extreme characters seem to be opposed to one another, rather than to the middle character, because the character who observes the mean has no recognized name.

Chapter 5

Gentleness is the observance of the mean with respect to anger. But note that we have no recognized name for the mean here, and scarcely any recognized names for the extremes. So the term gentleness — which strictly leans toward the deficiency in anger (itself nameless) — gets applied to the mean.

The excess may be called wrathfulness; for the emotion involved is wrath, or anger, though the things that provoke it are many and various.

The man who is angry on the right occasions and with the right persons, and also in the right manner, at the right season, and for the right length of time, is praised. Let us call him gentle, then, since gentleness is used as a term of praise. For the man we call gentle wants to keep his balance — not to be carried off by his emotions, but to be angry only in the manner, on the occasions, and for the length of time that reason prescribes. If he errs, he seems to err on the side of deficiency: he is slow to take revenge and quick to forgive.

The deficiency, though — call it wrathlessness or what you will — is blamed. People who are not angered by what ought to anger them seem to be fools, and so do those who are not angry in the way, at the time, and with the persons they ought to be. Such a man seems to feel nothing and to be pained by nothing, and, never being angered, to lack the spirit to defend himself. And to let yourself be insulted, or to stand quietly by while your friends are insulted, is slavish.

It is possible to exceed on every point: to be angry with people one ought not to be angry with, at things one ought not to be angry at, more than one ought, more quickly, and for longer. Not that all these errors show up in the same person — that would be impossible; for evil destroys itself, and if it appears complete, it becomes unbearable.

So we find that wrathful men get angry fast, with the wrong people and at the wrong things, and more than they ought; but they get over it just as fast, and that is the best point in their character. The reason is that they do not hold their anger in: their quick temper makes them retaliate at once, so that what is in them comes out into the open — and then they are done with it.

People called choleric are excessively quick-tempered, flaring up at anything and on any occasion; hence the name, which in Greek suggests boiling over.

Sulky men are hard to appease, and their anger lasts a long time, because they keep it in. For the moment we retaliate, we are relieved: vengeance ends our anger, replacing pain with pleasure. But the sulky man gets no such relief; he carries the weight of his wrath around with him. No one even tries to talk him out of it, since he does not show it, and it takes a long time to digest one's anger inside oneself. Such men are a great trial to themselves and to their closest friends.

Lastly, hard is the name we give to those who are offended by things that ought not to offend them, and more than they ought, and for longer — and who will not be appeased without vengeance or punishment.

Of the two extremes, the excess is the more opposed to gentleness. It is commoner (human beings incline more naturally to vengeance), and a hard-tempered person is worse to live with than one who is too easygoing.

What we said some time ago is made abundantly clear by what we have just been saying: it is not easy to define how, with whom, at what, and for how long one ought to be angry — how far one may rightly go, and at what point the fault begins. The man who strays a little from the right course is not blamed, whether toward excess or toward deficiency; sometimes we praise those who fall short and call them gentle, and sometimes we call the harsh-tempered manly, as men fit to rule. But how large and what kind of error makes a man blamable can hardly be defined; it depends on the particular circumstances of each case, and only immediate perception can decide it.

But this much at least is clear: the habit that observes the mean is to be praised — the habit that makes us angry with the right persons, at the right things, in the right manner, and so on — while all the habits of excess and deficiency deserve censure: slight censure if the error is trifling, graver if it is considerable, severe if it is great.

It is evident, therefore, that we must strive for the habit that observes the mean.

Let this stand, then, as our account of the habits that have to do with anger.

Chapter 6

In social life — living alongside others, sharing their conversation and their common business — some people show themselves obsequious: to please you they praise everything, never object to anything, and think their whole duty is to avoid giving pain to anyone they meet. Those who take the opposite line, who object to everything and never stop for a moment to think what pain they may cause, are called quarrelsome and sour.

It is plain enough that both these habits deserve blame, and that the habit which takes the middle course between them deserves praise — the habit that makes a man go along with what he should, in the right way, and likewise refuse to go along. This type of character has no recognized name, but it looks most like friendliness. For the man who shows this moderation is just the sort of man we mean when we speak of an upright friend — except that a friend also feels affection. The difference from friendship is that this quality involves no emotion, no affection for the people we deal with. The man who has it goes along when he should, not because he loves or hates, but because that is his character. He will behave the same way toward people he knows and people he does not, toward intimates and strangers — only, in each case, as is fitting; for we do not owe strangers the same consideration we owe our intimates, or the same care not to pain them.

We have already said in general terms that such a man will behave as he ought with others; but we must add that, while he tries to add to their pleasure and to avoid giving them pain, he always steers by what is noble and fitting. The pleasures and pains of social life seem to be his particular field. Whenever he finds it would not be noble — or would be positively harmful to himself — to help along one of these pleasures, he will refuse to go along and will choose to give pain instead. And if a pleasure would bring discredit on the man producing it, and no small discredit, or would do him some injury, while opposing it costs only a little pain, he will not go along with it; he will set his face against it. He will behave differently in the company of important people and of ordinary people, of close friends and of mere acquaintances, and so on, giving each his due — preferring, other things being equal, to promote pleasure, and reluctant to give pain, but letting the consequences govern his conduct when the consequences are serious: by consequences I mean what is noble and fitting. So for the sake of a great pleasure later, he will inflict a small pain now.

The man who hits the mean here, then, is something of this sort, but he has no recognized name.

The man who always makes himself pleasant, if he aims simply at pleasing and has nothing further in view, is obsequious; but if he does it to get some profit for himself, in money or in what money buys, he is a flatterer.

And the man who sets his face against everything is, as we have said, quarrelsome and sour.

Here the extremes seem to be opposed to one another rather than to the mean, because the mean has no name.

Chapter 7

The mean that lies between boasting and irony — this virtue, too, lacks a name — shows itself in almost the same field.

It will be worth examining these qualities as well; for we shall know more about human character when we have gone through each of its forms, and we shall be more fully convinced that the virtues are ways of observing the mean when we have surveyed them all and found that this holds of every one.

We have spoken of the characters that social life displays where pleasure and pain are concerned; let us now speak in the same way of those who show themselves truthful or untruthful — in what they say, in what they do, and in the claims they put forward.

First of all, then, the boaster seems fond of claiming the things people admire when he does not have them, or not to the degree he pretends; the ironical man, on the other side, disclaims what he has, or plays it down; while the man who observes the mean — the man who is "always himself" — is truthful in word and deed, admitting the plain facts about himself and neither inflating nor shrinking them.

Now each of these lines of conduct can be pursued with some further object in view, or with none.

When a man has nothing further in view, he speaks and acts and lives according to his character.

But falsehood is in itself vile and blamable, and truth is noble and praiseworthy in itself.

And so the truthful man, who observes the mean, deserves praise, while both the untruthful characters deserve blame — the boaster more than the ironical man.

Let us speak of each of them, then, and first of the truthful character.

Remember that we are not talking about the man who tells the truth in business dealings, or in matters that fall under justice and injustice — those belong to another virtue. The man we mean is the one who, where nothing so weighty is at stake, is truthful in his speech and in his life because that is his character.

Such a man would seem to be a good man. For he who loves truth, and tells it where nothing hangs on it, will tell it all the more surely where serious interests are involved; he will shun falsehood there as something base, having already shunned it elsewhere for its own sake — and such a man deserves praise.

He leans toward understating the truth rather than overstating it, and this seems the more fitting course, since all exaggeration is offensive.

The man who claims more than he has with nothing further in view — the boaster proper — seems not to be a good character (a good man would take no pleasure in falsehood), but he seems silly rather than bad.

Of boasters who do have a further object, the one whose object is reputation or honor is not censured very severely (no more than the boaster proper is); but the one whose object is money, or the means of making money, is held in greater disgrace.

Notice, though, what marks off the boaster proper from the other kinds of boasters: not his ability to boast, but his preference for boasting. He is a boaster by habit, because that is his character — just as there is, on one side, the liar proper, who delights in the falsehood itself, and on the other the liar who lies out of desire for honor or gain.

Those who boast for reputation claim the things that win a man praise or get him counted happy; those whose motive is gain claim the things that benefit others and whose absence can escape detection — skill in magic, say, or in medicine. And so it is usually things of this sort that men claim and boast of, since they meet both conditions.

Ironical people, on the other hand, with their way of talking themselves down, seem the more refined characters; for their motive seems to be not love of gain but a wish to avoid parade. And what they disclaim is, notably, exactly what people admire — Socrates was an instance of this.

But those who disclaim small advantages they obviously possess are called affected humbugs, and are the more easily despised. And sometimes this self-deprecation is barely distinguishable from boasting — dressing like a Spartan, for instance; for there is something boastful in extreme depreciation just as in exaggeration.

Those who use irony in moderation, and are ironical about matters not too obvious and plain, come across as men of refinement.

Finally, the boaster seems to be the special opposite of the truthful man; for he is worse than the ironical man.

Chapter 8

Again, since relaxation has its place in our life, and one form of relaxation is amusing conversation, it seems that here too there is a proper way of mixing with others — that there are things it is right to say, and a right way of saying them; and the same holds for listening. And here also it will make a difference what sort of people you are speaking in front of, or listening to.

Plainly, in these matters too, one can go beyond the mean or fall short of it.

Now those who go to excess in ridicule seem to be buffoons and vulgar fellows, straining for a laugh at any cost, and more set on raising the laugh than on keeping their jokes elegant and painless to their target. While those who will never say anything funny themselves, and scowl at those who do, are considered boorish and morose. But those who joke gracefully are called witty — men of ready wit, as it were quick and versatile men.

For a man's character seems to reveal itself in these playful sallies, and so we judge his moral makeup by them, just as we judge his body by its movements.

But because ridiculous things are everywhere, and most people take more delight in fun and joking than they should, the buffoon is often called witty because he delights his audience. That there is a difference between the two, and no small one, is plain from what we have said.

One element of the character that observes the mean here is tact. The man of tact will say, and listen to, only the sort of thing that suits an honest man and a gentleman; for there are things it is quite becoming for such a man to say and to hear by way of jest, and the jesting of a gentleman differs from that of a slavish nature, the jesting of an educated man from that of an uneducated one.

You can see this in the difference between the old comedy and the new: the earlier writers got their laughs from obscenity, the later ones from innuendo — and as decency goes, that is no small difference.

Can we define good jesting, then, as making jokes that befit a gentleman? Or jokes that do not pain the hearer — or that even give him pleasure? Surely that last is hopelessly indefinite, for different things are hateful and pleasant to different people.

But the things he will listen to will be of the same sort as the things he will say: the jokes a man can listen to, we think, he can make himself.

So there are jokes he will not make — though we cannot define them exactly; for to make a joke of a man is to vilify him in a way, and the law forbids certain kinds of vilification, and perhaps ought to forbid certain kinds of jesting too.

The refined and gentlemanly man, therefore, will regulate his wit accordingly, being as it were a law to himself.

Such, then, is the character of the man who observes the mean, whether we call him a man of tact or a man of ready wit.

The buffoon, on the other hand, cannot resist an opening for a joke; if he can just raise a laugh, he will spare neither himself nor anyone else, and will say things no man of refinement would say — some of which he would not even listen to.

The boor, lastly, is wholly useless in this kind of company: he contributes nothing and takes everything badly. And yet relaxation and amusement seem to be necessary ingredients in our life.

The ways of observing the mean in social life that we have described, then, are three in number, and all have to do with conversation or shared activity of some kind. They differ in that one is concerned with truth, while the other two are concerned with what is pleasant; and of the two concerned with pleasure, one has its field in our amusements, the other in all the rest of social life.

Chapter 9

Shame — the modesty of our earlier list — cannot properly be called a virtue, for it is more like a feeling or emotion than a habit. At any rate, it is defined as a kind of fear of disgrace, and it works much like the fear that danger excites: men blush when they are ashamed, while the fear of death turns them pale. Both, then, seem to be in a way physical, and that is held to be the mark of a feeling or emotion rather than of a habit.

Again, this feeling does not suit every time of life, but only youth. We think it proper for young people to be ready to feel shame because, living as they do by their emotions, they often go wrong, and shame holds them back from wrong actions.

And so we praise young men who are ready to feel shame, but no one would praise an older man for being apt to be ashamed; for we think he ought not to do anything that could make him ashamed of himself in the first place.

Indeed, shame is not the mark of a good man, since it is what vile acts occasion — and such acts should simply not be done. (Nor does it matter that some acts are really shameful and others shameful only in public opinion; neither kind should be done, and so a man ought not to be feeling shame.) It is the mark of a worthless man to be the sort who would do something shameful.

And if a man's character is such that he would be ashamed if he did one of these shameful acts, it is absurd for him to imagine that this makes him a good man; for shame attaches only to voluntary acts, and a good man will never voluntarily do vile acts.

At most, shame would be good conditionally: if he were to do the act, a good man would be ashamed. But there is nothing conditional about the virtues.

Again, granted that it is bad to be shameless — to feel no shame at doing shameful things — it does not follow that it is good to do them and be ashamed of it.

Self-restraint, in the same way, is not a virtue either, but something between virtue and vice.

But we will explain this point about self-restraint later; let us now treat of justice.

Book V

Justice

Chapter 1

We come now to justice and injustice. We have to ask what sorts of acts they are concerned with, in what sense justice observes the mean, and what the extremes are between which the just lies. And in this inquiry we will follow the same method as before.

We see that when people speak of justice, they all mean the sort of habit or character that makes a man apt to do just things — that makes him act justly and wish for what is just. And by injustice they mean, in the same way, the character that makes a man act unjustly and wish for what is unjust. Let us set this down, then, as our outline to work from.

Notice that we can start this way with justice, because a habit — a trained state of character — differs in one respect from a science or a faculty. Both members of a pair of opposites fall under one and the same science or faculty: medicine deals with sickness as well as health. But a habit that produces one result does not also produce the opposite result. Health produces only healthy performances, never unhealthy ones; we say a man walks healthily when he walks the way a healthy man walks.

Not that the two opposites are unconnected. In the first place, a habit is often known from its opposite habit, and often from its causes and results. If we know what good condition is, we can learn from it what bad condition is; and from the things that produce good condition we can infer what good condition itself is, and the other way around. If good condition is firmness of flesh, for instance, then bad condition must be flabbiness of flesh, and whatever tends to produce firmness of flesh tends to produce good condition.

In the second place, if one of a pair of opposite terms has more than one sense, then as a general rule the other has more than one sense too. So if "just" has several senses, "unjust" will have several as well.

And in fact it seems that both "justice" and "injustice" do have several senses. But because the different things covered by the common name are so closely related, the difference escapes notice. It does not strike us the way it does when the things are far apart — different in outward appearance, say. Everyone sees at once, for instance, that the kleis that lies under an animal's neck — the collarbone — is a different thing from the kleis we fasten the door with — the key — though the Greek word is the same.

Let us ask, then, in how many different senses a man is called unjust.

First, the man who breaks the laws is considered unjust; and second, so is the man who takes more than his share — the unfair man.

Plainly, then, the just man will be (1) the law-abiding man and (2) the fair man.

And a just thing will be (1) what is lawful and (2) what is fair; an unjust thing will be (1) what is unlawful and (2) what is unfair.

Now since the unjust man, in the second sense of the word, takes more than his share, his sphere of action will be goods — not all goods, but the ones good and bad fortune deal in: things that are always good in themselves, but not always good for us. These are what we humans pray for and chase after. We ought to do otherwise — pray that what is good in itself may also be good for us, and choose what is actually good for us.

But the unjust man does not always take more than his share. Sometimes he takes less — less, that is, of things that are bad in themselves. Since the lesser evil counts in a way as a good, though, and since grasping for more means grasping for more good, he is still said to take more than his share. In any case he is unfair; that is the wider term, and it covers both.

We found that the lawbreaker is unjust and the law-abiding man just. It follows that whatever is lawful is just in one sense of the word. And this is in fact how we talk; for whatever the legislator prescribes is lawful, and we call every such prescription just.

Now, the laws pronounce on all manner of things, aiming at the common interest of all, or of the best men, or of those who hold power in the state (a position determined by personal excellence or by some other such standard). So in one sense we apply the term just to whatever tends to produce and preserve the happiness of the political community, and the parts of that happiness. The law commands us to do the acts of a brave man — not to desert our post, not to run, not to throw away our weapons; and the acts of a self-controlled man — not to commit adultery or outrage; and the acts of a gentle man — not to strike or insult our neighbors; and so on through all the other virtues and vices, enjoining some acts and forbidding others — rightly, when the law is a good one, less rightly when it is a hasty improvisation.

Justice in this sense, then, is complete virtue — with this addition, that it is shown toward others. That is why it is often called the chief of the virtues, greater than any other; why the poet says "neither evening star nor morning star is so wonderful"; and why the saying has become a proverb: "Justice sums up all virtues in itself."

It is complete virtue, first, because it is the exercise of complete virtue. And it is complete because the man who has it can use his virtue in dealing with his neighbors, and not merely in his own affairs. Plenty of people manage to be virtuous at home and fail the moment they deal with their neighbors.

This is why people approve the saying of Bias: "Office will show the man." The man who holds office stands, by that very fact, in relation to others and has dealings with them.

This is also why justice, alone among the virtues, is thought to be "another's good": it implies this relation to others. What justice aims at is someone else's interest — the interest of a ruler, or of our fellow citizens.

The worst man, then, is the one who exercises his vice both toward himself and toward his friends. And the best man is not the one who exercises his virtue in his own affairs only, but the one who exercises it toward others — for that is the hard thing to do.

Justice in this sense, then, is not a part of virtue but the whole of it; and the injustice opposed to it is not a part of vice but the whole of it.

How virtue differs from justice in this sense is plain from what we have said. They are one and the same character, differently viewed: seen in relation to others, that character is justice; seen simply as a certain kind of character, it is virtue.

Chapter 2

But what we are looking for is the justice that is a part of virtue — for we maintain there is such a thing — and likewise the corresponding injustice, the one that is a part of vice.

That the word is used this way is easy to show. In the case of the other kinds of badness, the man who displays them acts unjustly, in one sense of the word, but he does not take more than his share. When a man throws away his shield out of cowardice, or reviles someone out of bad temper, or refuses to help a man with money out of stinginess, he takes nothing extra. But when a man does take more than his share, he often displays none of those vices — certainly not all of them — and yet he does display some kind of badness, since we blame him for it: namely injustice, in the second sense of the word.

So there is another sense of "injustice," in which it names a part of that whole injustice which is coextensive with badness; and another sense of "unjust," in which it applies to only a part of the things called unjust in the broad sense of "contrary to law."

Again, suppose one man commits adultery for gain and makes money by it, while another does it out of appetite, spending money and losing by it. The second man would be called not grasping but self-indulgent; the first would be called not self-indulgent but unjust, in the narrow sense. Clearly, then, it is because of the gain that he is called unjust.

Once more: every other unjust act is referred back to some particular vice. If a man commits adultery, we refer the act to self-indulgence; if he deserts his comrade in arms, to cowardice; if he strikes someone, to anger. But when a man makes an unjust gain, the act is referred to no other vice than injustice.

It is plain, then, that besides the injustice which is coextensive with vice there is a second kind of injustice — a particular vice. It bears the same name as the first because the same general notion underlies both definitions: both show themselves in our dealings with others. But the second is limited to things like honor, wealth, and security (perhaps a single name could be found to cover the whole class), and its motive is the pleasure of gain; while the sphere of the first is everything the good man's action covers.

We have established, then, that there is more than one kind of justice — that besides the kind identical with complete virtue there is another kind — and we now have to find what it is and what it is like.

We have already distinguished two senses of "unjust" — (1) the unlawful and (2) the unfair — and two senses of "just" — (1) the lawful and (2) the fair.

The injustice we have already dealt with corresponds to the unlawful.

But the unfair is not the same as the unlawful; they differ as part from whole. Everything unfair is unlawful, but not everything unlawful is unfair. So the unjust and injustice that correspond to the unfair are not the same as those that correspond to the unlawful, but differ from them as a part from the whole: this injustice is a part of complete injustice, and this justice a part of complete justice. It is justice and injustice in this limited sense, then — and the just and the unjust in this limited sense — that we must now discuss.

So let us set aside the justice that coincides with complete virtue, which is the exercise of complete virtue toward others, and the corresponding injustice, the exercise of complete vice.

It is also easy to see how the just and the unjust corresponding to them are to be defined — the lawful and the unlawful. The great bulk of lawful acts, we may say, are the acts the law commands with a view to complete virtue; for the law orders us to live out all the virtues and forbids us to live out any of the vices.

And the acts that tend to produce complete virtue are those lawful acts prescribed with a view to educating a man as a citizen. As for the education of the individual as such — the education that makes him simply a good man — we may set aside for now the question whether it belongs to the science of the state or to some other; for possibly being a good man is not the same as being a good citizen of any state whatever.

Now, of justice as a part of virtue — particular justice — and of the just in the corresponding sense, one kind is shown in the distribution of honor, wealth, and the other things that get divided among the members of the political community; for there one man's share can be fair or unfair compared with another's. The other kind is corrective: it sets things right in private transactions.

This corrective kind is subdivided in turn, for private transactions are of two sorts: (1) voluntary and (2) involuntary.

Voluntary transactions, or contracts, are things like selling, buying, lending at interest, pledging, lending without interest, depositing, and hiring. They are called voluntary because the parties enter into them of their own free will.

Involuntary transactions are of two kinds. Some involve secrecy: theft, adultery, poisoning, procuring, luring away slaves, assassination, false witness. Others involve open violence: assault, imprisonment, murder, robbery, rape, maiming, slander, insult.

Chapter 3

The unjust man, in this limited sense of the word, is unfair, we say, and the unjust thing is unfair.

Now, plainly there is a mean lying between the unfair on one side and the unfair on the other. And this is the fair, or equal; for in any act that admits of a too much and a too little, there is also a fair amount.

If the unjust is the unfair, then, the just is the fair — which everyone in fact admits without further argument.

And since the fair or equal is a mean between two extremes, it follows that the just is a mean.

But equality — fairness — implies at least two terms.

It follows that the just is at once a mean quantity and a fair amount, relative to something and to certain persons. As a mean, it lies between certain other quantities, a more and a less; as an equal or fair amount, it involves two shares; and as a just amount, it involves certain persons.

The just, then, implies at least four terms: two persons for whom it is just, and two things.

And the same "equality" — the same ratio — must hold between the persons as between the things: as the things are to one another, so must the persons be. For if the persons are not equal, their shares will not be equal; and this is exactly where disputes and accusations come from — when equals receive unequal shares, or unequals receive equal shares.

The point is plain, too, from the common phrase "according to merit." Everyone agrees that just distribution must go by merit or worth of some kind; what they do not agree on is the standard of worth. In democratic states the standard is free birth; in oligarchic states, wealth, or in some, noble birth; in the true aristocratic state, virtue or personal merit.

The just, then, is something proportionate. For proportion is not a property of abstract numbers only but of anything that can be counted; proportion means equality of ratios, and it requires at least four terms.

That a discrete proportion requires four terms is obvious at once. But a continuous proportion requires four terms too, for it uses one term as two and repeats it. For instance: a is to b as b is to c. The term b is repeated; counting b twice over, we find the terms of the proportion are four in number.

The just, then, requires at least four terms, and the ratio between the two pairs must be the same: the persons must stand to one another in the same ratio as the things.

Let us say, then: as a is to b, so c is to d — or, rearranging the terms, as a is to c, so b is to d.

The sums of the new pairs will then stand to one another in the original ratio: a plus c is to b plus d as a is to b, or as c is to d.

But these pairs are exactly the pairs the distribution joins together; and if the things are assigned in this way, the assignment is just.

This joining of a to c and of b to d, then, is the just in distribution; and the just in this sense is a mean quantity, while the unjust is what breaks the proportion. For the proportionate is a mean, and the just, as we said, is proportionate.

Mathematicians call this kind of proportion a geometrical proportion; for it is when four terms are in geometrical proportion that the sum of the first and third stands to the sum of the second and fourth in the original ratio of the first to the second, or the third to the fourth.

But this proportion, as it applies in justice, cannot be a continuous one; for no single term can stand for both a person and a thing.

The just in this sense, then, is the proportionate, and the unjust is the disproportionate. In the unjust case one quantity becomes more, or too much, and the other less, or too little. And this is what we actually see in practice: the man who wrongs another gets too much of the good in question, and the man who is wronged gets too little. With evil it runs the other way; for the lesser evil, compared with the greater, counts as a good — the lesser evil is more desirable than the greater, what is desirable is good, and what is more desirable is a greater good.

This, then, is one form of the just.

Chapter 4

It remains to treat the other form: corrective justice, the just in the way of redress, whose sphere is private transactions, voluntary and involuntary alike.

This differs in kind from the form we have just discussed.

For the just in the distribution of a common stock of goods always follows the proportion described above — even when it is a common fund being divided, the amounts the several partners take must stand in the same ratio as the amounts they put in — and the unjust in the corresponding sense is what violates this proportion.

But the just in private transactions, though it too is a sort of equality (and the unjust a sort of inequality), follows not geometrical proportion but arithmetical.

For here it makes no difference whether a good man has defrauded a bad man or a bad man a good one, or whether the man who committed adultery is good or bad. The law looks only to the difference the injury makes; it treats the parties as equals, and asks only whether one has done a wrong and the other suffered it — whether one inflicted damage and the other received it.

The unjust here, then, is something unequal, and the judge tries to make it equal. For even when one man strikes and another is struck, or one kills and another is killed, the doing and the suffering are divided unequally; the judge tries to restore equality by the penalty or loss he lays on the offender, subtracting it from his gain.

For in such cases, though the terms do not always quite fit, we generally speak of the doer's "gain" — the striker's, say — and the sufferer's "loss"; at any rate, once the court has assessed the injury, what the doer gets is called his "loss" or penalty, and what the sufferer gets is called his "gain."

The equal or fair, then, is a mean between more, or too much, and less, or too little. But gain and loss are each a more and a less in opposite ways: gain is more good and less evil, and loss the opposite.

And in the mean between them lies, as we found, the equal or fair — which we say is just.

Corrective justice, then, the just in the way of redress, is the mean between loss and gain. That is why, when disputes arise, men go to the judge; and going to the judge is going to justice, for the judge is meant to be, as it were, living justice. Men look for the middle from a judge — they ask that he be moderate, that he observe the mean — and some even call judges "mediators" (mesidioi, middle-men), meaning that if they hit the mean, they will hit the just.

The just, then, must be a sort of mean, if the judge is a "mediator."

What the judge does is restore equality. It is as if he found a line divided into two unequal parts, and cut off from the greater part the amount by which it exceeds the half, and added that amount to the lesser.

When the whole has been divided evenly, the parties say they "have their own" — each has now received an equal, fair amount.

And the equal or fair amount here is the arithmetic mean between the more, or too much, and the less, or too little. That, by the way, is why it is called dikaion (just): because the division is in half — dicha — as if the word were dichaion; and the dikastes (judge) is a dichastes, a halver.

For if you cut a part off one of two equal lines and add it to the other, the second line now exceeds the first by two such parts. (Had you only cut the part off the first without adding it to the second, the second would exceed it by just one part.) The second exceeds the mean by one part, and the mean exceeds the shortened first line by one part.

This tells us how much to take from the man who has more, or too much, and how much to add to the man who has less, or too little: to the latter's portion we must add the amount by which it falls short of the mean, and from the former's portion we must take away the amount by which it exceeds the mean.

To draw the picture: let AA′, BB′, and CC′ be three equal lines. From AA′ cut off the segment AE; and to CC′ add the segment D, equal to AE. Then the whole line DCC′ exceeds EA′ by CD plus CZ (each equal to AE, or CD), and it exceeds BB′ by CD.

And this holds not only in geometry but in the arts as well; they could not exist unless the material being worked on received an impression matching, in kind and quantity and quality, the work the artist puts in.

These terms "loss" and "gain," by the way, are borrowed from voluntary exchange. In voluntary exchange, ending up with more than your own is called gaining, and ending up with less than you started with is called losing — in buying and selling, I mean, and in the other transactions where the law gives free play. And when the result for each party is neither more nor less but exactly the amount he started with, then they say they "have their own," and are neither losers nor gainers. The just, then, is a mean between a gain and a loss, neither of which was intended, and consists in having after the transaction the equivalent of what you had before it.

Chapter 5

Some people go further and think that justice is simply reciprocity — that whatever a man has done to another should be done to him in return. That was the teaching of the Pythagoreans; their whole definition of the just came down to this: what you did, you suffer.

But simple reciprocity does not fit either distributive justice or corrective justice — though people try to read it into the rule of Rhadamanthus: "To suffer what you have done is justice." In many cases the two come apart. If a man holding public office strikes someone, he ought not to be struck back; but if someone strikes an officer, he ought not merely to be struck in return — he ought to be punished on top of it.

Further, it makes a great difference whether what was done to the other man was done with his consent or against it.

And yet, in the exchange of services, reciprocity of a kind is exactly the rule of justice that holds society together — not simple reciprocity, though, but proportionate reciprocity: not equal repaid with equal, but repayment in proportion. The very existence of a state depends on proportionate return. When men have been done evil, they look to return it — if a man cannot repay an injury, we count his condition slavish. And when men have been done good, they look to repay that too; for without repayment there is no exchange of services, and it is the exchange of services that binds us together in society.

This is why we set up a temple of the Graces — the spirits of grace and gratitude — where everyone can see it: to remind people to repay what they have received. For that is the special mark of grace: to return the kindness of those who have been kind to us, and then to take the lead in kindness ourselves.

Proportionate exchange is produced by what we may call cross-conjunction — a crosswise pairing.

For instance: let A stand for a builder, B for a shoemaker, C for a house, and D for shoes.

The builder must take some of the shoemaker's work, and give the shoemaker his own work in exchange.

Now, the result we want will follow if the exchange takes place after proportionate equality has first been established between the goods.

If it is not, there is no equality, and the dealing collapses; for there is no reason why the work of the one should not be worth more than the work of the other. Their products, then, must first be brought to an equality — appraised by some common standard of value.

And this holds for every other art and profession just as much as for building and shoemaking. None of them could survive if what the customer receives did not correspond, in quantity and in quality, to what the producer supplies.

For exchange does not happen between two physicians. It happens between a physician and a farmer — in general, between people of different professions whose work is of unequal worth. These unequal parties, then, have to be reduced to equality, measured by a common standard.

So everything to be exchanged — every good, every service — must somehow be reducible to a common measure.

This is what money was invented for. It serves as a medium of exchange; with it we can measure everything, and so we can measure how much one kind of work is worth against another — how many shoes, for instance, are equivalent to a house, or to a given quantity of food.

What we need, then, is for a certain number of shoes to stand to a house (or to a measure of grain) in the same ratio that a builder (or a farmer) stands to a shoemaker. Unless this adjustment is made, no dealing or exchange of services can happen at all; and it cannot be made unless the goods can somehow be made equal.

We need, in short, one common measure of value, as we said before.

And in truth that measure is demand — our need of one another's services — which is what holds the members of a society together in the first place. If men had no needs, or no needs in common, there would either be no exchange, or a very different sort of exchange from the one we know.

But money has been introduced by convention as a sort of representative of demand. That is why the Greek word for it is nomisma: its value comes not from nature but from law — nomos — and it lies with us to change it or to withdraw it from use.

Exchange, then, will take place once the wares have been equated — once prices have adjusted them — so that the quantity of the shoemaker's work stands to the quantity of the farmer's work in the same ratio that farmer stands to shoemaker. But this adjustment must be made not at the moment of exchange (for then one of the two parties would pocket both advantages), but while each still holds his own wares. Do it then, and the two are put on an equal footing and can deal with each other, because this kind of equality can be established between them.

If A stands for a farmer and C for a certain quantity of his work (or his grain), then B will stand for a shoemaker and D for the quantity of shoemaker's work that is valued as equal to C.

If people could not reciprocate in this way, there would be no exchange of services at all.

That it is need which forms, as it were, the common bond holding society together is plain from this: people do not exchange unless they need one another's services — both parties, or at least one of them. That is why, when one people has something others need — wine, say — the others offer to export grain in return; and this grain must then be made equal in value to the wine.

But even if we happen to need nothing at the moment, money is a sort of guarantee that we will be able to make an exchange at any future time when we do need something; for whoever brings money must always be able to take goods away in exchange.

Money, it is true, is subject to the same conditions as everything else: its value does not always stay the same. Still, it tends to be more constant than the value of anything else.

Everything, then, must be assessed in money; for this is what lets men exchange their services at all times, and so makes society possible.

Money, as a standard, reduces things to a common measure so that equal amounts of each can be taken. For there would be no society without exchange, no exchange without equality, and no equality without a common measure.

Strictly speaking, of course, no truly common measure can be found for things so wildly different. But our needs supply a standard that is accurate enough for practical purposes.

There must, then, be some one common unit, and it must be fixed by convention — which is why we call it money, nomisma, from nomos. Money makes all things commensurable, for everything is valued in money. For instance: let A stand for a house, B for ten minae (a sum of money), and C for a bed. Let A equal half of B — taking a house to be worth five minae — and let C, the bed, equal one tenth of B. Then we see at once how many beds equal one house: five.

Clearly this is how all exchange must have worked before money came into use; for it makes no difference whether you give five beds for a house, or the price of five beds.

So much for what is unjust and what is just. Now that these are settled, we can see that just conduct is a mean between doing injustice and suffering it: the one is having too much, the other having too little, of one's due.

We can see, too, that the virtue justice is a kind of observance of the mean — but not in quite the same way as the virtues discussed so far. It does aim at a mean; but here both extremes fall under a single vice, injustice.

Justice, then, is the habit in virtue of which the just man is said to be disposed to do deliberately what is just: that is, in dealings between himself and another (or between two other parties), to apportion things so that he does not grab more of what is desirable for himself and leave his neighbor less — and the reverse with what is burdensome — but so that each gets his fair share, meaning his proportionate share; and likewise when he is judging between two other parties.

Injustice, on the contrary, is the character that chooses what is unjust — a disproportionate amount: too much or too little of what is advantageous or disadvantageous, as the case may be.

That is why injustice is both an excess and a deficiency: it chooses both. In a man's own affairs, it chooses an excess of what is (as a general rule) advantageous and a deficiency of what is disadvantageous; in the affairs of others, it makes a similarly disproportionate assignment, though which way the proportion gets violated will depend on circumstances.

And of the two sides of an unjust deed, to suffer the injustice is a lesser evil than to do it.

Let this stand, then, as our account, in general terms, of the nature of justice and injustice, and of what is just and what is unjust.

Chapter 6

But since a man can do an unjust act without yet being an unjust man, we may ask: are there acts of injustice such that a single one stamps the doer at once as unjust in this or that way — as a thief, say, or an adulterer, or a robber?

Perhaps the answer is that the difference does not lie in the acts at all. A man might commit adultery knowing perfectly well what he was doing, and yet act not from deliberate choice but from a moment's passion. In that case he acts unjustly, but he is not an unjust man — not a thief, though he committed a theft; not an adulterer, though he committed adultery; and so on down the line.

We have already explained how reciprocity is related to what is just. But we must not lose sight of what we are actually looking for: justice in the simple, unqualified sense — and that is political justice, justice between citizens. This kind of justice exists among men who share a common life in order to supply their needs, men who are free and on a footing of equality, whether absolute equality or proportionate.

Where men are not on this footing, there is no political justice between them — only something that can be called justice by a stretch, metaphorically. For the term "just" strictly applies only where men have law to appeal to; and the existence of law implies the existence of injustice, since the administration of law is precisely the sorting of the just from the unjust.

And where there is injustice, there is unjust dealing (though not every unjust act implies an unjust man) — that is, taking too much of the goods of life and too little of its burdens. This is why we do not allow an individual man to rule over us, but rather reason — that is, law; for an individual is prone to take more for himself and turn tyrant.

The magistrate's job is to guard what is just, and if what is just, then what is equal and fair. And if he is a just man, it seems he gets no profit out of his office — he does not take a larger share of the good things of life, except where a larger share is proportionate to his worth. He works, in other words, for the benefit of others; which is why justice is sometimes called "another's good," as we remarked before. So some salary must be given him, and this he receives in the form of honor and privilege. It is when magistrates are not content with these that they make themselves tyrants.

What is just between master and slave, or between father and child, is not the same as political justice, though it resembles it. There can be no injustice, strictly speaking, toward what is part of oneself — and a man's property, and his children until they reach a certain age and set up for themselves, are as it were a part of him. No one deliberately chooses to injure himself; and so a man cannot be unjust toward himself.

Here, then, there is no political justice or injustice. That, we found, is a matter of law, and it exists between people whose situation implies law — people who share equally, or fairly, in ruling and being ruled.

The term "just," therefore, applies more properly to a man's relations with his wife than to his relations with his children and his property. This is household justice; but even it is not the same as justice between citizens.

Chapter 7

Now, political justice is partly natural and partly conventional. Natural justice is what has the same force everywhere and does not depend on our accepting or rejecting it. Conventional justice is what could originally have been settled one way or another indifferently, but once settled is indifferent no longer — for instance, that a prisoner's ransom be one mina, or that a sacrifice be a goat rather than two sheep; and, again, all the rules made for particular occasions, like the sacrifice decreed in honor of Brasidas at Amphipolis, and everything that has the character of a decree.

Now, some people think all justice is conventional, on this reasoning: what is natural never varies and has the same force everywhere — fire burns both here and in Persia — while what is just plainly does vary.

But this is not quite right, though there is something in it. Among the gods, perhaps, it is not true at all; but among us, part of what is just is natural, even though all of it is subject to change. All of it can change — and yet, I repeat, part is natural and part is not.

Nor is it hard to tell, among things that can be otherwise than they are, which are natural and which rest instead on law and convention — even though both kinds vary. The same distinction holds in other fields. The right hand, we say, is naturally the stronger; yet in any given man the left may become just as strong.

So with justice: the conventional part, the part prescribed with a view to some particular purpose, varies the way measures vary. Measures for wine and grain are not the same everywhere — they run larger where dealers buy and smaller where they sell. Likewise, what is just not by nature but by human ordinance is not the same everywhere, any more than constitutions are the same everywhere — though there is only one constitution that is by nature the best everywhere.

Each of the terms "just" and "lawful," in its several senses, stands for a universal that covers many particulars: the acts are many, but the notion is one, applying to them all alike.

Notice, too, that "what is unjust" is different from "an act of injustice," and "what is just" from "an act of justice." A thing is unjust by nature or by ordinance; but this same thing, once done, is called an act of injustice — before it was done, it was merely unjust. The same holds for an act of justice; though here Greek usage prefers dikaiopragema as the general term for a just act, and reserves dikaioma for the correcting of an act of injustice. But the several species of just and unjust acts — their nature, their number, the ground they cover — we must postpone for the present.

Chapter 8

Now that we know what is just and what is unjust, we can say this: a man acts unjustly or justly when he does these things voluntarily. When he does them involuntarily, he acts neither unjustly nor justly, strictly speaking, but only "accidentally" — he does something that happens to be just or unjust. For whether a deed counts as an act of injustice (or of justice) depends on whether it is voluntary or involuntary. If it is voluntary, the doer is blamed, and only then does the deed become an act of injustice. Something unjust may be done, then, without there being any act of injustice at all — if the voluntariness is missing.

By a voluntary act I mean, as I explained before, anything within the doer's control that is done knowingly — knowing the person, the instrument, and the result: whom he is striking, what he is striking with, and what the blow will do — with no accident and no compulsion intervening at any point. If someone else seizes your hand and strikes a third person with it, that is no voluntary act of yours: it was not in your control. Again, the man you strike may be your father, while you know only that he is a man, or one of the company, but not that he is your father. The same distinction applies to the result, and, in a word, to the whole act. What is done in ignorance, then — or done not in ignorance but outside our control, or under compulsion — is involuntary. (There are also many natural processes, like growing old and dying, in which we knowingly take part, actively or passively, and which are neither voluntary nor involuntary.)

An accidentally unjust act is just as possible as an accidentally just one. A man might return a deposit unwillingly, out of fear of the consequences: we cannot say he did what is just, or acted justly, except accidentally. And likewise a man who is forcibly prevented, against his will, from returning a deposit acts unjustly, or does what is unjust, only accidentally.

Voluntary acts divide in turn into those done from choice and those done without choice — that is, those done with previous deliberation and those done without it.

Now, there are three ways in which we may hurt our neighbor.

First, a hurt done in ignorance is generally called a mistake, when there is a misconception about the person affected, or the thing done, or the instrument, or the result: I did not think I would hit at all, or not with this instrument, or not this person, or not with this effect — but something followed other than what I had in mind. I meant a prick, not a wound; or I did not mean to wound this man; or not to deal a wound of this kind.

But we can draw the line more precisely. When the hurt comes about contrary to all reasonable expectation, it is a misadventure. When it is not contrary to reasonable expectation but still involves no vicious intent, it is a mistake. For a man makes a mistake when the origin of the trouble lies in himself — he set the train of events in motion; he meets with misadventure when it comes from outside.

Second, when the doer acts knowingly but without previous deliberation, it is an act of injustice — as when a man is driven by anger, or by any of the other passions that human beings are naturally and unavoidably subject to. In doing such hurts and committing such wrongs, the doer acts unjustly, and the acts are acts of injustice; but they do not stamp him as an unjust or wicked man, for the hurt was not done out of wickedness.

But third, when the hurt is done from choice, the doer is unjust and wicked.

This is why acts done in anger are rightly judged not to be done with malice aforethought: it was the man who gave the provocation who started it, not the man who struck in a passion.

In cases of this last kind, moreover, what people dispute is usually not whether the deed was done, but where the justice of the case lies; for it is the appearance of injustice that stirs the striker's anger. This is different from disputes about contracts. There the question is one of fact, and one of the two parties must be a vicious character — unless his memory is simply at fault. But here they agree about the facts and differ about which side is in the right (whereas the deliberate aggressor knows perfectly well where the right lies): the one thinks he has been wronged; the other thinks not.

But if a man hurts another from choice, he acts unjustly; and acts of injustice of this kind — violations of what is proportionate and fair — stamp the doer as an unjust character.

In the same way, a man is a just character when he acts justly from choice; but he is said merely to act justly whenever he voluntarily does what is just.

Of involuntary injuries, finally, some are pardonable and some are not. Wrongs committed not only in ignorance but because of ignorance are pardonable. Wrongs committed in ignorance, but not because of it — because of some passion neither natural nor human — are not.

Chapter 9

But we may still wonder whether we have really said enough about what it is to do injustice and to suffer it. Start with those strange lines of Euripides:

  A: I killed my mother — that is the whole of my story.
  B: But tell me: did both of you will the deed, or neither?

The question they raise is this: is it actually possible to suffer injustice — to be wronged — voluntarily? Or is being wronged always involuntary, in the way that doing wrong is always voluntary?

And is it always one or the other? Doing injustice is always voluntary; is suffering it always involuntary, or is it sometimes voluntary and sometimes not? The same question comes up on the positive side, with having justice done to you. Doing justice is always voluntary, just as doing injustice is; so you might expect the active and the passive to match up in both cases — that being wronged and having justice done to you would each be always voluntary or always involuntary. But it would surely be absurd to claim that having justice done to you is always voluntary; some people have justice done to them and certainly do not will it.

We can also put the question in a more general form: whenever a man has something unjust done to him, is he wronged? Or does suffering injustice, like doing it, require some further conditions?

Both what I do and what I have done to me can be just "accidentally," as we saw; and the same goes for unjust. Doing something unjust is not the same as doing injustice, and suffering something unjust is not the same as being wronged — and likewise for doing justice and having justice done to you. For to be wronged, there must be someone doing the wrong; to have justice done to you, someone doing the justice.

Now, if doing injustice means simply hurting a man voluntarily, and "voluntarily" means with knowledge of the person, the instrument, and the manner, then the weak-willed man, who voluntarily hurts himself, would voluntarily suffer injustice — and it would be possible for a man to wrong himself, which is itself one of the questions in dispute.

Again, a man might, through weakness of will, voluntarily let himself be hurt by another man acting voluntarily; so here too a man would be voluntarily wronged.

I think, rather, that the definition is faulty. To "hurting with knowledge of the person, the instrument, and the manner" we must add: "against his wish." On that definition, a man can voluntarily be hurt and suffer what is unjust, but no one can voluntarily be wronged. For no one wishes to be hurt — not even the weak-willed man, who acts contrary to his own wish. No one wishes for what he does not think good, and what the weak-willed man does is not what he thinks he ought to do. And the man who gives away what is his, as Glaucus in Homer gives to Diomede —

  gold armor for bronze, a hundred oxen's worth for nine —

is not wronged; for the giving rests with him, but being wronged does not rest with oneself: there has to be someone doing the wrong.

Clearly, then, suffering injustice cannot be voluntary.

Two questions remain of those we set out to discuss: (1) when an unfairly large share is assigned, who does the injustice — the man who assigns it or the man who receives it? and (2) is it possible to do injustice to yourself?

Take the first. If it is the assigner and not the receiver who does the injustice, then a man who knowingly and voluntarily gives another too much and himself too little does injustice to himself. And this is exactly what moderate people are often thought to do; the equitable man is inclined to take less than his due. But the case is not so simple. Perhaps he took a larger share of some other good — of reputation, say, or of the noble itself.

Besides, the difficulty dissolves under our definition of doing injustice: nothing is done to this man against his wish, so no injustice is done to him — at most, harm.

It is also plain that it is the man who makes the unjust award who does injustice, and not always the man who gets more than his share. A man does not do injustice whenever what he does can be called unjust, but only when he voluntarily does what is unjust — and that can be said only of the one in whom the action originates, which here is the distributor, not the receiver.

Again, "do" has many senses. In a certain sense an inanimate tool "kills"; so does my hand; so does my slave, acting under my orders. These can be said to do what is unjust, but not to act unjustly or to commit an act of injustice.

Again, if a judge gives an unjust judgment unknowingly, he does not commit injustice in the sense of violating what is legally just, and his judgment is not unjust in that sense — though in a certain sense it is unjust; for what is just according to law and what is just in the primary sense are different things. But if he gives unjust judgment knowingly, then he himself is grasping at more than his share — a share of favor with one party, or of revenge on the other. A judge who gives unjust judgment on those grounds takes more than his due exactly as if he split the unjust award itself; even in that case, a judge who awards a piece of land takes his cut not in land but in money.

Men imagine that since acting unjustly is in their power, being just must be easy. It is not. To sleep with your neighbor's wife, to strike your neighbor, to pass certain coins from your hand to his — all easy, and always in your power. But to do these acts as the expression of a certain character is not easy, and not always in your power.

In the same way men think that knowing what is just and what is unjust takes no great wisdom, since anyone can look up what the law prescribes (though what the law prescribes is only accidentally just, not essentially). But to know how such acts must be done, and how such distributions must be made, in order to be just — that is a harder matter than knowing what is good for health, and even that is no easy matter. It is easy enough to know what honey is, and wine, and hellebore, and cautery, and the knife; but to know how they must be applied, and to whom, and when, in order to produce health — that is so far from easy that to have this knowledge is to be a doctor.

For the same reason, some people think the just man is as capable of acting unjustly as justly — indeed more capable than another of performing the acts themselves, of sleeping with a woman or landing a blow — just as the courageous man is more capable than another of throwing away his shield, turning his back, and running for it. But playing the coward and acting unjustly do not consist merely in doing such acts (though the doer might be said to act unjustly "accidentally"); they consist in doing them in a certain frame of mind — just as practicing medicine and healing do not consist simply in cutting or not cutting, in giving a drug or withholding it, but in doing so in a particular fashion.

Lastly, justice presupposes persons who share in the things that are, generally speaking, good, but who can have too much of them or too little. For some beings — the gods, perhaps — no amount of such things is too much; for others — the incurably vicious — no amount does any good, everything hurts them; but for the rest of us they are useful within limits. Justice, therefore, is an essentially human affair.

Chapter 10

Next we must speak of equity and the equitable, and ask how equity is related to justice and the equitable to the just. On inspection, they seem neither simply identical nor different in kind. Sometimes we praise what is equitable and the equitable man — we even borrow the word as a general term of praise meaning "good," as if the more equitable a thing is, the better it is. At other times we stop and find it strange that the equitable should deserve praise if it is something other than the just. For if they differ, then either the just is not good or the equitable is not; and if both are good, they are the same thing.

These are the reflections that create the difficulty about equity. And in a way they are all correct, and not really in conflict. For the equitable, though better than one kind of just, is itself just; it is not better than the just as if it were something different in kind. The just and the equitable, then, are the same in kind, and both are good, though the equitable is the better.

What obscures the matter is this: the equitable is just, but it is not the legally just — it is a correction of it.

The reason is that every law speaks in general terms, while there are some matters about which it is impossible to speak correctly in general terms. Where one must speak generally but cannot do so correctly, the law takes the case that holds for the most part — fully aware that it does not hold for every case.

And the law is no less correct for that. The defect lies not in the law, nor in the lawgiver, but in the nature of the subject matter; it is built into the very conditions of human action.

So when the law lays down a general rule and a case then arises that the rule does not fit, it is right — where the legislator has fallen short and erred by speaking without qualification — to correct the omission: to say what the lawgiver himself would say if he were standing there, and what he would have written into the law if he had known of the case.

The equitable, then, is just, and better than the just in one sense of the word — not better than absolute justice, but better than the error that comes of stating things without qualification. And this is the very essence of the equitable: it is an amendment of the law where the law fails through the generality of its language.

The reason the law cannot cover every case is that some matters simply cannot be caught in a law, and so require a special decree. For what is variable needs a variable standard — like the leaden rule used in Lesbian masonry. That rule has no fixed shape of its own; it bends to fit the contour of each stone. Just so, the decree bends to fit the case.

We have found, then, what the equitable is: it is just, and it is better than what is just in a certain sense of the word. And from this it is easy to see what the equitable man is. He is the man disposed to choose such a course and follow it — who does not press his rights to the last penny at other people's expense, but is ready to take less than his due even with the law on his side. This is the equitable man, and the character is equity: a kind of justice, not some different character altogether.

Chapter 11

The foregoing discussion lets us answer the question whether it is possible for a man to act unjustly toward himself.

Justice in one sense of the word, we found, covers those exercises of the several virtues which the law prescribes. For example, the law does not order a man to kill himself, and what the law does not order it forbids. Further, when a man voluntarily and without provocation hurts another contrary to law — "voluntarily" meaning with knowledge of the person and the instrument — he acts unjustly. Now, the man who kills himself in a fit of rage does this voluntarily, against right reason, and does what the law forbids; therefore he acts unjustly.

But unjustly toward whom? Toward the state, surely, not toward himself: he suffers voluntarily, and no one can be wronged voluntarily. And this is in fact the ground on which the state punishes it — a particular loss of civic standing is pronounced upon the man who destroys himself, as one who has acted unjustly toward the state.

Again, take "unjust" in the other sense, in which it names not badness in general but one particular form of vice. In this sense too it is impossible to act unjustly toward yourself. (This sense, we found, differs from the first: the unjust man here is bad in the way the coward is bad — as having one specific vice, not a wholly vicious character; and in calling his act unjust we are not charging him with complete badness.) For if a man could wrong himself, the same thing could be taken from and given to the same person at the same time — which is impossible. In fact, the just and the unjust always require more than one person.

Further, an act of injustice must be voluntary and deliberate, and it must come first — the man who merely pays back the hurt he received is not held to act unjustly. But the man who hurts himself inflicts and suffers the very same hurt at the very same moment.

Again, if a man could wrong himself, it would be possible to be wronged voluntarily.

Further, no one acts unjustly without committing some particular act of injustice; and no man commits adultery with his own wife, or breaks into his own house, or steals his own property.

But the whole question of wronging oneself is settled, without going into detail, by our answer to the question whether a man can be wronged voluntarily.

(It is plain that suffering injustice and doing it are both bad — the one is having less, the other having more, than the mean amount, which plays the role here that health plays in medicine or good condition in athletic training. Still, doing injustice is the worse of the two. Doing injustice is blamable and implies vice — either complete, fully formed vice, what we call vice without qualification, or something well on the way to it; for a voluntary act of injustice does not always imply an unjust character. But having injustice done to you implies no vice and no injustice in you at all.

In itself, then, being wronged is the lesser evil, though accidentally it can be the greater. Science does not bother with such accidents: it calls pleurisy a worse ailment than a stumble, even though the stumble might on occasion turn out worse — if, say, stumbling made you fall and the enemy caught or killed you.)

Though we cannot speak strictly of justice between a man and himself, we can speak of it metaphorically, in virtue of a certain resemblance — justice not between a man and himself but between certain parts of him. Not justice in every sense, though: only the kind that holds between master and slave, or husband and wife. For that is the sort of relation in which the rational part of the soul stands to the irrational part.

And it is just this division into parts that makes people fancy there is such a thing as injustice toward oneself: one part of a man can have something done to it by another part against its desires, and so people suppose there is a kind of justice between these parts, as between ruler and ruled.

Let this, then, conclude our examination of justice and the other moral virtues.

Book VI

The Intellectual Virtues

Chapter 1

We said earlier that what we should choose is neither too much nor too little but "the mean," and that the mean is what "right reason" prescribes. Now we have to explain what that means.

Every virtue we have discussed implies — as every habit of mind implies — some target that the rational man keeps his eye on as he tightens or slackens his effort. In other words, there must be some standard that fixes the various forms of moderation we have been describing, the states that we say lie between excess and deficiency and accord with right reason. All of this is quite true — but it is not precise enough. In any occupation that can be reduced to rational principles, it is perfectly true to say that we must strain ourselves neither too much nor too little, but "in moderation," "as right reason orders." Yet by itself that tells you almost nothing. A man would hardly learn how to treat a patient by being told to treat him as the art of medicine prescribes, and as someone versed in that art would treat him.

So too with the habits and characters of the soul: it is not enough that the rule we have laid down is correct. We need to know precisely what this right reason is, and what standard it gives us.

The virtues of the soul, you will remember, we divided into two classes, calling one moral and the other intellectual. The moral virtues we have already discussed in detail; let us now examine the other class, the intellectual virtues — after a few preliminary remarks about the soul.

We said before that the soul consists of two parts, the rational and the irrational. Let us now divide the rational part in the same way, and assume that it contains two rational faculties: one by which we know the things that rest on invariable principles — things that cannot be otherwise — and one by which we know the things that vary. For generically different objects must answer to generically different faculties, if, as we hold, our faculties are able to know their objects through some kind of likeness or kinship with them.

Call the first the scientific or demonstrative faculty, and the second the calculative or deliberative faculty; for deliberating and calculating are the same thing, and no one deliberates about what cannot be otherwise. So one division of the rational faculty may fairly be called the calculative.

Our problem, then, is to find what each of these faculties is in its full development, its best state; for that will be its virtue.

And its virtue will refer directly to its proper function.

Chapter 2

Now, three things in us guide action and the grasp of truth: sense, reason, and desire.

The first of these originates no action. We can see that from the fact that the animals have sense but take no part in action.

Take the other two, and you find two modes of reasoning — affirmation and denial, assent and dissent — and two corresponding modes of desire — pursuit and avoidance, attraction and repulsion.

Now, moral virtue is a habit of choice, and choice is deliberate desire — desire that follows on deliberation.

It follows that if choice is to be everything it should be, the reasoning must be true and the desire right, and the very same things the reasoning affirms, the desire must pursue.

This, then, is the kind of reasoning — and the kind of truth — that has to do with action.

Speculative reasoning, which has to do with neither action nor production, is good or bad simply as it is true or false; for the function of the intellect as such is always the grasp of truth. But the function of the practical intellect is truth in agreement with right desire.

Choice, then, is the cause of action — not the final cause, the end it aims at, but the efficient cause, the source that sets it going — and the source of choice, in turn, is desire together with reasoning about means. So choice necessarily involves, on one side, the faculty of reason in exercise, and on the other a certain moral character, a certain state of the desires; for right action and its contrary are alike impossible without both reasoning and character.

Reasoning by itself, however, sets nothing in motion. Only reasoning that aims at an end and issues in action does that — what we may call practical reasoning. (Practical reasoning governs production too; for whenever you make something, you make it with a further object in view. The thing you make is not desired as an end in itself, but only as a means to, or condition of, something else. But the thing you do is an end in itself, for doing well — right action — is the end, and that is what desire aims at.)

Choice, then, may be called either reason that desires, or desire that reasons; and this power of originating action is what a man essentially is.
