No past event can be chosen: no one chooses to have sacked Troy. We deliberate not about what is past but about what is to come and can be otherwise; the past cannot be undone. So Agathon is right when he says:

  One power even God must go without —
  to make undone what has once been done.

We have found, then, that both divisions of the reason — both intellectual faculties — have the attainment of truth as their function; and the developed state in which each best attains truth will be its virtue.

Chapter 3

Let us describe these virtues, then, starting fresh from the beginning.

Assume that the ways in which the mind arrives at truth, by affirming or denying, are five in number: art, science, practical wisdom, wisdom, and intuitive reason. Conception and opinion are left off the list, because they can be mistaken.

What science is we can learn from the following considerations (we want a precise account here, and must not settle for metaphors). We all suppose that what we know scientifically cannot be otherwise; whereas of what can be otherwise, we cannot say, the moment it is out of sight, whether it still exists or not. The object of science, then, is necessary — and therefore eternal, for whatever is necessary by its own nature is eternal, and the eternal neither comes to be nor passes away.

Further, all science is held to be teachable, and what can be known scientifically can be learned. But all teaching starts from something already known, as we explained in the Analytics; for it proceeds either by induction or by syllogism. Induction leads the learner up to universal principles; syllogism starts from them. So there are principles from which syllogism starts that syllogism itself cannot reach — and which must therefore be reached by induction.

Science, then, may be defined as a habit of demonstration — with all the further qualifications listed in the Analytics. We must add those, because a man properly has scientific knowledge only when the principles behind his knowledge are accepted and known to him in a particular way; unless those principles are better known to him than the conclusions drawn from them, his knowledge is merely accidental.

Let that stand, then, as our account of science.

Chapter 4

The variable — what can be otherwise — includes both what man makes and what man does; and making, or production, is different from doing, or action (here we follow the popular distinction). So the reasoned habit of acting is different from the reasoned habit of producing. Neither includes the other; for action is not production, and production is not action.

Now, the builder's skill is one of the arts, and can be described as a certain reasoned habit of producing; there is no art that is not a habit of this kind, and no habit of this kind that is not an art. An art, then, is the same thing as a habit of producing with correct reasoning.

And every art is concerned with bringing something into being — with contriving and calculating how to bring into being some thing that can either be or not be, and whose cause lies in the producer, not in the thing produced. For art has nothing to do with what is or comes to be by necessity, nor with the products of nature; those carry the cause of their coming-to-be within themselves.

Since production and action are different, art of course belongs with production, not with action. And in a certain sense art and chance work the same ground, as Agathon says:

  Art waits on fortune, fortune waits on art.

Art, then, as we said, is a habit of production with correct reasoning; and its contrary — lack of art — is a habit of production with incorrect reasoning; and the field of both is the variable.

Chapter 5

To find out what practical wisdom is, let us first ask who the people are that we call practically wise.

The mark of a man of practical wisdom seems to be that he can deliberate well about what is good and useful for himself — not in some particular department, such as what makes for health or strength, but about well-being, about living well as a whole.

Our usage confirms this: we apply the name to people who deliberate well in some particular field when they calculate well the means to some particular good end in matters that no art covers. So we may say generally that the man who can deliberate well is practically wise.

But no one deliberates about what cannot be altered, or about what is not in his power to do.

Now science, as we saw, involves demonstration; but things whose principles or causes are variable do not admit of demonstration — for everything that depends on such principles is variable too — and things that are fixed by necessity admit of no deliberation. It follows that practical wisdom can be neither a science nor an art: not a science, because the sphere of action is what can be altered; not an art, because production and action are generically different.

What remains is this: practical wisdom is a reasoned and true habit of mind that issues in action, in the field of human good and evil. For production has an end beyond itself, but action does not — good action, doing well, is itself the end.

This is why Pericles and men like him are considered practically wise: they are able to see what is good for themselves and for men in general; and that, we take it, is the character of those who can manage a household or a state.

This, too, is why the Greeks gave self-control its name, sophrosyne — the word suggests "that which preserves practical wisdom." And what self-control preserves is precisely this kind of judgment. For pleasure and pain do not destroy or distort every judgment — not, for instance, the judgment that the angles of a triangle are equal to two right angles — but only judgments about matters of practice. The principles of action are the ends for whose sake we act; and the man corrupted by pleasure or pain straightaway loses sight of the principle: he no longer sees that this is the end for whose sake, and as a means to which, each particular act should be chosen and done. For vice is a destroyer of principles.

Our conclusion, then: practical wisdom is a reasoned and true habit that issues in action, in the field of human good.

Moreover, art has its excellence — its perfect development — in something beyond itself; not so practical wisdom. Again, in art the man who errs on purpose is better than the man who errs against his will; in practical wisdom the deliberate error is worse, as it is in all the virtues. Plainly, then, practical wisdom is itself a virtue, and not an art.

And since the rational parts of the soul are two, practical wisdom will be the virtue of the second — the calculative part, the faculty of opinion; for opinion deals with what can be otherwise, and so does practical wisdom.

Yet it is something more than merely "a reasoned habit of grasping truth." We can see that from this: a merely reasoned habit can be forgotten and lost, but practical wisdom, once acquired, is never lost.

Chapter 6

Science is a mode of judgment that deals with universal and necessary truths; and demonstrable truths depend on principles — since science proceeds by demonstration, every science has its principles. The principles on which scientific truths depend, then, cannot be the business of science itself, nor of art, nor of practical wisdom: for what is known scientifically can be demonstrated, while art and practical wisdom deal with what can be otherwise.

Nor are these principles the business of wisdom; for it is characteristic of the wise man to have demonstrative knowledge of certain things — and demonstration is not how principles are reached.

Now, the habits of mind by which we grasp truth without any admixture of error, whether about things invariable or things variable, are science, practical wisdom, wisdom, and intuitive reason. If none of the first three — practical wisdom, science, wisdom — can be the faculty that grasps first principles, only one conclusion is left: first principles are grasped by intuitive reason.

Chapter 7

The word "wisdom" is sometimes applied, in the domain of the arts, to the consummate masters of a craft: Phidias is called wise as a master of sculpture, Polyclitus for his skill in portrait statues; and in this use it means nothing more than excellence of art, the artistic faculty brought to perfection.

But there are also men we consider wise not in one department, not in any particular thing — as Homer says in the Margites:

  The gods gave him no skill with spade or plow,
  nor made him wise in anything at all —

but wise generally. In this general sense, then, wisdom will plainly be the most perfect of the sciences.

The wise man, then, must not only know what follows from the first principles of knowledge; he must also know the truth about the principles themselves. Wisdom, therefore, will be intuitive reason joined with demonstrative scientific knowledge — scientific knowledge of the noblest objects with its crowning perfection, so to speak, added to it. For it would be absurd to suppose that statesmanship, or practical wisdom, is the highest of our faculties — unless man is the best thing in the universe.

Now, "wholesome" and "good" mean one thing for men and another for fish, while "white" and "straight" mean the same thing always. In the same way, we must all allow that "wise" means one thing always, while "practically wise" means different things for different beings; for we call practically wise whatever sees clearly to its own affairs, and we judge it fit to be trusted with them. (This is why we sometimes apply the word even to animals — the ones that show foresight about their own lives.)

It is plain, too, that wisdom cannot be the same as statesmanship. If we call knowledge of our own advantage "wisdom," there will be many wisdoms; for there is no single knowledge of the good of all animals — each species has its own — any more than there is a single art of healing for every living thing.

It makes no difference that man may be superior to all the other animals; for there are things far more divine in their nature than man — to take the most conspicuous instance, the heavenly bodies.

After all this it is plain that wisdom is scientific, demonstrative knowledge joined with intuitive reason, about objects of the noblest nature.

And this is why people call Anaxagoras and Thales and men of that sort wise, but not practically wise — seeing that they are ignorant of their own advantage — and say that they know things that are extraordinary, wonderful, difficult, and divine, but useless, because it is no human good that they seek.

Practical wisdom, on the other hand, deals with human affairs, with matters that admit of deliberation. For the special function of the man of practical wisdom, as we conceive him, is to deliberate well; and no one deliberates about what cannot be otherwise, or about matters with no end in view — no achievable good. And the man who deliberates well, without qualification, is the man who can arrive, by reasoning and calculation, at the best thing attainable by man in action.

Nor does practical wisdom deal only in general propositions. It must know particular facts as well; for it issues in action, and the field of action is the field of particulars.

This is why some men who lack the theory are more effective in practice than others who have it — above all, men of wide experience. If you know that light meat is digestible and wholesome, but do not know which meats are light, you will not cure anyone; the man who only knows that chicken is light and wholesome will do better.

But practical wisdom is concerned with practice; so it needs knowledge of both kinds — general truths and particular facts — but especially the particular.

Here too, however, there must be a supreme form of the faculty; and to it we now turn.

Chapter 8

Statesmanship and practical wisdom are in fact the same habit of mind, though they show themselves differently.

Of this faculty as applied to the state, the supreme form is the legislative; the form that deals with particular cases is called by the generic name, statesmanship. This latter is concerned with action and deliberation — for a decree bears directly on action, as the last link in the chain. And this is why only men engaged in this work are said to take part in politics: they alone act, the way craftsmen do.

But the faculty is most of all regarded as practical wisdom when it is applied to the individual, to one's own affairs; and it is this form that receives the generic name, practical wisdom — the others being household management, legislation, and statesmanship in the narrower sense, which divides in turn into the deliberative and the judicial.

Knowing one's own good, then, would seem to be one kind of knowledge — though a very various one. In the general opinion, the man who knows and minds his own affairs is practically wise, while statesmen are busybodies — as Euripides has it:

  Call that wise? I could have lived carefree,
  one man in the ranks among the many,
  an equal share with every other man ...
  but those who reach too high, who do too much —

For men generally seek their own good, and suppose that this is what they ought to do; and from this opinion comes the notion that such men are the practically wise ones.

And yet, perhaps, a man cannot manage his own affairs well without managing a household — and even taking part in the management of a state.

Moreover, how a man ought to manage his own affairs is not obvious; it needs looking into. Here is the evidence: a young man may become expert in geometry or mathematics, and wise in such matters, but nobody thinks a young man can have practical wisdom. The reason is that practical wisdom deals with particular facts, and particular facts become familiar only through experience — which a young man cannot have, for experience is the fruit of years.

We might ask, while we are here, why a boy can be a mathematician but not wise, and no expert in nature either. Surely the answer is that mathematics is an abstract science, while the principles of wisdom and of natural science are drawn only from long experience. A young man can repeat the propositions of the latter, but he does not really believe them; whereas the meanings of mathematical terms he grasps without trouble.

Again, error in deliberation may lie either in the universal judgment or in the particular one: you may be wrong in judging that all water that weighs heavy is unwholesome, or wrong in judging that this water weighs heavy. But practical wisdom, for all its universal judgments, is plainly not science; for, as we said, it deals with the ultimate particular fact — the last link in the chain — since that is what can actually be done.

And so it stands, in a way, opposite to intuitive reason as well. Intuitive reason grasps the primary principles, for which no demonstration can be given; practical wisdom grasps the ultimate particular, which cannot be scientifically proved but is perceived — not by one of the special senses, but by the kind of perception by which, in mathematics, we see that this particular figure before us is a triangle; for there too our reasoning must come to a stop. Still, this faculty of grasping particulars is really perception rather than practical wisdom, though it is perception of another kind.

Chapter 9

Inquiry and deliberation are not the same thing: deliberation is one particular kind of inquiry. But we need to pin down what good deliberation is — is it a kind of science, or opinion, or lucky guessing, or something else altogether?

It is not science; for we do not inquire into what we already know, and good deliberation is a kind of deliberation, and when we deliberate we are inquiring and calculating.

Nor is it lucky guessing. A lucky guess comes without calculation and in a flash, while deliberation takes time. Hence the common saying: be quick to carry out what you have decided, but slow to decide.

Shrewdness, again — quickness at hitting the mark — is not the same as good deliberation; shrewdness is a kind of lucky guessing.

Nor is good deliberation any sort of opinion.

But consider: when we deliberate badly we go wrong, and when we deliberate well we go right. So good deliberation is clearly some kind of correctness — but a correctness neither of science nor of opinion. Science does not admit of correctness, since it does not admit of error; and correctness of opinion is simply truth. Besides, whatever we hold an opinion about is already something settled.

Still, good deliberation is impossible without calculation.

The only option left, then, is that it is correctness of thinking; for thinking is not yet assertion. Opinion is not an inquiry but already a definite assertion, whereas a person deliberating — well or badly — is still inquiring and calculating.

But good deliberation is a certain correctness in deliberation; so we must first ask what deliberation is and what its field is.

Now, correctness comes in several kinds, and clearly not every kind of correctness in deliberating counts as deliberating well. A weak-willed man or a bad man may, by careful calculation, arrive at exactly the goal he has set himself; he will have deliberated correctly, though what he wins is a great evil. But to have deliberated well is thought to be a good thing; so it is only one particular kind of correctness in deliberation that is called good deliberation — the kind that arrives at what is good.

Further, one can reach what is good by a false syllogism: reach the right conclusion about what to do, but by the wrong route and on the wrong grounds, the middle term being false. So this too falls short of good deliberation — hitting the right conclusion, but not on the right grounds.

Again, one man may reach the right conclusion only slowly, another quickly. So we must add one more condition: good deliberation means coming to a right conclusion about what is useful and ought to be done, and coming to it in the right manner and at the right time.

Once more, we speak of deliberating well without qualification, and of deliberating well toward a particular kind of end. Good deliberation without qualification is what reaches right conclusions about the means to the end without qualification; a particular kind of good deliberation is what reaches right conclusions about the means to a particular kind of end. And so, since deliberating well is the mark of people of practical wisdom, good deliberation will be correctness in judging what is useful toward that end of which practical wisdom has a true conception.

Chapter 10

Understanding — the faculty we credit when we call someone understanding, or a person of good understanding — is not the same as science in general, nor as opinion (if it were, everyone would have it). Nor is it identical with any particular science, the way medicine deals with health or geometry with magnitudes. For understanding is not concerned with what is eternal and unchangeable, nor with events of every kind, but only with the things a person might puzzle over and deliberate about. Its field, then, is the same as that of practical wisdom; but the two are not identical. Practical wisdom issues orders — its business is what should be done or not done — while understanding only judges. (Understanding and good understanding come to the same thing here; so do a man of understanding and a man of good understanding.)

Understanding is neither the possession of practical wisdom nor its acquisition. Rather, just as a student is said to show understanding when he puts to use the science he hears from his teacher, so in the sphere of practical wisdom a man shows understanding when he uses the opinions he hears from others to judge — and to judge fittingly; for judging "well" here means judging fittingly.

And it is from this use of the word in learning that its use for the faculty comes — the faculty we mean when we call a man understanding; for we often speak of a learner's understanding.

Chapter 11

Judgment — what we mean when we speak of a considerate judge of others, or say that a man "has judgment" — is the correct discernment of what is equitable. Here is the sign: we think the equitable man above all shows consideration in his judgments, and we count it equitable to judge some things with consideration. And consideration — sympathetic judgment — is simply judgment that correctly discerns what is equitable; and correctly means truly.

Now all these formed faculties we have listed tend, naturally enough, toward the same point. We apply all of these terms — judgment, understanding, practical wisdom, intuitive reason — to the same people: we say that someone has, by a certain age, come to have judgment and reason, and is practically wise and understanding. For all four faculties deal with ultimate and particular facts; and it is by having discernment in the matters practical wisdom deals with that a person counts as understanding, or as a man of sound or considerate judgment — for the equitable is common ground in all our good dealings with others.

But what is to be done is always some particular thing, something ultimate. Knowing it, as we have seen, is the business of the man of practical wisdom; and understanding and judgment also concern things to be done — that is, ultimates.

And the intuitive reason — the last of the four — also deals with ultimates, in both senses of the word. Both first principles and ultimate particular facts are grasped by intuitive reason, not by demonstration. On one side, in demonstrations, reason grasps the unalterable first principles; on the other, in practical calculations, reason grasps the ultimate, alterable fact — the minor premise of the practical syllogism. These particular facts are our starting points for the end we aim at: the universal principle is drawn out of the particulars. Of these particulars, then, we must have a kind of perception — and this perception is intuitive reason.

That is why these faculties are thought to be natural gifts: nature never makes a man wise, but she does, we think, endow men with judgment, understanding, and reason. A sign of this is that we believe these powers keep pace with our time of life — that a certain age brings reason and judgment — as though nature were the cause.

(Intuitive reason, then, is both beginning and end; for demonstrations start from these ultimate truths and terminate in them.)

And on this account we ought to attend to the undemonstrated sayings and opinions of experienced and older people, and of people of practical wisdom, no less than to their demonstrations. Experience has given them an eye, and with it they see correctly.

We have now said, then, what practical wisdom is and what wisdom is, what each deals with, and that each is the virtue of a different part of the soul.

Chapter 12

But here an objection may be raised. "What is the use of them?" someone may ask. Wisdom does not study any of the things that make a man happy, for it does not ask how anything comes about. Practical wisdom does — but why do we need it? Practical wisdom deals with what is just and noble and good for man, the very things it is the good man's part to do; but knowing them makes us no readier to do them, if — as we have said — the virtues are habits. It is just as with things called "healthy" or "sound," meaning not what produces health but what flows from a healthy state: knowing medicine or gymnastics makes us no more able to do such things.

"But suppose we say a man should have practical wisdom not in order to do these acts but in order to become able to do them — then it is of no use to those who are already good, nor even to those who are not. For it will not matter whether they have it themselves or take the advice of others who do. It will be enough to do here as we do about health: we wish to be healthy, but we do not go off and learn medicine.

"Again, it seems strange that practical wisdom, though inferior to wisdom, should nevertheless govern it — since in every field the practical faculty holds sway and issues orders."

We must now take up these points; so far we have only been stating the difficulties.

First, then, we may say that both practical wisdom and wisdom must be desirable in themselves, since each is the virtue of one of the parts of the soul — even if neither of them produced anything at all.

Next, they do produce something.

On one hand, wisdom produces happiness — not in the sense in which medicine produces health, but in the sense in which health produces health. Wisdom is a part of complete virtue, and so possessing it and exercising it make a man happy.

On the other hand, in the sphere of action, a man performs his function perfectly when he acts in accordance with both practical wisdom and moral virtue: virtue makes the end we aim at right, and practical wisdom makes the means to it right.

(The fourth part of the soul, the vegetative part or faculty of nutrition, has no such virtue; for nothing is in its power to do or not to do.)

As for the objection that practical wisdom makes us no readier to do what is noble and just, let us go a little deeper, beginning here.

We allow, on one hand, that some who do just acts are not yet just — those, for instance, who do what the laws command unwillingly, or in ignorance, or for some outside motive and not for the sake of the acts themselves (though they do what they ought, and everything a good man should do). And on the other hand, it seems that when a man does the several acts in a certain condition, he is good — namely, when he does them from choice, and for the sake of the acts themselves.

Now, virtue makes the choice right; but deciding what should be done to carry out the choice belongs not to virtue but to another power. Let us dwell on this point a moment and make it quite clear.

There is a power we call cleverness: the power to carry out the steps toward any proposed goal and so achieve it. If the goal is noble, the power deserves praise; if the goal is base, it is the power of a villain. That is why we call both the practically wise man and the villain clever.

Practical wisdom is not this power, but it does not exist without it. And this eye of the soul, as we may call it, does not reach its full development without moral virtue — as we said before, and as can be shown. All syllogisms about what is to be done have as their starting point "the end — the supreme good — is such-and-such" (whatever it may be; any definition of the good will do for the argument). But it is only to the good man that this appears as the good; for vice perverts us and makes us go wrong about the starting points of action. So it is plain, as we said, that it is impossible to be practically wise without being morally good.

Chapter 13

This suggests a further look at moral virtue; for here the case is closely parallel. As practical wisdom stands to cleverness — not identical, but closely akin — so full virtue stands to natural virtue.

Everyone grants that in a certain sense the several types of character are given us by nature: justice, a tendency toward self-control, courage, and the rest show themselves from the moment of birth. Nevertheless we look for developed goodness as something different from this, and expect to find these same qualities in another form. Even children and animals have the natural dispositions, but without reason to guide them these are plainly harmful. This much, at least, seems clear: just as a strong-bodied creature that cannot see stumbles heavily when it moves, because it has no sight, so it is with natural virtue. But when it is lit by reason, it acts surpassingly well; and the natural virtue — which before only resembled virtue — will then be virtue fully developed.

We find, then, that just as the calculative part has two forms, cleverness and practical wisdom, so the moral qualities have two forms, natural virtue and full virtue — and full virtue is impossible without practical wisdom.

That is why some people say that all the virtues are forms of practical wisdom — why Socrates in particular held this view. His inquiry was partly right and partly wrong: wrong in thinking that all the virtues actually are forms of practical wisdom, right in saying that they are impossible without it.

Here is a confirmation: even now, everyone defining virtue will name its field and then add that it is a habit "in accordance with right reason" — and right means in accordance with practical wisdom. Everyone, it seems, has a sort of inkling that a settled character of this kind — one in accordance with practical wisdom — is virtue.

Only a slight change is needed in the expression. Virtue is not simply a habit in accordance with right reason, but a habit that implies right reason. And right reason in these matters is practical wisdom.

So where Socrates held that the virtues are forms of reason (for he held they are all modes of knowledge), we hold that they imply reason.

It is evident, then, from what has been said, that it is not possible to be good in the strict sense without practical wisdom, nor practically wise without moral virtue. And this lets us answer an objection that may be urged. "The virtues," someone may say, "are found apart from each other: a man strongly disposed by nature to one virtue is not equally drawn to all the others, so he will have acquired this one while he still lacks that one." We may answer that this can happen with the natural virtues, but not with the virtues that make a man good without qualification. For where the single virtue of practical wisdom is present, all the moral virtues are present with it.

And so it is plain, first, that even if it did not bear on practice, we would still need practical wisdom, because it is the virtue of a part of our nature; and second, that choice cannot be right without both practical wisdom and moral virtue — for virtue makes us desire the end, and practical wisdom makes us take the right means to it.

Nevertheless, practical wisdom is not the mistress of wisdom or of the better part of our nature, any more than medicine is the mistress of health. Practical wisdom does not employ wisdom in its service; it provides for wisdom's coming to be. It does not rule wisdom, but rules in wisdom's interest. To assert the contrary would be like asserting that statesmanship rules the gods, because it issues orders about everything in the city — including the worship of the gods.

Book VII

Characters Other Than Virtue and Vice

Chapter 1

Let us now make a fresh start and say this: the forms of moral character to avoid are three in number — vice, weakness of will, and brutishness. For two of these the opposites are obvious: the opposite of vice we call virtue, and the opposite of weakness of will we call self-restraint. But for the opposite of the brutish character, the best candidate is an excellence that is beyond us — the excellence of a hero or a god. So Homer has Priam say of Hector that he was surpassingly good:

  He seemed no child of mortal man,
  But of a god.

If, then, superlative excellence turns men into gods, as the stories tell us, it is evident that the opposite of the brutish character must be some excellence of that order. For just as a brute has neither virtue nor vice, neither does a god: what belongs to a god is something higher than virtue, and what belongs to a brute is something different in kind from vice.

Now, a godlike man is rare — to borrow the phrase the Spartans use; when they admire a man intensely, they call him seios aner, "a divine man" — and the brutish character is just as rare among human beings. It is found most often among the barbarians; sometimes disease or bodily injury produces it; and, thirdly, we use the name as a term of abuse for people who carry vice to an extreme pitch.

We shall have to say something about this disposition further on, and vice we have already discussed. So let us now speak of weakness of will, softness, and love of luxury, and also of self-restraint and endurance — for we must treat these as names of states of character that are neither identical with virtue and vice nor different from them in kind.

Here we must follow our usual method: first set down the appearances — the current opinions about these conditions — then work through the puzzles they raise, and finally establish, if we can, the truth of all the common opinions on the subject, or, failing that, of most of them and the most important. For if the difficulties are resolved and the popular notions left standing, we shall have attained as much certainty as the subject allows.

What, then, is commonly thought? (1) That self-restraint and endurance are good and praiseworthy things, while weakness of will and softness are bad and blameworthy. (2) That the self-restrained man is simply the man who stands by his reasoned conclusions, and the weak-willed man the one who abandons them. (3) That the weak-willed man knows that an act is bad and is driven to it by passion anyway, while the self-restrained man knows his desires are bad and is held back from following them by reason. (4) That the man of self-control is self-restrained and enduring — though as to the converse, some hold that every self-restrained and enduring man is self-controlled, and others deny it; and while some say the self-indulgent man is weak-willed and the weak-willed man self-indulgent, using the terms interchangeably, others draw a distinction between them. (5) As for the man of practical wisdom, people sometimes say it is impossible for him to be weak-willed, and at other times say that some men who are practically wise, and clever too, are weak-willed. (6) Lastly, people are called weak-willed even about anger, honor, and gain.

These, then, are the things people say.

Chapter 2

But an objection arises at once: in what sense can a man be judging rightly when he acts against his own judgment?

Some maintain that he cannot act that way if he really knows what is right. It would be strange, Socrates thought, if knowledge were actually in a man and something else mastered it and dragged it about like a slave. In fact Socrates fought the whole position root and branch: there is no such thing as weakness of will, he held; no one acts against what is best while judging it to be best — when a man acts that way, it is through ignorance.

Now this theory plainly contradicts experience. And if the passion that sways the weak-willed man really is due to ignorance, we must ask what kind of ignorance it is. For this much is clear: the man who acts against his better judgment does not think the act good before the passion is upon him.

There are others who agree with Socrates in part and disagree in part. They grant that nothing can prevail against knowledge, but they do not grant that men never act against what seems best; and so they say that when the weak-willed man gives in to pleasure, what he has is not knowledge but only opinion.

But if it really is opinion and not knowledge — if what opposes the desires is not a strong conviction but a weak one, like the belief of a man in doubt — then we excuse a man for not standing by it in the face of strong desires. But in fact we do not excuse vice, or anything else we call blameworthy.

Is it practical wisdom, then, that opposes desire in the cases where we blame a man for yielding? It is, after all, the strongest form of conviction. But surely that would be absurd: the same man would then be practically wise and weak-willed at once, and no one would say that a man of practical wisdom would voluntarily do the vilest acts. Besides, we have already shown that practical wisdom is essentially a capacity that issues in action — it deals with the last step, the thing to be done — and that it implies the possession of all the moral virtues.

Again: if a man cannot be self-restrained without having strong and bad desires, then the man of self-control will not be self-restrained, nor the self-restrained man self-controlled; for it is no part of the self-controlled character to have desires that are either violent or bad.

Yet in the self-restrained man the desires must be both strong and bad. If they were good, the habit that keeps him from following them would be bad, and self-restraint would not always be a good thing. If they were weak and not bad, resisting them would be nothing to respect; and if they were bad but weak, it would be nothing to admire.

Again: if self-restraint makes a man stand by any opinion whatsoever, it is a bad thing — as when it makes him stand by a false one. And if weakness of will makes a man abandon any opinion whatsoever, then there will be a good kind of weakness of will. Take Neoptolemus in Sophocles' Philoctetes: he deserves praise for failing to stick to the plan Odysseus had talked him into — and what stopped him was the pain he felt at telling a lie.

Again, the well-known argument of the sophists, fallacious as it is, creates a genuine difficulty. Wanting to establish a paradox — so that they will look clever if they pull it off — they build a syllogism that traps the hearer: his reason is fettered, since he is unwilling to rest in a conclusion that revolts him, but unable to move forward, since he cannot find the flaw in the argument. Thus it can be argued that folly combined with weakness of will is virtue: because of his weakness of will, a man does the opposite of what he judges to be good; but he judges that the good is bad and not to be done; the result is that he will do the good and not the bad.

Again, on this showing the man who pursues and does what is pleasant from conviction — who deliberately chooses these things — would seem to be better than the man who does so not on calculation but from weakness of will. For the first man is easier to cure: change his convictions and you change him. But the weak-willed man invites the proverb, "If water chokes you, what will you wash it down with?" If he were convinced that what he does is good, a change of conviction might stop him; but as it is, he is already convinced that something else is good — and he does this anyway.

Again, if weakness of will and self-restraint can show themselves in anything whatever, who is the man we call weak-willed simply? No one man unites all the various forms of it, yet there are people to whom we apply the term with no qualification at all.

Such, roughly, are the puzzles that arise. Some of them we must clear away, and some we must leave standing; for the solving of a puzzle is the discovery of the truth.

Chapter 3

Our questions, then, are these: (1) Does the weak-willed man act with knowledge or not — and what does "knowledge" mean here? (2) What is the field in which self-restraint and weakness of will operate — all pleasures and pains, or certain definite classes of them? (3) Are the self-restrained man and the man of endurance the same or different? And so on with the other questions that belong to this inquiry.

(Though the proper starting point is to ask whether the self-restraint and the weakness of will we are discussing are marked off from other kinds by their field or by their manner — I mean, whether a man is weak-willed in this special sense merely because his reason loses control in certain matters, or because it loses control in a certain way, or on both grounds; and, connected with this, whether this weakness of will and this self-restraint can show themselves in everything. Our answer will be: the man who is called weak-willed simply, with no qualification, does not show his character in everything, but only in the matters where the self-indulgent man shows his. Nor is it simply an uncontrolled disposition toward those matters that makes him what he is — that would make weakness of will identical with self-indulgence — but a particular kind of uncontrolled disposition. For the self-indulgent man is carried along by his own deliberate choice, holding that the pleasure of the moment is always to be pursued; the weak-willed man thinks otherwise — and pursues it all the same.) Now to question (1).

As for the argument that it is true opinion, not knowledge, against which men act when they act weak-willedly — it makes no difference here. Some people who merely hold opinions are in no doubt whatever; they think they have exact knowledge.

If the reply is that those with mere opinion act against their judgment more readily because their belief is weak, we answer that knowledge and opinion do not differ in that way: some people believe their bare opinions as strongly as others believe what they really know. Heraclitus is proof enough of that.

But we use the word "know" in two different senses. A man who has knowledge he is not now using is said to know a thing, and so is a man who is now using it. It will make a difference, then, whether a man has knowledge of what he ought not to do without that knowledge being present to his mind, or has it actually present. Only the latter seems strange — acting against knowledge you are attending to; acting against knowledge you merely possess does not.

Again, since reasoning of this kind involves two sorts of premises — a universal proposition for the major and a particular for the minor — nothing prevents a man from acting against his knowledge while having both, if he is using only the universal and not the particular; for it is the particular that gets acted on.

Again, there are different kinds of universal: one may concern the agent himself, another the thing. For instance, a man may reason, "dry food is good for every man, and I am a man," or "food of such-and-such a kind is dry" — but whether this food here is of that kind is something he may not know, or may know without the knowledge being awake in him.

These distinctions make an enormous difference — so much so that acting against knowledge in the one way seems not at all strange, while in the other way it does seem strange.

But there is yet another way of "having knowledge" besides those just mentioned. Within "having without using," we can see, there are different modes of having, so that a man may in one sense have knowledge and in another sense not have it — a man who is asleep, for instance, or mad, or drunk. And people in the grip of passion are in just such a state; for anger, sexual desire, and the like visibly alter the condition of the body, and in some cases actually produce madness. Clearly, then, we must say that the weak-willed man has knowledge in the same sort of way as the sleeping, the mad, and the drunk.

And saying the words of knowledge is no proof of really having it. Even in the grip of these passions, people recite demonstrations and verses of Empedocles — just as beginners string sentences together before they understand what they mean; the meaning has to grow into them, and that takes time. So we must suppose that the weak-willed say the words the way actors do.

Again, one may look for the cause of the phenomenon in a way proper to its own nature, as follows. There is (1) the universal judgment, and (2) the judgment about particular facts, which falls to sense-perception. When the two are joined into one, the soul must — in theoretical matters — affirm the conclusion, and in practical matters must carry it straight out into action. For instance, if you judge "everything sweet should be tasted" and "this thing before me is sweet" — a particular fact — then, if you have the power and are not prevented, you cannot help but act on the conclusion at once: taste it.

Now suppose there is on one side the universal judgment forbidding you to taste, and on the other side the universal "everything sweet is pleasant," with its particular "this thing before me is sweet" — and it is the particular judgment that is operative — and appetite happens to be present. The first chain of reasoning tells you to avoid the thing; appetite drives you toward it, for appetite can set each of the bodily parts in motion.

So it turns out that when people act weak-willedly, it is in a way under the influence of reason — of opinion, that is — but of opinion that is opposed to right reason not in itself, only incidentally. For it is the desire, not the opinion, that is opposed to right reason.

And this is why brutes cannot be weak-willed: they have no universal judgments, only images and memories of particular facts.

As for how the weak-willed man gets out of his ignorance and recovers his knowledge, the account is the same as for the man who is drunk or asleep; nothing about it is peculiar to this phenomenon, and for that account we must go to the students of natural science.

But since the last premise — the minor — is an opinion about something perceived, and is what determines action, this is the one the weak-willed man in his passion either does not have, or has only in the sense in which, as we explained, having means not knowing in the full sense but saying the words, as a drunk man recites the sayings of Empedocles.

And because the last term is not universal, and does not seem to be an object of knowledge in the way the universal is, even what Socrates was trying to establish seems to come out true. For when passion carries a man away, what it drags about is not what we regard as knowledge in the strict sense — nor is it such knowledge that gets perverted by passion — but only the knowledge of perception.

Chapter 4

So much, then, for whether the weak-willed man knows or not, and in what sense it is possible to act weak-willedly with knowledge. We must next ask whether a man can be weak-willed simply, or only weak-willed in some particular respect — and, if the former, what the field is in which the character shows itself.

It is evident that it is in pleasures and pains that the self-restrained and the enduring, and the weak-willed and the soft, show what they are.

Of the sources of pleasure, some are necessary, and others are desirable in themselves but admit of excess. The necessary ones are the bodily processes — nutrition, the propagation of the species, and generally the bodily functions we said self-indulgence and self-control are concerned with. The others are not necessary but are desirable in themselves: victory, honor, wealth, and good and pleasant things of that kind.

Now, those who go to excess in this second class against their own better reason we do not call weak-willed simply, but with a qualifier: weak-willed about money, or gain, or honor, or anger — not simply, because they are different characters and get the name only by resemblance. (Compare the man who won at the last Olympic games: he was called Man, for that was his name; the word as applied to him meant almost, but not quite, what it ordinarily means.)

Here is the proof: weakness of will is blamed not merely as a mistake but as a kind of vice — either vice simply or some particular vice — but the people just described are not blamed that way.

Among the characters concerned with the bodily enjoyments, though — the ones we say self-control and self-indulgence deal with — take the man who goes to excess in pursuing what is pleasant and avoiding what is painful, in the matter of hunger and thirst, heat and cold, and everything that reaches us through touch or taste, and who does this not from deliberate choice but against his choice and his reasoning. Him we call weak-willed with no addition — not "weak-willed about this particular thing, say anger," but simply weak-willed.

A proof of this: people are also called soft in these latter matters, but not in any of the former — not about honor or gain.

And this is why we group the weak-willed man with the self-indulgent, and the self-restrained with the self-controlled — but none of those who are only metaphorically called self-restrained and weak-willed — because they are in a way concerned with the same pleasures and pains. They deal with the same matters, but they behave differently: the other three deliberately choose what they do; the weak-willed man does not.

And so a man who pursues excessive pleasure with no desire, or only a moderate one, and who runs from even slight pains, is more properly called self-indulgent than one who is driven to it by violent desires. For what would the first man do if the violent passions of youth were added — if going without his natural appetites were violent pain to him?

Now, some of our desires and pleasures belong to the class of the noble and good (for some of the things that please us are naturally desirable), others to the opposite class, and others lie in between, as we distinguished before — money, gain, victory, and honor falling in the first class. With regard to all of these, the first class and the intermediate alike, men are blamed not for being affected by them, or desiring them, or caring for them, but for doing so in certain ways and beyond the due amount. So we blame those who are moved by some good and noble object, or pursue it, past the bounds of reason — people who care too much about honor, say, or about their children or parents. These are noble objects, and men are praised for caring about them; but even here one can go too far — if, like Niobe, one were to fight against the gods themselves, or carry things as far as Satyrus, nicknamed Philopator, "father-lover," whose devotion to his father seemed to reach the pitch of outright folly.

In these matters, then, there is no room for vice or wickedness, for the reason given: each of these things is in itself desirable, and only the excess in them is bad and to be avoided.

Similarly, there is no room here for weakness of will in the strict sense — for weakness of will is not only to be avoided; it is positively blameworthy. But because the condition looks similar, we do use the term with a qualification, saying "weak-willed in this" or "in that" — just as we call a man a "bad doctor" or a "bad actor" whom we would never call bad flat-out. In those cases the term "badness" applies not simply but with a qualification, because each of these qualities is not strictly a vice but only analogous to one; and likewise here, it is plain, we must count as weakness of will (or self-restraint) in the strict sense only what shows itself in the matters that self-control and self-indulgence deal with, while what shows itself about anger gets the name only by metaphor. That is why we add the qualifier and call a man "weak-willed in anger," as "in honor" or "in gain."

Chapter 5

Some things are pleasant by nature — some of them pleasant in themselves, others pleasant to particular kinds of animals or people. Other things are not naturally pleasant at all, but come to be pleasant in one of three ways: (1) through physical damage, (2) through habit, or (3) through an originally corrupt nature. And each of these three classes has a character type to match.

Take the third class first: the brutish characters. There is the creature in a woman's form that is said to tear open pregnant women and devour their unborn children; there are the peoples around the Black Sea who are said to delight in things like eating raw flesh or human flesh, or handing over their children to one another for the feast; and there are the things reported of Phalaris.

These are what we call brutish natures. In other cases the disposition comes from disease or madness — like the man who killed and ate his mother, or the slave who ate the liver of his fellow slave. These belong to the first class.

Other habits are either symptoms of a morbid state or the products of habituation, and so fall under the first class or the second: plucking out one's hair, for instance, or biting the nails, or eating cinders and earth, or unnatural sexual practices. These come sometimes from nature, sometimes from habit — as with those who have been abused from childhood.

Now, wherever nature is the cause of such morbid habits, no one would think of using the term "weakness of will" — any more than we would call women weak-willed for the part they play in reproduction. Nor would we use the term of those who have brought themselves into a morbid state by habitual indulgence.

Having habits of this kind, then, falls outside the boundaries of vice, just as brutishness does; and when a man who has such impulses masters them, or is mastered by them, that is not self-restraint or weakness of will in the strict sense, but only by a metaphor — just as the man who is mastered by his temper cannot strictly be called weak-willed. For even folly, cowardice, self-indulgence, and bad temper, whenever they run beyond a certain pitch, are themselves either brutish or morbid. A man so constituted by nature that he is frightened of everything, even the squeak of a mouse — his cowardice is brutish, inhuman; in the well-known case of the man who was terrified of a weasel, disease was the cause. So too with irrational human beings: those who lack reason by nature and live by their senses alone are brutish, like some races of remote barbarians, while those whose irrationality comes from disease (epilepsy, say) or insanity are morbidly irrational.

Again, a man may on occasion have one of these impulses without being mastered by it — if Phalaris, say, had felt the desire to eat a child's flesh, or some monstrous lust, and held himself back. And it is equally possible not merely to have the impulse but to be mastered by it.

To sum up, then: just as with vice there is one sort that is human and is called vice simply, and another sort that carries a qualifying label — "brutish" or "morbid" vice, not vice simply — in the same way there is plainly a brutish weakness of will and a morbid weakness of will, while only the kind that pairs with human self-indulgence is weakness of will simply.

We have now shown that weakness of will and self-restraint proper have to do only with the things that self-indulgence and self-control have to do with, and that in other matters there is a kind of weakness of will so called only metaphorically, and with a qualifying label.

Chapter 6

The next point to consider is that weakness of will about anger is less disgraceful than weakness of will about appetite.

Anger, it seems, listens to reason a little, but mishears it — like a hasty servant who dashes off before you have finished speaking and so gets the errand wrong, or like a dog that barks the moment it hears a noise, without waiting to find out whether it is a friend. Just so anger, hot and hasty by nature, hears something — but not what reason actually orders — and rushes off to take revenge. For when reason or imagination announces an insult or a slight, anger draws the inference, so to speak, that the offender is to be treated as an enemy, and instantly boils over. Appetite, on the other hand, needs only for reason or the senses to announce "this is pleasant," and it rushes to enjoy it. So anger, in a way, obeys reason, and appetite does not. Appetite is therefore the more disgraceful; for the man who is weak-willed about anger gives in, in some sense, to reason, while the other gives in not to reason but to appetite.

Again, it is more excusable to follow impulses that are natural — even with our appetites, it is more pardonable to follow the ones common to all men, and the more common they are, the more pardonable. And anger and bad temper are more natural than the desire for excessive and unnecessary pleasures. Recall the man who defended himself for beating his father: "He beat his own father," he said, "and that father beat his; and this boy here" — pointing to his son — "will beat me when he is grown. It runs in the family." And there is the other story of the man being dragged out of the house by his son, who told him to stop at the doorway — for he had dragged his own father exactly that far, and no further.

Again, the more a man is given to calculated malice, the more unjust he is. Now the hot-tempered man is not given to calculated malice, and anger is not underhanded — it asserts itself in the open. Appetite is another matter: say of it what the poets say of Aphrodite, "craft-weaving daughter of Cyprus," or what Homer says of her embroidered belt,

  whose charm steals away the reason even of the wise.

If, then, this weakness of will is the more unjust, it is also more disgraceful than weakness of will about anger; it is weakness of will simply, and a sort of vice.

Again, when a man commits a wanton outrage he feels no pain in the doing — rather pleasure — while a man who acts in anger always feels pain as he acts. If, then, the acts that most justly rouse our indignation are the more unjust, it follows that weakness of will about appetite is the more unjust; for outrage of that kind is never committed in anger.

Plainly, then, weakness of will about appetite is more disgraceful than weakness of will about anger, and self-restraint and weakness of will proper are concerned with bodily appetites and pleasures.

But now let us look at the differences among these bodily appetites and pleasures.

As we said at the outset, some are human and natural in kind and degree; others are marks of a brutish nature; others still are the result of injury or disease.

Now, self-control and self-indulgence have to do only with the first class. That is why we call animals neither self-controlled nor self-indulgent — except metaphorically, if some whole species stands out from the rest by its lewdness and wantonness and greed. For there is no choice or deliberate calculation in what animals do; they stand outside the rational order, like madmen.

Brutishness is less dangerous than vice, but more horrifying. For in the brutish man the noble part is not corrupted, as it is in a man who is vicious in the merely human way — it is absent altogether. So asking which is worse is like comparing the inanimate with the animate: the badness of a thing that lacks the governing principle is always less destructive, and here reason is the governing principle — and the brutish man lacks it. It is like comparing injustice with an unjust man: each, in its own way, is the worse. For a bad man will do ten thousand times the harm a brute will.

Chapter 7

Now, with regard to the pleasures and pains of touch and taste, and the desires and aversions that go with them — the field we earlier marked out as belonging to self-indulgence and self-control — it is possible to be so disposed that you give in to temptations most people resist, or so disposed that you resist temptations most people give in to. Where pleasures are concerned, the first of these characters is the weak-willed man and the second the self-restrained; where pains are concerned, the first is soft and the second enduring. The character of most people lies between the two — leaning, perhaps, toward the worse.

Now some pleasures are necessary and others are not, and the necessary ones are necessary only in certain quantities — not too much and not too little; and the same holds of appetites and pains. The man who pursues pleasures beyond the pale of legitimate pleasure, or pursues any pleasure to excess, and does so by deliberate choice, for their own sake and not for any further consequence — that man is self-indulgent. Such a man is necessarily incapable of remorse, and therefore incurable; for the man who feels no remorse cannot be cured. At the opposite extreme is the man who falls short (while the man who hits the mean is self-controlled). The same goes for the man who runs away from bodily pains not because he is overcome in the moment, but by deliberate choice.

Of those who act without deliberate choice, one sort is driven by the pleasure itself, the other by the wish to escape the pain of unsatisfied desire; so we must distinguish these from one another.

But everyone would agree that a man who does something disgraceful with no desire, or only a mild one, is worse than one who does it under violent desire — that a man who strikes another in cold blood is worse than one who strikes in anger. For what would he do if passion had hold of him? That is why the self-indulgent man is worse than the weak-willed.

Of the characters we have described, then, we must mark off softness from self-indulgence.

The self-restrained character is the opposite of the weak-willed, and the enduring the opposite of the soft. For endurance means holding out, while self-restraint means overcoming — and holding out is a different thing from overcoming, as avoiding defeat is different from winning a victory. So self-restraint is the better of the two.

But the man who caves in where most men can and do hold out is soft and luxurious (for luxury, too, is a kind of softness) — the sort of man who lets his cloak trail on the ground rather than take the trouble to lift it, who plays the invalid without supposing himself wretched, though a wretched man is exactly what he is imitating.

It is the same with self-restraint and weakness of will. If a man gives way to pleasures or pains that are violent and excessive, we are not surprised; we are ready to forgive him, if he struggled — like Philoctetes bitten by the viper in Theodectes' play, or Cercyon in the Alope of Carcinus, or like people who try to hold in a laugh and burst out in one violent explosion, as happened to Xenophantus. What does surprise us is when a man gives way to, and cannot resist, what most people are able to hold out against — unless the cause is inherited constitution or disease, as softness is hereditary in the Scythian kings, and as the female is naturally softer than the male.

The man who is devoted to amusement is generally thought to be self-indulgent, but in fact he is soft. For amusement is relaxation, a rest from work; and the man devoted to amusement is one of those who take too much of it.

Weakness of will comes in two kinds: the impetuous and the weak. The weak deliberate, but under the influence of passion do not stand by the results of their deliberation; the impetuous are swept off by passion because they never deliberated at all. For — just as you cannot easily tickle a man who has just been tickling you — some people, when they see what is coming, forewarned, rouse themselves and their reason in advance, and so are able to resist the impulse, whether it is pleasant or painful. It is people of quick feeling, and people of a melancholic temperament, who are most prone to the impetuous kind of weakness of will: the first through the speed of their impressions, the second through their intensity, they do not wait to hear the voice of reason, but follow their imagination.

Chapter 8

Again, the self-indulgent man, as we said, is not given to remorse, for he stands by his deliberate choice; but the weak-willed man is always apt to feel remorse. So the matter does not stand as it was put in one of the difficulties we listed: the self-indulgent man is incurable, the weak-willed man curable. For full-formed vice is like diseases such as dropsy or consumption, while weakness of will is like epilepsy: the one is chronic badness, the other comes in fits.

Indeed, we may say flatly that weakness of will and vice are different in kind; for the vicious man does not know the nature of his acts, and the weak-willed man does.

Among the weak-willed themselves, those who momentarily lose their reason are not as bad as those who keep their reason and disobey it; for the latter give way to a slighter impulse, and cannot, like the former, claim to have acted without deliberating. The weak-willed man is like someone who gets drunk quickly and on little wine — on less than it takes most men.

We have seen that weakness of will is not vice; and yet in a manner of speaking it perhaps is. The difference is that the vicious man acts by deliberate choice and the weak-willed man against it. But for all that, their acts look alike — as Demodocus said against the Milesians: "The Milesians are not fools, but they do just what fools would do." So the weak-willed man is not unjust, but he will act unjustly.

It is the mark of the weak-willed man to pursue bodily pleasures that are excessive and contrary to right reason without being convinced that they are good; the self-indulgent man is convinced they are good, because it is his character to pursue them. The first, then, can easily be brought to a better mind; the second cannot. For virtue preserves the principle — the starting point — while vice destroys it; and in conduct the motive, the end we act for, is the principle of action, holding the same place here that the hypotheses hold in mathematics. In mathematics, no reasoning or proof can teach us the starting points; and here, likewise, it is not reasoning but virtue — whether natural or acquired by training — that teaches us to hold right opinions about the principle of action. The man of this character is self-controlled; the man of the opposite character is self-indulgent.

But there is a class of men who are apt to be momentarily carried out of their right senses by passion — swayed by it far enough that they do not act according to reason, but not so far that it has become part of their nature to believe such pleasures should be pursued without limit. These are the weak-willed. They are better than the self-indulgent, and not absolutely bad; for the best part of their nature, the principle of right conduct, still survives in them.

Opposed to them is another class of men: those who stand by their resolutions and are not carried out of their senses by passion, at least. From all this it is plain that self-restraint is a good state of character and weakness of will a bad one.

Chapter 9

Now, who is to be called self-restrained — the man who stands by reasoning of any kind and a choice of any kind, or only the man who stands by the right choice? And who is to be called weak-willed — the man who abandons any kind of choice and any kind of reasoning, or the man who abandons true reasoning and right choice? This was a difficulty we raised before. Is not the answer this: incidentally it may be any kind, but essentially it is true reasoning and right choice that the one stands by and the other abandons? For if you choose or pursue A for the sake of B, it is B that you pursue and choose essentially, and A only incidentally. And by "essentially" we mean "absolutely" or "simply." So in a certain sense it can be opinion of any kind; but speaking absolutely, it is true opinion that the one holds to and the other deserts.

But there is another class of people who are apt to stick to their opinions no matter what — the ones we call stubborn — because they resist persuasion and are not easily talked into changing their minds. These bear some resemblance to the self-restrained man, as the extravagant man resembles the generous one and the foolhardy man the courageous; but on many points they differ. What the self-restrained man refuses is to change his mind under pressure of passion and appetite; when occasion calls, he yields to reason readily enough. But it is precisely to reason that the stubborn man will not listen — and he is quite capable of conceiving a passion, and is often led around by his pleasures.

The opinionated, the ignorant, and the boorish are all stubborn — the opinionated from motives of pleasure and pain: they delight in the sense of victory when no argument can budge them, and are pained when their opinions are overruled, like decrees struck down. So they resemble the weak-willed man rather than the self-restrained.

Sometimes, too, people abandon their resolutions from something other than weakness of will — as Neoptolemus does in Sophocles' Philoctetes. Pleasure, it is true, was his motive for abandoning his resolution — but a noble pleasure; for telling the truth was a fine thing in his eyes, and Odysseus had persuaded him to lie. For not everyone who acts with pleasure as his motive is self-indulgent, or worthless, or weak-willed — only the man whose motive is a base pleasure.

Again, there is a character that takes too little delight in the pleasures of the body, and swerves from reason in that direction; and the self-restrained man stands between this character and the weak-willed. For the weak-willed swerve from reason through an excess, and these others through a deficiency, while the self-restrained man holds fast and is not pulled aside by either.

And if self-restraint is a good thing, both of the opposed characters must be bad — as in fact they evidently are. But since the second extreme shows itself rarely and in few people, weakness of will comes to be regarded as the only opposite of self-restraint, just as self-indulgence comes to be regarded as the only opposite of self-control.

We often apply names metaphorically, and so we come to speak — metaphorically — of the self-restraint of the self-controlled man. For both the self-restrained man and the self-controlled man are so made that they never do anything against reason for the sake of bodily pleasure; but the self-restrained man has bad desires and the self-controlled man has none; and the self-controlled man is so constituted that he takes no delight in anything contrary to reason, while the self-restrained man does take delight in such things — but is not carried away by them.

The weak-willed and the self-indulgent resemble each other too, though they are different: both pursue bodily pleasures, but the self-indulgent man pursues them on principle, and the weak-willed man does not.

Chapter 10

The same man cannot be at once practically wise and weak-willed; for we have shown that a man cannot have practical wisdom without being morally good at the same time.

Besides, a man is not practically wise just because he knows; he must also be ready to act on what he knows. But the weak-willed man is not ready to act on his knowledge. (Nothing prevents a man who is clever at calculating means from being weak-willed — which is why people sometimes think a man can be practically wise and weak-willed at once: cleverness is related to practical wisdom in the way we explained earlier, resembling it as an intellectual power but differing from it because choice is absent.) Nor does the weak-willed man know in the way a man knows who is actually using his knowledge; he knows the way a man asleep or drunk knows.

He acts voluntarily — in a sense he knows what he is doing and why — and yet he is not a bad man, for his choice is good. He is only half bad. Nor are weak-willed men unjust, for they are not deliberately malicious: some of them are the kind who swerve from their deliberate resolutions; others, of the melancholic temperament, act without deliberating at all. The weak-willed man, then, is like a city that always passes excellent decrees and has good laws, but never enforces them — as Anaxandrides joked:

  So willed the city that cares nothing for its laws.

The bad man, on the contrary, is like a city that enforces its laws, but its laws are bad.

Both weakness of will and self-restraint go beyond the average run of men: the self-restrained man is more steadfast than most men can manage, the weak-willed man less.

Of the kinds of weakness of will, the melancholic sort is more curable than that of the men who make resolutions and fail to keep them; and the kind that comes from habit is more curable than the kind rooted in nature. It is easier to change a habit than to change one's nature — indeed, the very reason habits are hard to change is that they are a kind of second nature, as Euenus says:

  Train men long enough to what you will,
  and in the end it will be their nature.

We have now said what self-restraint and weakness of will are, and endurance and softness, and how these types of character stand to one another.

Of Pleasure

Chapter 11

Pleasure and pain also fall within the province of the political philosopher, since it is his business to fix the end by reference to which we call each thing good or bad.

Besides, we are bound to discuss them. We said that moral virtue and vice have to do with pleasures and pains, and most people say that happiness involves pleasure — which is why the blessed man is called makarios, from chairein, "to rejoice."

Now (1) some people think no pleasure is good, either in itself or incidentally; good and pleasure, they say, are simply two different things. (2) Others think that some pleasures are good but most are bad. (3) Others again think that even if every pleasure is good, still the highest good cannot be pleasure.

(1) It is argued that pleasure cannot be good at all: (a) because every pleasure is a felt transition to a natural state, and a transition or process is never the same kind of thing as an end — the building of a house is one kind of thing, the house another; (b) because the self-controlled man avoids pleasures; (c) because the man of practical wisdom pursues the painless, not the pleasant; (d) because pleasures get in the way of thinking, and the more intense the pleasure the worse the interference — sexual pleasure, for instance: no one in the middle of that could think at all; (e) because there is no art of pleasure, yet every good thing has an art devoted to producing it; (f) because pleasure is what children and animals pursue.

(2) It is argued that not all pleasures are good, because some are base and disgraceful, and some are actually harmful — some pleasant things are unhealthy.

(3) It is argued that pleasure is not the highest good, because it is not an end but a process or transition.

These, then, are the current opinions on the subject.

Chapter 12

But these arguments do not prove that pleasure is not good, or even that it is not the highest good. Here is why.

In the first place, "good" is used in two senses — good in itself and good for somebody — so natures and faculties will be called good in two senses, and so will motions and processes. When these are called bad, it sometimes means bad in themselves, though for particular persons not bad but desirable; sometimes not even desirable for particular persons except now and then, for a little while, though not desirable in themselves. And some of them are not even pleasures at all, though they look like it — I mean the ones that involve pain and are taken as medicine, like the pleasures of the sick.

In the second place, since "good" applies both to activities and to faculties, the activities that restore us to our natural state are only incidentally pleasant.

But in the satisfaction of the animal appetites, what is active is not the part of our nature that feels the want, but the part that is in its normal state. For there are pleasures that involve no previous pain or appetite at all — the pleasures of philosophic study, for instance — where our nature is conscious of no want.

This is confirmed by the fact that while a natural want is being filled we do not delight in the same things as when it has been filled. Once the want is filled, we delight in things pleasant in themselves; while it is filling, in their opposites — in sharp and bitter things, none of which is pleasant by nature or pleasant in itself. So the pleasures these things give are not real pleasures either; for pleasures stand to one another as the things that produce them do.

Again, it does not follow, as some maintain, that there must be something better than pleasure, on the ground that the end is better than the process toward it. Pleasure is not a transition, and it does not even always involve one. It is an activity, and itself an end. Further, pleasure comes not from becoming something but from doing something. And lastly, the end is not always different from the process: that holds only where something is being brought to the completion of its nature.

For these reasons it is not right to call pleasure a felt transition. Call it rather an activity of our nature in its natural state, and in place of "felt" write "unimpeded."

Some people, it is true, think pleasure must be a transition precisely because it is good in the full sense — they suppose that activity is a transition. But activity is in fact something different.

To say that pleasures are bad because some pleasant things are unhealthy is like saying that health is bad because some healthy things are bad for moneymaking. Both are bad in that one respect, but that does not make them bad; even philosophic study is sometimes bad for your health.

As for pleasure being an obstacle to thinking: neither practical wisdom nor any other faculty is hindered by the pleasure proper to its own exercise — only by alien pleasures. The pleasure we take in study and learning makes us study and learn all the more.

That there is no art devoted to producing pleasure is only natural, for art never produces an activity; it only makes activity possible. (Though perfumery and cookery are usually counted arts of pleasure.)

As for the arguments that the self-controlled man avoids pleasure, that the man of practical wisdom pursues the painless life, and that children and animals pursue pleasure — one answer meets them all. We have already explained in what sense all pleasures are good in themselves and in what sense they are not. The pleasures that children and animals pursue, and whose corresponding pains the man of practical wisdom seeks to escape, are the pleasures that involve appetite and pain — that is, the bodily pleasures (for those are of this kind) — and the excesses of them, the deliberate pursuit of which is exactly what makes a man self-indulgent. These, then, are the pleasures the self-controlled man avoids. But he has pleasures of his own.

Chapter 13

Everyone agrees that pain is a bad thing and to be avoided: partly bad in itself, partly bad as somehow impeding activity. But the opposite of what is to be avoided — opposite in the very respect in which it is to be avoided and bad — is good. It follows that pleasure is a good thing. Speusippus tried to dodge this argument with an analogy: the greater, he said, is opposed both to the equal and to the less. But the dodge fails, for no one would say that pleasure is essentially a bad thing.

Moreover, there is no reason why a certain kind of pleasure should not be the highest good, even though some pleasures are bad — just as a certain kind of knowledge might be, though some kinds are bad. Indeed, perhaps we ought to put it more strongly: since every faculty admits of unimpeded activity, then whether happiness is the activity of all our faculties or of some one of them, that activity must be most desirable when it is unimpeded. But unimpeded activity is pleasure. So a certain kind of pleasure will be the highest good, even if most pleasures should turn out to be bad in themselves.

And this is why all men suppose the happy life is a pleasant one and weave pleasure into happiness — reasonably enough, for no activity is complete if it is impeded, and happiness we count among complete things. That is why the happy man needs the goods of the body and external goods and good fortune as well: so that his activity may not be impeded. As for those who say that a man on the rack, overwhelmed by misfortune, is happy so long as he is good — whether they know it or not, they are talking nonsense.

Because fortune is a necessary condition, some people take good fortune to be the same thing as happiness. It is not. Good fortune in excess actually becomes an impediment, and then perhaps no longer deserves the name; for good fortune is defined by its relation to happiness.

Again, the fact that all animals and all men pursue pleasure is some sign that it is in some way the highest good:

  A saying never wholly dies
  that many peoples share.
