But since neither the nature of man nor the best development of his faculties is the same for all, or is thought to be, the pleasure men pursue is not always the same either — though all pursue pleasure. Yet perhaps they are in fact pursuing not the pleasure they think they pursue, and would say they pursue, but one and the same pleasure for all; for every being has something divine implanted in it by nature.

But the bodily pleasures have taken over the very name of pleasure, because they are the ones most often reached and the ones everybody shares. Being the only pleasures men know, they are supposed to be the only pleasures there are.

And it is plain that unless pleasure — that is, unimpeded activity — is good, we can no longer say that the happy man lives a pleasant life. Why should he need pleasure if it is not good? He might just as well live a painful life; for pain is neither bad nor good, if pleasure is neither — so why avoid it? The good man's life, then, would be no pleasanter than anyone else's, unless the activity of his faculties were pleasanter too.

Chapter 14

Those who grant that some pleasures — the noble ones — are highly desirable, but deny it of the bodily pleasures that are the self-indulgent man's territory, should look harder at those bodily pleasures. Why, if they are bad, are the opposite pains bad? For the opposite of bad is good. Shall we say the "necessary" pleasures are good only in the sense that whatever is not bad is good? Or that they are good up to a point?

Faculties and activities that cannot run to excess beyond what is good cannot yield excessive pleasure; those that can run to excess admit excessive pleasure too. Now, bodily goods do admit of excess, and the bad man is bad because he pursues the excess, not because he pursues the necessary pleasures — everyone takes some delight in food and drink and sex, but not everyone as he ought. With pain it is the reverse: the bad man does not merely avoid excessive pain, he avoids pain altogether. And this is quite consistent with pain being bad; for the opposite of excessive pleasure is not painful, except to the man who chases the excess.

But we ought to state not only the truth, but also the cause of the error; it helps to produce conviction. Once we see what would naturally make a false view look true, we believe the true one more readily. So we must say why the bodily pleasures seem more desirable.

First of all, then, bodily pleasure drives out pain. Because pain can be extreme, men pursue excessive pleasure — and bodily pleasure generally — as its antidote. These remedies are intense, and that is why they are pursued: they stand out in sharp contrast to the opposite pain.

(As we said before, there are two reasons pleasure has a bad name: some pleasures are the doings of a bad nature — bad from birth, as with an animal, or bad by habit, as with bad men; while the remedial pleasures imply want, and it is better to be in the natural state than to be on the way to it. These remedial pleasures are felt while a want is being filled up, and so they are only incidentally good.)

Again, bodily pleasures are pursued for their intensity by people who cannot enjoy anything else — some even work up a thirst on purpose. When the pleasures are harmless, we do not blame this; when they are harmful, the pursuit is bad. Such people simply have no other sources of enjoyment, and to many the neutral state is itself painful, because of their nature. For an animal is always laboring, as natural science teaches us — even seeing and hearing are labor and pain, it says; we have just grown used to them, as the saying goes. In youth, likewise, men are in a state resembling drunkenness, because they are growing — and youth is pleasant. Men of a melancholic nature, on the other hand, are always needing something to restore their balance; their bodies keep vexing them, thanks to their temperament, and they live in a state of violent desire. Now pain is driven out by the opposite pleasure — indeed by any pleasure, if it is strong enough. That is how such men come to be self-indulgent and bad.

But pleasures that have no antecedent pain do not admit of excess. These come from things that are pleasant by nature and not merely incidentally. By incidentally pleasant I mean the restoratives: the restoration happens only because the part of the system that remains healthy is somehow active, and so the restoration itself seems pleasant. By naturally pleasant I mean what stimulates the activity of a healthy system.

But no one thing can go on pleasing us without interruption, because our nature is not simple; there is a second element in us — this is what makes us mortal beings — so that whatever the one element does runs against the nature of the other, and when the two are in balance, what we are doing feels neither painful nor pleasant. If there were a being with a simple nature, the same activity would always be most pleasant to that being. This is why God enjoys forever one simple pleasure: for there is activity without movement as well as activity of movement, and there is truer pleasure in rest than in motion. Change is "the sweetest of all things," as the poet says, only because of a certain badness in us: just as it is the bad man who is most apt to change, so it is the bad nature that needs change — for it is neither simple nor good.

We have now considered self-restraint and weakness of will, and pleasure and pain — what each of them is, and in what way some of them are good and some bad. It remains to speak of friendship.

Book VIII

Friendship or Love

Chapter 1

After what has gone before, a discussion of friendship naturally comes next; for friendship is a sort of virtue, or at least it goes hand in hand with virtue, and besides that, it is one of life's absolute necessities. No one would choose to live without friends, even if he had every other good thing. In fact, it is precisely when a man is rich, when he holds power and authority, that he seems to need friends most of all. What is the use of all that prosperity if he has no chance to do good with it? And doing good is shown most often, and most admirably, toward friends. Or how could such a position even be kept safe without friends? The greater it is, the more danger it stands in. And in poverty, and in every other misfortune, we treat our friends as our one refuge. We need friends when we are young, to keep us from going wrong; when we are old, to care for us and to carry out the plans we no longer have the strength to execute ourselves; and in the prime of life, to help us do noble deeds — "two going together," as Homer says; for two together are stronger both in thought and in action.

Love seems to be planted by nature in the parent for the offspring and in the offspring for the parent — not only among human beings, but among birds and most animals too. It is planted in members of the same species for one another, and among human beings especially; that is why we praise those who love their fellow men. And anyone who travels can see how every human being is akin to every other, and dear to him.

Again, friendship seems to be the bond that holds states together, and lawgivers seem to care even more about securing it than about securing justice. For concord looks like a close cousin of friendship, and concord is what lawgivers want most to preserve, while faction is what they want most to drive out as an enemy. If citizens are friends, they have no need of justice; but even if they are just, they still need friendship or love as well — indeed, justice in its completest form seems to be a matter of friendship or love too.

More than that: friendship is not only necessary, it is a noble thing. We praise those who love their friends; having many friends is thought to be something noble; and some people even think that a good man and a friend are one and the same.

But opinions about friendship differ on quite a few points. Some hold that it is a kind of likeness — that people who are like one another are friends. Hence the sayings "Like to like" and "Birds of a feather flock together," and others of the same sort. Others say the opposite: "Two of a trade never agree."

Still others dig deeper into these questions and look for the causes behind the appearances. Euripides, for instance, says:

  The parched earth loves the rain,
  and the high heaven, heavy with moisture,
  loves to fall to earth.

Heraclitus says, "Opposites fit together," and "From discordant elements comes the fairest harmony," and "It is by strife that all things come into being." Others take the opposite view — Empedocles above all — and say that like desires like.

Now, the difficulties that concern the constitution of the universe we may set aside; they do not really belong to our present inquiry. But the ones that concern human nature, and are bound up with human character and feeling, we will discuss. For instance: can friendship arise in all men, or is it impossible for bad men to be friends? And is there one form of friendship, or many? Those who suppose there is only one kind, on the ground that friendship admits of more and less, are relying on insufficient evidence; for things that differ in kind can also differ in degree. (But we have spoken about this point already.)

Chapter 2

Perhaps these difficulties will clear up once we determine what it is that is lovable. For it seems we do not love just anything, but only what is lovable, and the lovable is either good or pleasant or useful. But "useful" would seem to mean whatever helps us get some good or some pleasure — so that, in the end, only the good and the pleasant are loved as ends.

Now, do men love what is good, or what is good for themselves? For the two sometimes come apart.

The same question can be asked about the pleasant.

It seems that each man loves what is good for himself: the good is lovable in itself, but what is lovable to each man is what is good for him. Someone may object that each man loves not what is really good for him but what seems good for him. It makes no difference; the lovable we are talking about will then simply be the apparently lovable.

The motives of love, then, are three. Now, love for inanimate things is not called friendship; for there is no return of affection there, and no wishing for the thing's own good. It would be absurd to wish well to wine, for instance — at most we wish that it may keep well, so that we can have it. But it is commonly said that we must wish our friend's good for his own sake. A man who wishes another's good in this way is called a well-wisher when the wish is not returned; when the well-wishing is mutual, it is called friendship.

But shouldn't we add that each must be aware of the other's goodwill? A man often wishes well to people he has never seen but supposes to be good or useful, and one of those people may feel the same way about him. These two are plainly well-wishers of one another — but who could call them friends, when neither knows how the other feels?

To be friends, then, two people must wish each other's good — from one of the three motives named above — and each must be aware of the other's feelings.

Chapter 3

But these three motives differ from one another in kind; therefore the affections and friendships based on them will differ in kind too. The kinds of friendship, accordingly, are three — equal in number to the motives of love; for any one of the three can be the basis of a mutual affection of which each party is aware.

Now, those who love one another wish each other's good with respect to the very thing that is the motive of their love. So those whose love for one another rests on the useful do not love each other for what they are, but only insofar as each gets some good from the other.

It is the same with those whose affection rests on pleasure: people are fond of a wit, for instance, not for what he is, but as a source of pleasure to themselves.

Those who love on the ground of the useful, then, care for each other for the sake of their own good, and those who love on the ground of pleasure care for each other for the sake of what pleases them — each loving the other not as being the man he is, but as useful or pleasant.

These friendships, then, are "accidental"; for the person is loved not as being the person and character he is, but as a source of some good or some pleasure. Friendships of this kind are easily dissolved, since the parties do not stay the same: when they cease to be pleasant or useful to each other, their love ceases. And the useful is nothing permanent — it shifts from one time to another. So when the motive of the friendship disappears, the friendship dissolves with it, since it existed only with a view to that motive.

Friendship of this kind seems to be found especially among the elderly (for at that age men pursue the useful rather than the pleasant), and among those middle-aged and young men who keep a sharp eye on profit. Friends of this sort do not generally even live in each other's company; sometimes they are not even pleasant to each other — and they have no need of such constant company unless it pays. They make themselves pleasant exactly as far as they have hopes of getting something good out of it.

With these friendships people generally class the kind that exists between host and guest.

The friendship of young men is thought to rest on pleasure. Young men live by impulse, and mostly pursue what is pleasant to them here and now. But as they grow older, the things that please them change. That is why they are quick to make friendships and quick to drop them: the friendship changes along with the thing that pleases, and pleasure of that sort turns over fast.

Young men are also prone to falling in love; for erotic love is mostly a matter of impulse and rests on pleasure. So they fall in love, and soon fall out of it, passing from one state to the other many times in a single day.

Friends of this kind do want to spend their days together and live together; for that is how they get what their friendship is for.

But the perfect kind of friendship is the friendship of good men who are alike in virtue. Each alike wishes the other well as a good man, and each is a good man in his own essential character. And those who wish well to their friends for the friends' own sake are friends in the truest sense; for they feel this way toward each other because of what each is, not by accident. So their friendship lasts as long as their virtue lasts — and virtue is a lasting thing.

Again, each of them is both good simply and good for his friend; for good men are both good without qualification and useful to one another.

In the same way they are pleasant too; for good men are pleasant both in themselves and to each other. Every character delights in the acts that are proper to it and in acts of the same kind, and the acts of good men are the same or similar.

A friendship like this, as we should expect, is lasting, since it unites in itself every condition of true friendship. Every friendship has as its motive some good or some pleasure — whether in itself, or relative to the one who loves — and rests on some likeness. But in this case everything required belongs to the friends in their own nature: here there is likeness, and the rest — what is good simply and pleasant simply — and these are the most lovable things of all. So it is between people of this sort that love and friendship are found in their truest and best form.

Naturally, such friendships are rare, because such people are rare. And a friendship of this kind needs time and long acquaintance besides. As the proverb has it, people cannot know one another until they have eaten the requisite amount of salt together. Nor can they accept one another as friends, or be friends, until each has shown himself to the other, and been approved, as worthy of love. Those who are quick to treat each other as friends may wish to be friends, but they are not really friends unless they are lovable and know each other to be so. The wish for friendship can spring up quickly; friendship cannot.

This kind of friendship, then, is complete — complete in duration and in every other respect; and in it each gets from the other the same or nearly the same as he gives, which is how it should be between friends.

Chapter 4

The friendship whose motive is pleasure bears some resemblance to this one; for good men, too, are pleasant to each other. So does the friendship whose motive is the useful; for good men are useful to one another as well. And in these cases too, the friendship is most likely to last when what each gets from the other is the same — pleasure, say — and not only the same, but drawn from the same source: a friendship between two wits, for instance, rather than one between a lover and his beloved. For in the latter case the pleasure does not come from the same source for both: the lover delights in gazing at his beloved, while the beloved likes receiving attentions. And when the bloom of youth fades, the friendship sometimes vanishes with it — the one no longer finds the beauty that pleased him, the other no longer gets the attentions. Yet they often do remain friends: namely, when their time together has brought them to care for each other's characters, and their characters are alike.

Those who in love affairs exchange not pleasure but profit are friends in a lesser degree, and less lastingly. The friendship whose motive is profit ends when the advantage ends; for it was never each other that they loved, but the profit.

For pleasure or for profit, then, even bad men can be friends with one another, and good men with bad, and men who are neither with anyone at all. But clearly only good men can be friends for each other's own sake; for bad men take no delight in one another unless there is something to be gained.

The friendship of good men is also the only one that is proof against slander; for a man is not quick to believe anyone's word against a friend he has tested for himself over the years. Between such friends there is trust, and the certainty that neither would ever wrong the other, and everything else that true friendship demands. In the other kinds, there is no such security.

Now, since men do apply the name "friends" to those who love each other for profit's sake — as states do, for expediency is thought to be the ground on which states form alliances — and also to those who love each other for pleasure's sake, as children do, perhaps we too should call such people friends, and say that there are several kinds of friendship. First, friendship in the primary and strict sense: the friendship of good men as good men. Second, the other kinds, which are called friendships by resemblance to this one; for those people are friends insofar as their relation involves some element of good, and that is the resemblance — the pleasant, after all, is a good to those who love pleasure. But these two lesser kinds do not often coincide: the same people do not usually become friends for the sake of profit and of pleasure both, since accidental properties are not often combined in one subject.

Now that we have sorted friendship into these kinds, we can say: bad men will be friends for pleasure or profit, being alike in that respect, while good men, when they are friends, love each other for what they are — that is, as good men. Good men, then, are friends simply; the others are friends accidentally, and by resemblance to them.

Chapter 5

Just as with the virtues we distinguish the settled character or capacity from its exercise, so it is with friendship. When friends are living in each other's company, they take pleasure in each other and do each other good; when they are asleep, or far apart, they are not exercising their friendship, but they still have the disposition that would issue in friendly acts if it were exercised. Distance does not dissolve friendship itself, only its exercise. But if the absence lasts long, it is thought to wear away even the friendship itself. Hence the saying:

  Full many a friendship has been loosed
  for want of talk together.

Old men do not seem apt for friendship, nor do sour-tempered men; there is little in them to give pleasure, and no one can spend his days in the company of what is painful, or even of what fails to please. For our nature seems to shun the painful and seek the pleasant above all things.

Those who approve of each other but do not live in each other's company seem to be well-wishers rather than friends. Nothing is so characteristic of friendship as living together. The needy seek help that way; but even the happy desire company — a solitary life suits them least of all men. And people cannot spend their lives together unless they are pleasant to each other and delight in the same things, which seems to be what comradeship requires.

The truest friendship, then, is the friendship of good men, as we have said again and again. For what is lovable and desirable, it seems, is what is good or pleasant in itself; but to each man, what is good or pleasant for him — and the friendship of good men for each other rests on both grounds at once.

But it seems that while love is a feeling, friendship is a habit. For love can just as well be directed at inanimate things, but the love of friends for one another involves choice, and choice proceeds from a habit. And when friends wish well to those they love for their own sakes, they are moved not by feeling but by habit. Again, in loving a friend, each loves what is good for himself; for whoever gains a good man as a friend gains a good for himself. So each loves what is good for himself, and each gives back the equal of what he gets, in good wishes and in pleasure. For, as the old saying joins them, "friendship is equality" — and both are found in the highest degree in the friendship of good men.

Chapter 6

Sour-tempered men and old men are the less apt for friendship the harsher their temper is and the less pleasure they take in company; for delight in company seems to be, more than anything else, the mark of friendship and the thing that produces it. That is why the young make friends quickly and the old do not — people do not make friends with those who give them no pleasure — and neither do the sour-tempered. Such men may indeed be well-wishers: they wish each other good, and help each other in need. But they are hardly friends, since they do not spend their days together or delight in each other — and those, more than anything else, are thought to be the marks of friendship.

It is impossible to be a friend, in the full sense, to many people at the same time, just as it is impossible to be in love with many people at once. Friendship of that sort is something intense, and intense feeling takes a single object. Besides, it is not easy for one man to find many people at once who deeply please him — perhaps not many good ones either. And the friends must test each other and grow used to each other, which is a very hard thing to accomplish. In the way of profit or pleasure, on the other hand, it is quite possible to please many people; men of that sort are not rare, and their services take little time.

Of these lesser kinds, the one that comes nearer true friendship is the friendship for pleasure, when each renders the same to the other and both take pleasure in each other, or in the same things — the friendships of the young are typically like this, for a generous spirit is commoner in the young. The friendship whose motive is the useful, by contrast, is the friendship of petty traders. The happy have no need of useful friends, but they do need pleasant ones: what they want is people to live with. They may put up with what is painful for a little while, but no one could endure anything continually if it pained him — not even the good itself. So they require their friends to be pleasant. But they ought, we may add, to require them to be good as well as pleasant — and good for them; then everything a friend should be will be combined in one person.

Men in high places seem to keep their friends in separate compartments. Some of their friends are useful and others are pleasant, but rarely is the same man both; for they are not looking for people who are at once agreeable and virtuous, or people useful for noble ends. When they want pleasure they seek out wits, and for everything else they choose men who are clever at carrying out orders — and those two qualities are seldom found in one person.

The good man, as we have said, is both pleasant and useful at once. But such a man does not become the friend of a man above him in station unless the superior is also superior in virtue; otherwise the good man cannot establish proportionate equality — inferior in one respect exactly as he is superior in another. And great men are not much in the habit of conceding this.

In the friendships discussed so far, the parties are equals: they do the same for each other and wish the same for each other, or else they exchange equal amounts of different things — pleasure for profit, say. (We have already explained that these last are friendships in a lesser degree, and less lasting. Being at once like true friendship and unlike it, they seem both to be and not to be friendships. Insofar as they resemble the friendship based on virtue — the one involving pleasure and the other profit, both of which true friendship contains — they seem to be friendships; but insofar as true friendship is proof against slander and lasting, while they change quickly and differ in many other ways, they seem not to be friendships at all. The unlikeness shows.)

Chapter 7

But besides these there is another kind of friendship — friendship between unequals: of a father for a son, and generally of an older person for a younger, of a man for a woman, of a ruler of any kind for a subject.

These, too, differ among themselves. The friendship of parent for child is not the same as that of ruler for subject; nor, for that matter, is the friendship of father for son the same as that of son for father, nor that of man for woman the same as that of woman for man. For each of these persons has a different virtue and a different function, and the grounds of their affection differ; so their love and their friendship differ too. What each does for the other, then, is not the same, and they should not expect it to be the same. But when children give their parents what is owed to those who gave them life, and parents give their children what is owed to children, then the friendship between them will be lasting, and what it ought to be. And in every friendship between unequals, the love on each side should be proportional: the better of the two — and the more useful, and so on in each case — should receive more love than he gives. For when love is proportioned to desert, a sort of equality is established; and equality seems to be a necessary condition of friendship.

But equality does not seem to work the same way in friendship as it does in justice. In the sphere of justice, "equal" means first of all "proportionate to merit," and "equal in quantity" only secondarily; in friendship it is the reverse — "equal in quantity" comes first, "proportionate to merit" second.

You can see this plainly wherever a great gap opens between two people in virtue, or vice, or wealth, or anything else: they no longer are friends, and no longer even expect to be. It is plainest of all in the case of the gods, whose superiority in every good thing is greatest. But it shows in the case of kings as well: men far beneath them do not claim their friendship, nor do men of no account expect to be friends with the best and wisest in the state. There is no drawing an exact line in such cases, no fixing the point up to which friendship can survive. Much can be taken away and the friendship remain; but when one party is removed to a vast distance — as God is — it can no longer exist.

This has prompted an objection: that in the end a friend does not really wish his friend the greatest of all goods — namely, to become a god — for then he would lose him as a friend, and so lose a good; for a friend is a good thing. If, then, we were right to say that a friend wishes his friend's good for the friend's own sake, we must add: "the friend remaining what he is." He will wish him the greatest goods a man can have — though perhaps not all of them; for every man wishes good most of all to himself.

Chapter 8

Most people, out of a desire for honor, seem to want to be loved rather than to love; and that is why most men are fond of flatterers. For the flatterer is a friend in an inferior position — or pretends to be one, and pretends to love more than he is loved; and being loved is thought to come close to being honored, which is what most men are striving for.

But it seems they desire honor not for its own sake, but incidentally. Most men delight in being honored by those in power because of what they expect: they hope to get from them whatever they happen to want, and so they enjoy the honor as a token of good things to come. Those, on the other hand, who want the respect of good men, of men who know, are seeking to confirm their own opinion of themselves; they take joy in the assurance of their worth, trusting the judgment of those who declare it.

Being loved, however, men delight in for its own sake. It would seem to follow that being loved is better than being honored, and that friendship is desirable in itself.

But friendship seems to lie more in loving than in being loved. The proof is the delight mothers take in loving. Some mothers give their children to others to bring up, and love them, knowing them for their own, without seeking love in return, when they cannot have both — content to see the children doing well, and loving them even though the children, in their ignorance, give back nothing of what is due a mother.

Since friendship lies more in loving than in being loved, and since we praise those who love their friends, it seems that loving is the virtue of friends. So when people love each other in proportion to their worth, they are lasting friends, and theirs is a lasting friendship.

This is also how unequals can be friends most truly; for in this way they are made equal. And equality and likeness are what make friendship — above all the likeness of those who are alike in virtue. For such men, being little liable to change, stay as they were, in themselves and toward each other; they never ask anything unworthy of one another, or do anything unworthy for one another — rather, they hold each other back from anything of the sort. For it is the mark of a good man neither to go wrong himself nor to let his friend go wrong.

Bad men, as friends, have no such steadiness; they do not even stay like themselves. They become friends for a short spell, delighting in each other's wickedness.

Friends who are useful or pleasant to each other last longer than that: as long, that is, as they keep supplying pleasure or profit.

The friendship whose motive is the useful seems, more than any other kind, to be a union of opposites — rich and poor, ignorant and learned; for when a man wants something, he will give something else in exchange to get it. And perhaps we could pull the lover and his beloved, the beautiful person and the ugly one, into this class. This is why lovers sometimes make themselves ridiculous by demanding to be loved as they love. If they were equally lovable, the claim might stand; but when there is nothing lovable about them, it is absurd.

But perhaps nothing desires its opposite as such — only accidentally, the desire being really for the mean between the two, since that is what is good. For the dry, for instance, the good is not to become wet, but to reach the intermediate state; and so with the hot, and the rest of these opposites. But let us set these questions aside; they are, in truth, somewhat foreign to our present purpose.

Chapter 9

It seems, as we said at the outset, that friendship and justice have the same field and arise on the same occasions. Every community, every association, gives some occasion for justice — and some occasion for friendship too. At any rate, people speak of their partners on a voyage or in a campaign as friends, and the same with partners in other associations. And just as far as the partnership extends, so far extends the friendship; for so far extends the justice as well.

Moreover, "friends' goods are common property," as the proverb says — and says rightly, for friendship implies community. Brothers and close comrades have everything in common; other friends have certain definite things in common, some more and some less — for friendships, too, differ in degree. And what justice requires differs in the same way. It does not demand the same from parents toward children as from brothers toward one another, nor the same from comrades as from fellow-citizens, and so on through the other kinds of friendship.

Injustice, too, takes different forms in these different relations, and it grows more serious as the friendship grows closer. It is a grosser wrong to rob a comrade than a fellow-citizen, to refuse help to a brother than to a stranger, to strike your father than to strike any other man. The claims of justice, in fact, naturally increase as friendship increases; the two cover the same ground and grow step for step together.

But all associations, all communities, seem to be — so to speak — parts of the political community, the association of citizens. For in every one of them, people join together with a view to some common interest, in pursuit of one or another of the things they need for life. But the association of citizens seems both to have been founded, and to endure, for the sake of the common interest as a whole; for this is what lawgivers aim at, and what serves the common interest of all is called just.

The other associations, then, aim at some particular advantage. Sailors work together for a successful voyage, with a view to making money or something of the kind; soldiers for a successful campaign, whether the further goal is riches, or victory, or the founding of a state; and so with the members of a tribe or a district. Other associations seem to aim at pleasure — people joining together for a feast or a club dinner, where the whole point is the food and the company. But all of these stand subordinate to the association of citizens, for that association aims not at the interest of the moment but at the interest of our whole life — even when its members hold festivals and gatherings on such occasions, honoring the gods and providing rest and amusement for themselves. For the ancient festivals and assemblies seem to have come after the harvest was gathered in — a kind of dedication of the firstfruits — since those were the seasons when people had the most leisure.

All associations, then, seem to be parts of the association of citizens; and the several kinds of friendship will correspond to the several kinds of association.

Chapter 10

Now, of constitutions there are three kinds, and an equal number of perverted forms — corruptions, so to speak, of the three. The true constitutions are kingly government and aristocracy; and third, there is a form of government based on a property assessment, which strictly ought to be called timocracy, though most people simply call it constitutional government.

Of these, kingly government is the best and timocracy the worst. The perversion of kingly government is tyranny. Both are monarchies, but there is a vast difference between them: the tyrant seeks his own interest, the king the interest of his subjects. For a man is not properly a king unless he is self-sufficient and supplied with every good thing in overflowing measure; such a man wants nothing more, so his aim will not be his own advantage but his subjects'. A king of any other character would be a mere king by lot.

Tyranny is the opposite of kingly rule, because the tyrant pursues his own good. And of tyranny it is quite obvious that it is the worst of all — for the opposite of the best must be the worst.

Kingly government degenerates into tyranny; for tyranny is monarchy gone bad, and the bad king becomes a tyrant.

Aristocracy degenerates into oligarchy through the vice of the rulers — who, instead of distributing public property and honors according to merit, take all or most of the good things for themselves, keep the offices always in the same hands, and set the greatest store by wealth. You then have a small number of bad men in power instead of the best men.

Lastly, timocracy degenerates into democracy — and indeed the two border closely on each other; for even timocracy is meant to be rule by the many, and everyone who meets the property qualification counts as equal.

Democracy is the least bad of the corrupt forms, for it departs only slightly from the constitution it corrupts.

These, then, are the changes to which the constitutions are most liable; for these are the smallest shifts, and the ones that meet the least resistance.

Likenesses of these forms of government — patterns of them, so to speak — can be found in families. The association of a father and his sons has the form of kingly rule, for the father cares for his children. That is why Homer addresses Zeus as father; for kingly government aims at being a fatherly government. In Persia, however, the relation of father to son is tyrannical, for fathers there use their sons as slaves. The relation of master to slave is tyrannical too, since it is the master's interest that it secures. But this seems a legitimate kind of tyranny, while the Persian kind seems wrong; for different beings require different kinds of rule.

The association of husband and wife seems to be aristocratic. The husband rules in proportion to his worth — that is, in the matters that are his province — and entrusts to his wife the matters that properly belong to her. But when the man lords it over everything, he perverts the relation into an oligarchic one; for then he takes rule where he has no title to it, and not only where he is the better. Sometimes, on the other hand, the wife rules, because she is an heiress. In such cases authority rests not on merit but on wealth and influence — just as in oligarchies.

The association of brothers resembles a timocracy, for they are equal except so far as they differ in age. That is why, if they differ very widely in age, their friendship can no longer be a brotherly one.

A democratic form of association is found chiefly in households that have no master — for there everyone stands on an equal footing — or where the head of the house is weak and everyone does as he likes.

Chapter 11

In each of these forms of government, friendship has a place exactly as far as justice does. The king shows his friendship for his subjects by the surpassing benefits he confers: being good, he does good to his subjects, and tends them with a view to their welfare as a shepherd tends his sheep — which is why Homer calls Agamemnon "shepherd of peoples."

The friendship of a father for his child is of the same kind, though the benefits conferred are greater still. For the father is the author of the child's very existence — which seems the greatest benefit of all — and of his nurture and education; and we credit these to our forefathers generally. Thus it is according to nature that fathers rule their children, forefathers their descendants, and kings their subjects.

These friendships involve the superiority of one side over the other; and that is why parents receive honor as well as service. What justice requires here is not the same on both sides, but what is proportionate to each party's worth — for that is the rule of friendship as well as of justice.

The friendship of husband and wife, again, is the same as the friendship found in an aristocracy: each benefits in proportion to merit, the better getting the greater good and each what is fitting — which is the rule of justice too.

The friendship of brothers resembles that of comrades, for they are equal and of like age — and people of that sort generally have the same feelings and the same character. The friendship in a timocracy is of this same type; for the citizens there mean to be equal and fair, so they take office in turn and share it equally, and their friendship follows the same rule.

In the corrupt forms, just as there is little room for justice, so there is little room for friendship — and least of all in the worst: in a tyranny there is little or no friendship. For where ruler and subject have nothing in common, there can be no friendship, just as there can be no justice — no more than between a workman and his tools, or the soul and the body, or master and slave. Tools, body, and slave are all benefited by those who use them; but our relations with inanimate things admit of no friendship and no justice — nor do our relations with a horse or an ox, nor with a slave as slave. For master and slave have nothing in common: the slave is a living tool, and the tool is a lifeless slave. As a slave, then, the master's relations with him admit of no friendship; but as a man, they may. For there seems to be room for some kind of justice between any man and any being that can share in law and contract — and if for justice, then for some kind of friendship too, just so far as the other deserves the name of man.

And so friendship and justice are found to some small extent even in tyrannies; but they are found to a greater extent in democracies than in any other of the corrupt forms, for there the citizens are equal and have many things in common.

Chapter 12

All friendship, as we have said, implies association; but we may set apart from the rest the friendship of kinsmen and that of comrades. The friendships of fellow-citizens, fellow-tribesmen, fellow-sailors, and the like look, by contrast, more like association pure and simple, for they seem to rest on a kind of compact. With these we might also class the friendship of host and guest.

The friendship of kinsmen seems to include several species, but all of them appear to depend on the friendship of parent and child. Parents love their children as part of themselves; children love their parents as the source of their being. But parents know their children better than the children know that these are their parents; and the one who gives life is more closely attached to what springs from him than the offspring is to its maker. For what proceeds from us belongs to us — as a tooth or a hair or anything of the sort belongs to its owner — but we do not belong to it at all, or belong to it in a lesser degree.

There is a difference in time as well: parents love their offspring from the moment of birth, but children come to love their parents only after time has passed, when they have acquired understanding, or at least perception.

These considerations also show why mothers love their children more than fathers do.

Parents, then, love their children as themselves — for what proceeds from us is, as it were, a second self, a self set apart — while children love their parents as the source of their being, and brothers love one another because they spring from the same source. Their common relation to that source makes an identity between them; hence the sayings "the same blood," "the same stock," and so on. They are, in a way, the same thing in separate persons.

Friendship between brothers is greatly furthered by being brought up together and by nearness in age; for those of the same age naturally take to one another, as the saying goes, and those who are used to one another naturally make comrades of one another — which is why the friendship of brothers comes to resemble the friendship of comrades.

Cousins and other kinsfolk are attached to one another for the same reason — namely, that they come of the same stock; and the attachment is closer or looser according as the founder of the family is nearer or more remote.

The friendship of children for their parents — like that of men for the gods — is friendship for what is good and superior to oneself: for the source of the greatest benefits, of life itself, of nurture, of education from birth onward.

Friendship of this kind brings more of both pleasure and profit than the friendship of strangers, in proportion as the life shared is greater.

The friendship of brothers has all the marks of the friendship of comrades, and has them in a greater degree — provided the brothers are good, and generally alike — inasmuch as they belong more to each other and have loved each other from birth; they are more alike in character, being of the same stock, brought up together, and educated alike; and they have had the longest and surest experience of one another.

In all the other friendships of kinship, the same elements will be found in proportion to the closeness of the tie.

The friendship of husband and wife seems to be natural; for human beings are by nature more inclined to join in couples than to form states — since the family is earlier than the state and more indispensable, and the propagation of the species is more fundamental to animal life. The other animals associate for this purpose alone; but husband and wife live together not merely to have children but to supply the needs of life. For their functions are divided and distinct from the start: they supply each other's wants, each contributing to the common stock. And so this kind of friendship is thought to bring both pleasure and profit with it. But it will rest on virtue too, if the two are good; for each has a virtue of their own, and each will delight in the goodness that answers it.

Children, moreover, seem to be a bond that knits husband and wife together — which is why childless unions dissolve more quickly. For children are a good that both have in common, and what people have in common holds them together.

To ask on what terms a man should live with his wife — and, in general, friend with friend — is the same as asking what justice requires in each case; for what is required of a man toward a friend is not what is required toward a stranger, a comrade, or a fellow-student.

Chapter 13

There are three kinds of friendship, as we said at the outset; and within each kind there are friendships between equals and friendships between unequals. Sometimes two equally good men become friends, sometimes a better man and a worse; and so with those who are pleasant to each other, and with those whose friendship aims at profit — sometimes their services are equal, sometimes not.

Now, those who are equal should establish equality by loving each other, and doing everything else, equally; those who are unequal should establish it by making what each renders proportionate to the other's greater or lesser merit.

But accusations and reproaches arise solely, or mostly, in friendships whose motive is profit — as we should expect. Those whose friendship rests on virtue are eager to do good to one another (for that is the office of virtue and of friendship), and between people who are rivals in that, no accusations or quarrels can arise. No one is embittered against a man who loves him and does him good; if he has any grace in him, he pays the service back with a like service. And the one who outdoes the other will not reproach his friend, since he is getting exactly what he desires — for what each desires is the good.

Nor do such quarrels readily arise in friendships whose motive is pleasure; for both get what they want at the same moment, if they delight in each other's company. And a man who accused his friend of not being agreeable would only make himself ridiculous, since he was under no compulsion to keep the company.

But the friendship whose motive is utility is fruitful in accusations. Since the parties use each other purely for their own advantage, each always wants the larger share, each thinks he has less than his due, and each reproaches the other for not doing as much for him as he needs and deserves — though in truth the one doing the service can never supply all that the other wants.

Now it seems that, just as the rules of justice are of two kinds — the unwritten, and those set down in law — so the friendship of utility is of two kinds: one moral, resting on character and trust, the other legal, resting on stated terms. And accusations arise most readily when the relation is understood in one sense at the beginning and in the other at the end.

The legal kind is the kind with specified conditions, and it has two forms. One is purely commercial, on the principle of cash on delivery; the other allows more time, but still stipulates a definite return for a definite service. In this second form what is owed is plain and cannot be disputed, but the delay in payment carries an element of friendliness — which is why in some states there is no legal recovery in such cases: it is held that a man who gives credit must take the consequences.

The moral kind has no specified conditions: a man gives a present, or whatever it may be, as one friend to another. But afterward he claims as much back, or more — treating what he gave not as a gift but as a loan. And since he wants to end the relation in a different spirit from the one in which he entered it, he will accuse the other. This happens because all men, or nearly all, wish for what is noble but choose what is profitable; and while it is noble to do a good turn without expecting a return, it is profitable to be on the receiving end.

In such cases, then, we should make an equivalent return for what we received, if we have the power — for we must not hold a man as a friend against his will. We should recognize that we made a mistake at the start and accepted a benefit from the wrong person: he was no friend and was not acting for the deed's own sake, so we should close the relation just as if we had received a service on stated terms. And what we should return is what we would have agreed to repay had we been able; if we had been unable, the giver himself would not have expected repayment. So we may fairly say: repay if you can.

But at the very outset we ought to consider carefully who is doing us the service, and on what understanding — so that we can accept it on that understanding, or else decline it.

It is a debatable question whether the return should be measured by the value of the service to the receiver or by its cost to the giver. Receivers tend to say that what they got was a small matter to their benefactors and could just as well have been had from others — talking the service down. Givers say the opposite: that it was the best they had, that it could not have been had from anyone else, and that it was given in a moment of danger or some other pressing need.

Perhaps we may settle it this way: if profit is the friendship's motive, the benefit to the receiver is the measure. He is the one who asked for the service, and the other renders it expecting an equal return; so the amount of the assistance is fixed by the extent of the benefit, and the receiver should pay back as much as he got — or even more, for that would be the nobler course.

In friendships based on virtue, on the other hand, accusations of this sort do not occur; there, the measure of the service would seem to be the giver's choice — the purpose behind the deed. For virtue and moral character lie in choice.

Chapter 14

Quarrels occur in unequal friendships too; for sometimes each party claims the larger share — and when that happens, the friendship dissolves. The better of the two thinks he ought to have more: "the larger share belongs to the good man," he says. The more useful of the two makes the same claim: "everyone agrees," he says, "that a useless person should not share equally; friendship turns into unpaid charity unless what each gets out of it is proportionate to what he puts in." Such people imagine that the same rule should hold in friendship as in a business partnership, where those who invest more take the larger share.

The needy man and the inferior man argue the opposite way: "it is precisely a good friend's business," they say, "to help you when you are in need. What is the use of being friends with a good man or a powerful man if you are to get nothing by it?"

It seems that both claims are right — that each ought to receive the larger share, but not of the same things. The superior should get more honor, the needy man more profit; for honor is the tribute due to virtue and to doing good, while need finds its proper relief in material gain.

This is recognized in constitutions as well. No honor is paid to the man who contributes nothing to the common stock of good; what is common is distributed among those who benefit the community, and honor is part of what is common. A man cannot make money off the community and expect to be honored by it too; no one is content to take the smaller share of everything. So to the man who spends his money on public objects we pay honor, and to the man whose services can be paid in money we pay money; for by giving each what is proportionate to his merit, equality is established and friendship preserved — as we said before.

The same principles, then, must govern the dealings of unequal individuals. He who is benefited by another — in his purse or in his character — must give honor in return, repaying with what it is in his power to give. For friendship asks for what is possible, not for what is strictly owed; what is owed is sometimes impossible to pay, as with the honor owed to the gods and to parents. No one could ever pay that debt in full; but the man who serves them as well as he is able is held to fulfill his obligation.

For this reason it would seem that a son may not disown his father, though a father may disown his son. A debtor must pay; and nothing a son can do will ever make full return for what he has received, so he remains forever in debt. But a creditor is free to release his debtor — and so a father is free to cast off his son. At the same time, it seems unlikely that anyone would ever actually disown a son unless the son were an utter scoundrel; for natural affection aside, it is only human not to push away the support a son would bring. The son, though — if he is a scoundrel — will look for ways to avoid helping his father, or at least will not be eager to; for most men are glad enough to receive benefits, but avoid conferring them as unprofitable.

So much, then, for these questions.

Book IX

Friendship or Love—Continued

Chapter 1

In all friendships between unlike partners, it is proportionate exchange that keeps things equal and keeps the friendship alive — we have said this already. It works just as it does in the association of citizens, where the shoemaker gets, in exchange for his shoes, a return proportionate to what they are worth, and so with the weaver and everyone else.

In those civic cases, a common measure is ready to hand: money. Money is the standard everything is referred to and measured against.

In erotic friendships, though, there is no such standard, and so the lover sometimes complains that he loves intensely and gets no love back — when, perhaps, there is simply nothing lovable about him. And often the beloved complains that the lover once promised everything and now delivers nothing.

Complaints like these tend to arise when one party is in the friendship for pleasure and the other for profit, and neither is getting what he wants. A friendship built on those motives dissolves the moment the partners stop getting the very thing they became friends for; neither one loved the other's self — each loved only something the other had, and something not built to last. Which is why these friendships are not built to last either. But friendship based on character is its own reward, and so, as we said, it tends to endure.

Friends also quarrel when they find they are getting something different from what they wanted — for when you fail to get what you want, it is as if you got nothing at all. There is a story about a harper that makes the point. A man promised him that the better he played, the more he would be paid; but when dawn came and the harper claimed what was promised, the man replied that he had already paid — pleasure for pleasure. Now, if pleasure was what both of them wanted, that would settle it. But if one wanted pleasure and the other wanted profit, and one has what he wants while the other does not, the bargain has not been fairly carried out; for what a man happens to want is what he sets his heart on, and it is for the sake of that — and only that — that he agrees to render his particular service.

But who should set the value of the service — the one who gives first, or the one who receives first? The one who gives first seems to leave the pricing to the other. They say this was the practice of Protagoras: after teaching a subject, he would tell the pupil to estimate what the knowledge was worth to him, and that is the fee he would take.

Some prefer the rule of the proverb: "Let a friend be content with his stated wage."

But a man who takes his money up front and then delivers nothing — because he promised more than he could perform — is rightly held accountable; he has not fulfilled his contract. (The sophists, perhaps, are forced to take payment in advance, since nobody would pay for what they know once they had heard it.)

So: whoever fails to do what he has already been paid for is rightly charged with it. But where there is no explicit agreement about the service, two cases arise. (a) When one man helps another freely, for that other's own sake, no accusation can arise — as we said, that is the very nature of friendship based on virtue. Here the return should be measured by the purpose of the man who did the first service, for purpose is the mark of both the friend and virtue. The same rule seems to apply between a philosopher and his students: the worth of what he gives cannot be measured in money, and no honor could fully repay him; but here, as with gods and parents, what is possible is accepted as enough. (b) If, however, the first gift was made not in that spirit but on the understanding that there would be a return, then the return should, ideally, be one that both parties agree is proportionate to what was given. And if they cannot agree, it seems not only necessary but just that the one who received the first benefit should set its value: whatever advantage he actually got from it, or whatever he would have paid for the pleasure of it, that amount repays the giver in full. This is plainly how it works in sales; and in some states the law gives no remedy at all in voluntary contracts, on the principle that once you have given a man credit, you must finish the bargain in the same spirit in which you began it. It is held fairer for the service to be valued by the man who was trusted than by the man who trusted him. For most things are valued differently by those who have them and those who want them: what belongs to us, and what we give away, always looks precious to us. All the same, the return must be measured by the value the receiver sets on the service — though he ought, perhaps, to price it not at what it seems worth now that he has it, but at the value he put on it before he had it.

Chapter 2

Some further questions come up here. Should a father's claims on his son be unlimited, so that the son obeys him in everything — or should a sick son obey the doctor, and should a citizen electing a general choose the man skilled in war? Similarly: should you help your friend rather than a good man, and repay a benefactor rather than do a favor for a comrade, if you cannot do both?

Perhaps we may say that laying down precise rules for all such cases is scarcely possible; the cases differ in every possible way — in how much is at stake, and in whether the act is noble or merely necessary. But this much is fairly clear: no single person's claims override everyone else's; and as a general rule we should repay benefits received before doing favors for comrades — just as, if we had borrowed money, we ought to pay our creditors before giving presents to our friends.

But even this rule may not hold in every case. Suppose a man has been ransomed from bandits. Should he in turn ransom his ransomer, whoever that may be — or repay him when he asks, even though he has not been captured — in preference to ransoming his own father? It would seem a man ought to ransom his father even before himself.

So, as we said: generally speaking, we should repay what we owe; but if giving instead is nobler, or meets a more pressing need, we should lean that way. Sometimes it is not even fair to repay the original service — for instance, when one man helped another knowing him to be good, while the repayment would mean helping a man he believes to be bad. Sometimes, likewise, you are not bound to lend in turn to a man who once lent to you: A may have lent to B fully expecting repayment, because B is honorable, while B has no hope of ever being repaid by A, who is a scoundrel. If that is the true state of things, A's demand for a return loan is not fair; and even if the facts are otherwise, so long as they think this of each other, their conduct will seem natural enough.

As we have said many times, statements about human feelings and actions can only be as definite as their subject matter allows.

It is fairly plain, then, that on the one hand not everyone's claims are the same, and on the other hand the father's claims do not override all others — just as Zeus does not receive all our sacrifices. The claims of parents, brothers, comrades, and benefactors are all different, and to each we must render what is properly his own and his due.

And this is how people actually behave. To a wedding they invite their kin, for kin share in the family and so in everything that concerns it; and for the same reason it is thought that kin have first claim to be invited to funerals.

Parents would seem to have a special claim on us for their support: we owe it to them, and it is nobler to sustain the lives of those who gave us our own than to sustain ourselves.

Honor, too, we should pay our parents, as we do the gods — but not every kind of honor. The honor due a father is not the same as the honor due a mother, nor do we owe either of them the honor due a wise man or a good general, but precisely a father's honor and a mother's honor.

To all our elders, again, we should pay the honor their age is due — rising when they enter, giving them the place of honor at the table, and so on. Between comrades and brothers, on the other hand, there should be frank speech and everything in common. And to kin, to fellow tribesmen, to fellow citizens, and to everyone else, we should always try to give what is due — assigning to each what properly belongs to him, according to how close he is to us and how good or useful he is. When the people involved are of one kind, the assignment is fairly easy; when they are of different kinds, it is harder. But that is no reason to shirk the task; we must draw the distinctions as best we can.

Chapter 3

Another hard question: should we or should we not break off friendship with people who are no longer what they were?

Where the friendship was based on profit or pleasure, perhaps there is nothing puzzling: when those give out, the friends naturally part. It was the profit or the pleasure they loved; when that is gone, it is only to be expected that the love goes too. But complaints are likely if a man who loved another for profit or pleasure pretended to love him for his character. As we said at the outset, quarrels between friends most often arise when the real basis of the friendship is not what it was supposed to be. If a man deceives himself — assuming he is loved for his character when the other's behavior gives no ground for it — he has only himself to blame. But if he is taken in by the other's pretense, he has fair ground for complaint against the impostor — more, even, than against a counterfeiter of the coinage, since what has been tampered with is more precious.

But suppose you take a man into your friendship as a good man, and he becomes — and shows himself to be — a bad one. Should you still love him? Surely that is impossible: not everything is lovable, only the good. What is bad is not lovable and should not be loved; we ought not to love what is bad, nor make ourselves like what is worthless — and as we said before, like makes friends with like.

Should the friendship, then, be broken off at once? Perhaps not in every case — only where the badness is incurable. Where reform is possible, we are more bound to help a friend's character than his fortune, since character is the nobler thing and has more to do with friendship. Still, a man who does withdraw his friendship in such a case does nothing unnatural: it was not this man he made friends with. His friend has become someone else, and since he cannot restore him, he keeps his distance.

But suppose one friend stays as he was while the other improves and comes to far surpass him in virtue: should the better man still treat the other as a friend? It is hardly possible. We see this most plainly when the gap grows wide — take a boyhood friendship. If one of the two remains a child in understanding while the other has become a man in the fullest sense of the word, how can they still be friends, when the things that please them, the things they rejoice and grieve over, are no longer the same? They will not even agree in their tastes about each other; and without that, we found, people cannot be friends, for they cannot live together. (But this point has already been discussed.)

Shall we say, then, that the better man should treat his old friend as no more than a stranger, exactly as if they had never been friends? Perhaps we can go further: he should not entirely forget the old intimacy. Just as we hold that we should serve friends before strangers, so former friends retain some claim on us for the friendship that once was — unless it was extraordinary depravity that parted us.

Chapter 4

Our friendly relations toward others — all the marks by which friendship is defined — seem to be derived from our relations toward ourselves. A friend is sometimes described as one who wishes and does what is good, or seems good, for the other person's own sake; or as one who wishes his friend to exist and to live, for the friend's sake. (This is what mothers feel toward their children, and what quarreling friends still feel for one another.) Others describe a friend as one who spends his life with another and chooses what he chooses, or as one who shares his friend's griefs and joys. (This, too, is found above all in mothers.) And friendship is regularly defined by one or another of these marks.

Now, every one of these marks is found in the good man's relation to himself — and in other men just so far as they suppose themselves good; but, as we have said, virtue and the good man seem to be the standard in everything. For the good man is of one mind with himself: he desires the same things with his whole soul. He wishes for himself what is good, and what seems good, and he does it — for it is the mark of the good man to work out what is good — and he does it for his own sake, that is, for the sake of the rational part of him, which seems to be what a man's self really is. He wishes himself to live and be preserved, and above all the part of him with which he thinks; for to the good man, existence is a good. And it is for himself that each of us wishes what is good: no one would choose to have every good in the world (as, say, God is in complete possession of the good) on condition of becoming someone else — only on condition of remaining just who he is. And a man's reason would seem to be his self, or at least to be his self more truly than anything else in him.

Such a man also wishes to spend time with himself; his own company is pleasant to him. The memory of his past life is sweet, and his hopes for the future are good — and such hopes are pleasant. His mind is well stocked with matter for contemplation. And he shares his own griefs and joys: the same things pain and please him at every season, not one thing now and another thing later — he is, so to speak, not a man who changes his mind.

Since, then, all these marks are found in the good man's relation to himself, and since he stands to his friend as he stands to himself — for his friend is another self — friendship is defined by one or another of these marks, and those in whom they are found are called friends.

Whether friendship toward oneself is possible we may set aside for now; though it would seem possible insofar as a man is two or more selves — that follows from what has been said, and from the fact that the extreme of friendship for another is likened to friendship for oneself.

But the marks we have listed appear to be found in the general run of men, who are not good. Perhaps we should say that men share in them just so far as they are satisfied with themselves and believe themselves good. The utterly worthless and impious never have them — never even seem to. Indeed we might almost say flatly that no one who is not good has them; for such men are not at one with themselves. They desire one thing and wish another, like the weak-willed, who choose what is pleasant but harmful instead of what they themselves hold to be good. Others, through cowardice or laziness, shrink from doing what they believe is best for them. And those who have done many terrible things out of wickedness come to hate life, wish to be rid of it, and sometimes actually destroy themselves.

Bad men hunt for company to pass their time in and run from their own; alone, they remember much that is painful and expect more of the same, but other people's company distracts them from it. There is nothing lovable in them, so they have no friendly feeling toward themselves.

The man who is not good, then, cannot share his own griefs and joys, for his soul is divided against itself. One part of him, because of its viciousness, is pained at holding back from something while another part is pleased; one part pulls this way, another that, as if tearing him to pieces between them. Or if it really is impossible to be pained and pleased at the same moment, then at least a little while later he is pained that he was pleased, and wishes those pleasures had never come his way — for men who are not good are full of remorse.

So we may say flatly: the man who is not good has no friendly feeling even toward himself, since there is nothing lovable in him. And if to be in this state is utter misery, we ought to strain every nerve to avoid vice and to try to be good; for that is how a man comes to be a friend to himself, and comes to be a friend to others.

Chapter 5

Goodwill looks like friendship, but it is not friendship. We can have goodwill toward people we do not know, and who have no idea we wish them well — and there can be no friendship there. But this we have said already.

Nor is goodwill the same as affection, for it has none of the intensity and desire that go with affection.

Affection, moreover, requires close acquaintance, while goodwill can spring up in a moment — as it does toward athletes competing for a prize. We become well-wishers of a competitor and are eager for him to win, though we would not lift a finger to help him; for, as we said, our goodwill in such cases is sudden, and the fondness is only skin-deep.

The truth seems to be that goodwill is the germ of friendship, just as pleasure in the sight of a person is the germ of erotic love. No one falls in love without first being delighted by visible beauty; but delighting in someone's beauty is not yet being in love — not unless you also ache in his absence and long for his presence. In the same way, people cannot be friends without first becoming well-wishers, but well-wishers are not yet friends on that account: they merely wish good to those they favor, and would not actually help them in anything, or put themselves out for them. So one might say — stretching the term — that goodwill is friendship lying idle, which with time and close acquaintance may become friendship proper. Not the friendship whose motive is profit, though, nor the one whose motive is pleasure, for goodwill has no place in those. The man who has received a benefit does return goodwill to his benefactor, and justly so; but the man who wishes another prosperity in hopes of the good things he can get through him does not really wish the other well — he wishes himself well, just as a man is no real friend if his service has one eye on profit.

Generally speaking, goodwill is founded on some kind of excellence or goodness: it arises when someone strikes us as beautiful, or brave, or endowed with some other fine quality — as we said in the case of the athletes.

Chapter 6

Concord — unity of mind — also seems to be a mark of friendship. That is exactly why it is not mere agreement in opinion, which is possible even between total strangers.

Nor do we speak of concord when people agree in judgment on just any subject — on astronomy, say; being of one mind about that has nothing to do with friendship. We say concord prevails in a state when the citizens agree about what is in the common interest, choose the same course, and carry out what they have decided together. Concord, then, is about practical matters — and among those, matters of importance, where both parties, or all parties, can get what they want. A state is of one mind, for instance, when all the citizens agree that offices shall be elective, or that an alliance shall be made with Sparta, or that Pittacus shall govern — Pittacus himself being willing to take the office. But when each man wants the power for himself, like the brothers in Euripides' Phoenissae, there is discord. Being of one mind is not just each thinking of the same thing, whatever it may be, but thinking of it under the same terms — as when the common people and the upper classes both agree that the best men should govern; for then everyone gets what he wants.

Concord, then, appears to be — just as it is called — the friendship of states: it concerns the common interest, and things that bear heavily on how we live.

This kind of concord is found among good men. They are of one mind with themselves and with each other, standing, so to speak, always on the same ground; their wishes hold steady and do not ebb and flow like the Euripus. They wish for what is just and for the common interest, and they work for it together. But men who are not good cannot be of one mind, except to a small degree — just as they cannot be friends except briefly. They strive to take more than their share of the profits while taking less than their share of the labors and public burdens; and each man, while aiming at this for himself, keeps a sharp eye on his neighbor and blocks him from doing the same — for if they are not on guard against each other, the community is ruined. The result is discord: each compelling the other to do what is just while refusing to do it himself.

Chapter 7

Benefactors seem to love the people they have benefited more than the beneficiaries love them back. This looks irrational, and people hunt for the explanation.

Most people think the reason is that the one is a debtor and the other a creditor. Just as, with a loan, the debtor would be glad to see his creditor out of the way while the lender positively worries over his debtor's safety, so — the theory goes — the benefactor wants his beneficiary to stay alive in hopes of a return favor, while the beneficiary is in no hurry to repay.

Epicharmus might perhaps say that people who talk this way are "looking from the cheap seats," but it sounds true to life; most men do have short memories, and are more eager to receive benefits than to confer them.

But the real cause, it would seem, lies deeper in the nature of things, and the creditor's case is not even a true parallel. Creditors feel no affection for their debtors — only a wish that they stay safe long enough to pay. But those who have done a kindness feel real love and affection for those they have benefited, even if the beneficiaries are of no use to them and never will be.

The same thing can be seen in craftsmen: every craftsman loves the work of his own hands more than the work would love him if it came to life. Poets perhaps carry it furthest: they love their own poems to excess, doting on them as if they were their children.

The benefactor's case is like theirs. The person he has benefited is, so to speak, his handiwork; and so he loves his work more than the work loves its maker. The reason is this: existence is something we all desire and love; but we exist in activity — in living and doing; and what a man makes is, in a way, its maker in activity. So he loves his work because he loves existence. And this is simply the nature of things: what a thing is potentially, its work exhibits in activity.

Besides, for the benefactor there is something noble in his act, so that he delights in the person who embodies it; but for the beneficiary there is nothing noble in the benefactor — at most something useful, and that is less pleasant and less lovable.

Again, what is pleasant is the activity of the present, the hope of the future, the memory of the past; and the most pleasant — and most lovable — is what we are actively engaged in. For the benefactor, his deed endures (for the noble is lasting); for the beneficiary, the advantage soon passes away.

Again, the memory of noble deeds is pleasant, the memory of merely profitable ones hardly pleasant at all, or less so — though with expectation the reverse seems true.

Again, loving is like doing something; being loved is like having something done to you. And the ones who have the doer's part in the transaction naturally feel, and show, the more love.

Again, we are all fonder of what has cost us effort — those who made their money love it more than those who inherited it. Now, receiving a benefit costs no labor, while conferring one takes trouble. This is also why mothers love their children more than fathers do: bearing them costs the mother more, and she knows better that they are her own. And that would seem to be the mark of benefactors too.

Chapter 8

Here is another question people raise: ought we to love ourselves most, or someone else?

We criticize, it is said, those who love themselves most, and we call them "self-loving" as an insult. The bad man, it is thought, does everything with an eye to his own advantage — and the worse he is, the more he does so; hence the complaint that he never acts except for himself. The good man, by contrast, is supposed to act because the act is noble — and the better he is, the more the noble is his motive — and for his friend's sake, setting his own interest aside.

But this theory disagrees with the facts, and no wonder. For everyone grants that we ought to love best the man who is most truly a friend; and the truest friend is the one who, in wishing another well, wishes it for the other's own sake, even if no one will ever know. But all these marks of friendship — these and all the others that go into its definition — are found most of all in a man's relation to himself.
