We have already seen that all our friendly relations to others grow out of our relation to ourselves. And the proverbs all point the same way: "Friends have one soul," "Friends hold everything in common," "Equality makes friendship," "The knee is nearer than the shin." Every one of these holds above all of a man's relation to himself, for a man is his own best friend. So, it seems, he ought to love himself most of all.

Naturally people are puzzled about which side to take, since both have their appeal. Perhaps the right method with conflicting claims of this kind is first to pull them apart, and then to determine how far, and in what sense, each is true. So here: if we first find out what "self-loving" means in each claim, the difficulty may clear itself up.

Those who use "self-loving" as a reproach apply it to people who take more than their share of money, honors, and bodily pleasures. These are the things most men crave; they set their hearts on them as the best things in the world, which is why the competition for them is so fierce. Those who grab for more than their share of them are indulging their animal appetites and their passions generally — in a word, the irrational part of their nature. And most men are like this; that is how the word came by its bad sense — from the fact that most self-love is self-love of the bad kind. People who are self-loving in this way deserve the reproach.

That it really is to grabbers of this sort that most people apply the word is easy to show. If what a man always insisted on were that he, rather than anyone else, should do the just thing, or the self-controlled thing, or any other virtuous thing — if, in a word, he were always claiming the noble course for himself — no one would call him self-loving, and no one would blame him.

And yet such a man would seem to be the truer lover of self. At any rate, he assigns the noblest things to himself, the things most truly good; he gratifies the ruling part of himself and obeys it in everything. Just as the ruling part of a state, or of any other system, seems more than anything else to be the state or the system, so the ruling part of a man seems most truly to be the man himself. The man who loves and gratifies this part of himself is the truest lover of self.

Again, we call a man self-restrained or weak-willed according to whether his reason is or is not in command — implying that his reason is his self; and when a man has acted under the guidance of his reason, we say in the fullest sense that he did the deed himself, and did it willingly.

Clearly, then, this part of us is our self, or is most truly our self, and the good man loves it more than anyone else does. So he, above all men, will be self-loving — but in a different sense from the man we reproach, differing from him by the whole distance between living by reason and living by passion, between desiring the noble and desiring what looks profitable.

Those who care beyond other men for noble deeds are welcomed and praised by everyone. And if all men were competing with each other for the noble, each straining every nerve to act as nobly as possible, then the community would have everything it needs, and each man individually would have the greatest of all goods — for that is what virtue is.

The good man, therefore, ought to be self-loving: by doing what is noble he benefits himself and helps others at the same time. The bad man ought not to be: by following his passions, which are not good, he will injure both himself and his neighbors.

With the bad man, then, what he ought to do and what he does pull apart; with the good man, what he ought to do is exactly what he does — for reason always chooses what is best for itself, and the good man obeys his reason.

Again, it is quite true that the good man does much for his friends and for his country, and will even die for them if he must. He will throw away money, honors — in a word, all the goods men fight over — keeping the noble for himself. He would rather have one brief spell of intense joy than a long stretch of mild pleasure; one year of noble living than many years of the ordinary kind; one great and noble deed than many little ones. That, we may say, is what the man gets who gives his life for others: he chooses for himself something noble on a grand scale.

Such a man will hand over wealth to make his friend richer: the friend gets the money, but he gets the noble deed — so the greater good falls to him.

He acts the same way with honors and offices: he will give them all up to his friend, since that, to him, is noble and praiseworthy.

No wonder, then, that we count him good, when he chooses the noble above everything else.

He may even give up an opportunity for action to his friend: it can be nobler to be the cause of your friend's doing a deed than to do it yourself.

In everything for which he is praised, then, the good man plainly assigns the larger share of the noble to himself. In this sense, as we said, a man ought to be self-loving — but not in the sense in which most men are.

Chapter 9

Another disputed question: does the happy man need friends or not?

It is said that the blessed and self-sufficient have no need of friends. They are already stocked with good things; being self-sufficient, they need nothing more; and a friend — being another self — is there to supply what you cannot supply for yourself. Hence the line:

  When the god provides, what need of friends?

But it seems strange, while granting the happy man every good thing, to deny him friends — supposedly the greatest of all external goods.

Besides, if it is more the mark of a friend to confer benefits than to receive them, and if doing good to others is the mark of the good man and of virtue, and if it is nobler to benefit friends than strangers, then the good man will need friends to receive his benefits.

Hence the further question whether friends are more needed in prosperity or in adversity — the thought being that in adversity we need people to help us, and in prosperity people for us to help.

Again, it is surely absurd to make the happy man a hermit. No one would choose to have every good in the world on condition of having it alone; for man is a social being, built by nature to share his life with others. So the happy man must have this good too, since he has everything that is naturally good for man. And obviously it is better to spend one's days with friends and good men than with strangers and whoever happens along. The happy man, then, must have friends.

Then what do the people on the other side mean, and in what sense are they right? Is it that most men take "friends" to mean useful people? Friends of that sort the blessed man certainly will not need, since he already has everything good. Nor will he need — or will need only a little — the friendship based on pleasure: his life is pleasant already and requires no imported pleasure. Because he does not need those kinds of friends, people conclude that he does not need friends at all.

But that conclusion, I think, is false. We said at the outset that happiness is a certain activity, an exercise of our powers; and an activity plainly comes to be in time — it is not a possession, like property acquired once and for all. Now, if happiness consists in living and being active; if the good man's activity is good and pleasant in itself, as we said at the start; if the sense that a thing is our own is itself a source of pleasure; and if we can contemplate our neighbors better than ourselves, and their actions better than our own — then the actions of good men who are his friends are a pleasure to the good man, for they unite both marks of the naturally pleasant. The blessed man, therefore, will need friends of this kind, since he desires to contemplate actions that are good and that are his own — and the actions of a good man who is his friend are both.

Again, the happy man's life is supposed to be pleasant. But if he is solitary, life is hard for him: it is very difficult to keep continuously active all by yourself, and much easier in company and toward others. With friends, then, his activity will be more continuous — and it is pleasant in itself, which is just what the blessed man's life ought to be. For the good man, as good, delights in acts of virtue and is pained by acts of vice, exactly as a musician delights in good music and winces at bad.

Besides, as Theognis says, living with good men gives a kind of training in virtue.

But if we look a little deeper into the nature of things, a good friend appears to be desirable to the good man by nature. Here is the argument.

What is naturally good, we have already said, is good and pleasant in itself to the good man.

Now, life in animals is defined by the power of feeling; in man, by the power of feeling or thought. But a power points to its exercise, and the reality lies in the exercise. So life, in its full reality, seems to be feeling or thinking.

Life, again, is one of the things good and pleasant in themselves; for it is determinate — formed — and the formed belongs to the nature of the good. And what is naturally good is good to the good man; which is why life seems pleasant to everyone. (But we must not take as our sample a bad and corrupt life, or a life of pain; such a life is formless, as is everything in it. We shall try to throw some light on the nature of pain later on.)

Life itself, then, is good and pleasant — as we can also see from the fact that everyone desires it, and the good and blessed most of all; for life is most desirable to them, and their life is the most blessed.

Now, he who sees is aware that he sees; he who hears is aware that he hears; he who walks is aware that he walks. Whatever we do, something in us perceives that we are active; so whenever we feel or think, we are conscious that we feel or think.

But to be conscious that we feel or think is to be conscious that we exist; for our existence, we found, is feeling or thinking.

And consciousness of life is pleasant in itself; for life is naturally good, and to be conscious that a good thing is present is pleasant.

Life, then, is desirable, and most of all desirable to the good man, because his existence is good and pleasant to him: the consciousness of what is good in itself gives him joy.

But the good man stands to his friend as he stands to himself — for his friend is another self. So, just as his own existence is desirable to each man, his friend's existence is desirable too, in the same way or nearly so.

Now, existence, we found, is desirable because of the consciousness that one's self is good — and such consciousness is pleasant in itself.

So the good man needs to be conscious of his friend's existence as well; and this consciousness comes from living together and sharing talk and thought. That, surely, is what "living together" means when we say it of human beings — not grazing in the same field, which is what it means when we say it of cattle.

Put it all together, then. If his own existence is desirable in itself to the good man, being naturally good and pleasant; and if his friend's existence is desirable to him in nearly the same way — then a friend is one of the things desirable for him. But what is desirable for him he ought to have, or else he is incomplete at just that point. Our conclusion, therefore: the man who is to be happy must have good friends.

Chapter 10

Should we make as many friends as possible? Or — as with guest-friends, where we approve the saying "neither a host of guests nor yet none" — is it best in friendship too neither to be friendless nor to have too many friends?

For friends chosen with an eye to usefulness, the saying fits perfectly. Repaying the services of a crowd is troublesome, and life is simply not long enough to do it. More useful friends than one's own life requires are superfluous, and a hindrance to living nobly; so we have no need of them.

Of friends chosen for pleasure, again, a few are enough — as a little sweetening is enough in our diet.

But good men — should we make as many of them our friends as we can, or is there a limit to the size of a circle of friends, as there is to the size of a state? Ten men do not make a state, and a hundred thousand make something that is no longer a state. The right number of citizens is perhaps not one fixed figure, but any number within certain limits. So too with friends there is a limit, and it is, we may say, the largest number one can live with — for living together, as we saw, is one of the most essential marks of friendship, and it is quite plainly impossible to live with a great many people and spread yourself among them all.

Moreover, one's friends must be friends with one another if they are all to spend their days together; and among many, this is hard to arrange.

Again, it becomes hard to share properly in the joys and sorrows of many; you are likely to find yourself called on to rejoice with one and grieve with another at the same moment. Perhaps, then, the best plan is not to collect as many friends as possible, but as many as suffice for a life in common; indeed, an intense friendship with a great number seems impossible.

For the same reason, one cannot be in love with several people at once: erotic love is a sort of superlative friendship, and that is possible only toward one person — just as intense friendship is possible only toward a few.

And this is what we actually find. People are not bound together in crowds by the sort of friendship comrades have; the friendships the poets celebrate are friendships of two. The man with a host of friends, who greets everyone like a brother, seems to be really friends with no one — except in the way fellow-citizens are friends. He is the man we call obsequious.

As fellow-citizens, it is true, one can be friends with a great many people without being obsequious — being, in fact, a genuinely good man. But the friendship that rests on virtue and on the friend's own self cannot be felt for many; we should be well content to find even a few friends of that kind.

Chapter 11

Is it in prosperity or in adversity that we need friends more? We turn to them in both: in misfortune we need help; in prosperity we need people to live with and people to do good to — for we wish to do good.

In adversity, then, the need is more pressing, and what we need there are useful friends. But friendship is a nobler thing in prosperity, and there we seek out good men as friends; for it is more desirable to do good to men like that and to spend our days in their company.

The mere presence of friends is sweet, even in misfortune; our grief is lightened when our friends share it. One might ask whether they literally take up part of it, as of a weight, or whether it is rather that their presence, which is sweet, and the consciousness of their sympathy make the grief smaller. Whether this or something else is the cause of the relief, we need not pursue; the fact, at any rate, is as we said.

But their presence seems to be a mixed thing. On the one hand, the very sight of friends is pleasant, especially in adversity, and does something to ease the grief; for a friend, if he has tact, can comfort us greatly both by his face and by his words — he knows our character, and what pleases us and what pains us. On the other hand, to see a friend grieving over our misfortunes is itself painful, for no one likes being a cause of sorrow to his friends. That is why a man of firm nature is careful not to pass his grief along to his friends: he shrinks from the pain it would give them, unless the relief to himself far outweighs it; and in general he does not allow others to join in his laments, since he is not given to lamenting himself. Weak women, and men who resemble them, delight in people who wail along with them, and love them as friends and fellow-sufferers. But plainly, in everything, our model must be the better man.

In prosperity, again, the presence of friends not only makes the time pass pleasantly but brings with it the consciousness that our friends are pleased at our good fortune. Hence, it would seem, we should be eager to invite our friends to share our prosperity — for readiness to confer benefits is noble — but slow to summon them into our adversity, since we ought to give others as small a share of our evils as possible; whence the saying, "That I suffer is suffering enough." We should be least reluctant to call them in when they can relieve us greatly at little trouble to themselves.

Conversely, when our friends are in trouble, the right course, I think, is to go to them unsummoned and with a will; for it is a friend's part to serve his friend, above all when the friend is in need and has not asked — then it is nobler and pleasanter for both. When they prosper, we should go readily to help in their affairs (for this too is one of the uses of a friend), but not so readily to take a share of their good things; for eagerness to receive benefits is not a noble thing. Still, we must take care that our refusal does not come across as ungracious, as sometimes happens.

The presence of friends, then, in conclusion, is plainly desirable in all circumstances.

Chapter 12

Lovers delight in the sight of each other above everything, and would keep that sense sooner than all the rest, because love owes its being and its origin to it more than to any other. In just the same way, we may venture to say, friends desire nothing so much as to live together. For friendship is, at bottom, community; and a man stands to his friend as he stands to himself. Now, the consciousness of his own existence is desirable to him; so the same consciousness of his friend's existence is desirable too; and it is in a life in common that this consciousness becomes active; so it is natural that friends aim at a life in common.

And whatever existence means for each man — whatever it is that makes life worth choosing for him — in that he wishes to pass his time with his friends. So some drink together, some dice together, others train together, or hunt together, or do philosophy together: each group spending its days in the thing it loves best of everything in life. For, wishing to live in common with their friends, they do the things, and share in the things, which for them make up a life in common.

So the friendship of bad men turns out to be a positively bad thing. Having no stability of character, they confirm each other in pursuits that are not good, and they become positively bad as each grows more like the other. But the friendship of good men is good, and it grows with their companionship. They even seem to become better as they are active together and correct each other's faults; for each molds himself on what he admires in the other — whence the line,

  From good men you will learn good things.

So much, then, for friendship. Our next task is to consider pleasure.

Book X

Pleasure

Chapter 1

Our next business, I think, is to discuss pleasure. For nothing seems more intimately bound up with our nature than pleasure; which is why, in educating the young, we use pleasure and pain as the rudders by which we steer them. And to delight in what we ought to delight in, and to hate what we ought to hate, seems to matter as much as anything for the formation of a virtuous character. These feelings run through the whole of life, and they carry weight enough to pull a man toward virtue and happiness — for we choose what pleases us and shun what pains us.

Surely, then, we cannot pass over these questions, especially since they are so hotly disputed. Some people say that pleasure is the good. Others say the very opposite — that pleasure is thoroughly bad: some of them, perhaps, because they are convinced it really is, others because they think it has a good effect on men's lives to declare pleasure a bad thing even if it is not. Most men, they say, lean toward pleasure and are slaves to it, and so they ought to be pulled in the opposite direction — that way they will be brought to the middle.

But I cannot think this is right. Where feeling and conduct are concerned, words carry less weight than deeds; and when what a man says is contradicted by the plain facts, his words fall into contempt and drag the truth down with them. When a man who denounces pleasure is caught, now and then, desiring it himself, his very attraction to it is taken as proof that he really considers all pleasure desirable — for most men do not draw fine distinctions. True statements, then, seem to be the most useful ones, for practice as well as for theory: because they square with the facts, they are believed, and so they persuade those who understand them to regulate their lives by them. But enough of this; let us now go through the current opinions about pleasure.

Chapter 2

Eudoxus held that pleasure is the good, because he saw that all beings, rational and irrational alike, strive after it. In every case, he said, what is desirable is good, and what is most desirable is best; so the fact that all beings incline toward one and the same thing shows that this thing is best for all. (Each being, after all, finds out what is good for itself — its own food, for instance.) But what is good for all, and what all strive after, is the good.

His arguments won acceptance more because of the excellence of his character than on their own merits; for he was known as a man of remarkable self-control, and so people concluded that he was not saying all this out of love of pleasure — what he said must really be true.

Eudoxus also thought his point could be proved just as clearly from pleasure's opposite: pain, in itself, is something all beings shun; therefore its opposite is desirable for all.

Again, he argued, the most desirable thing is what we choose not on account of something else, but for its own sake; and pleasure is admitted to be like this. Nobody ever asks a man his motive for feeling pleasure — we take it for granted that pleasure is desirable in itself.

Again, he argued that any good thing whatever — just or self-controlled conduct, for instance — becomes more desirable when pleasure is added to it; and only the good can increase the good.

Now, this last argument does seem to show that pleasure is a good thing, but not that it is one bit better than any other good thing; for any good becomes more desirable when another good is added to it than it is by itself.

Indeed, Plato uses an argument of exactly this shape to show that pleasure is not the good. The pleasant life, he says, is more desirable with wisdom than without; but if the combination of the two is better, pleasure itself cannot be the good — for nothing can be added to the good to make it more desirable. And it is equally clear that nothing else can be the good either, if it becomes more desirable when combined with anything that is good in itself. What good is there, then, that cannot be improved by addition, and that men can actually share in? For that is the sort of good we are looking for.

But those who insist, on the other side, that what all beings desire is not good are surely talking nonsense. What everyone thinks, that, we say, is true; and to the man who tells us to put no trust in the opinion of mankind, we reply that we can hardly put more trust in his. If it were only irrational creatures that desired these things, there might be something in the objection; but since rational beings desire them too, how can it be anything but nonsense? And perhaps even in the lower creatures there is some natural principle of good, stronger than they are, that strives after their proper good.

Nor does what Eudoxus' opponents say against his argument from the opposite seem sound. They say that even if pain is bad, it does not follow that pleasure is good; for one bad thing can be opposed to another bad thing, and both of them to some third thing that is neither. Now, this is not a bad point in itself, but it does not hold in the present case. If both were bad, both alike ought to be shunned; if neither were bad, neither should be shunned — or at any rate one no more than the other. But in fact men plainly shun the one as bad and choose the other as good. That, then, is how the two are opposed.

Chapter 3

Again, even if pleasure is not a quality, it does not follow that it is not a good thing. The activity of virtue is not a quality either; neither is happiness itself.

It is objected, next, that the good is determinate while pleasure is indeterminate, because it admits of more and less.

Now, if the evidence for this is that one can be more or less pleased, then the same can be said of justice and the other virtues. We plainly speak of people as being, and showing themselves, more or less virtuous: one man is more just or more brave than another, and it is possible to act more or less justly and with more or less self-control.

But if what they mean is that one pleasure can be more or less of a pleasure than another, I suspect they miss the real reason when they say it is because some pleasures are pure and some mixed. Why should it not be with pleasure as it is with health? Health is something determinate, yet it admits of more and less. The balance of elements that constitutes health is not the same for everyone, nor always the same for the same person; it can vary within certain limits without ceasing to be health, being now more and now less truly health. The same may hold of pleasure.

Again, assuming that the good is complete while motions and comings into being are incomplete, they try to show that pleasure is a motion and a coming into being.

But they do not seem to be right even in calling it a motion. Every motion, it seems, is necessarily quick or slow — either absolutely, like the motion of the universe, or relatively; but pleasure is neither quick nor slow. One can become pleased quickly, just as one can become angry quickly; but the feeling itself cannot be quick — not even relatively, as walking and growing can be. The passage into pleasure, then, may be quick or slow; but the active state itself — the feeling of pleasure — cannot.

And how could pleasure be a coming into being?

It seems that not just anything comes out of just anything; rather, what a thing comes out of is what it dissolves back into. So if pleasure is the coming into being of something, pain must be that thing's dissolution. And indeed they say just this: that pain is a falling short of the natural state, and pleasure its replenishment.

But these are bodily processes. If pleasure is the replenishment of the natural state, then the thing in which the replenishment happens — that is, the body — must be the thing that feels pleasure. And that does not seem to be the case. Pleasure, then, is not the replenishment; rather, while the replenishing goes on we may feel pleasure, as while the depleting goes on we may feel pain.

This whole view of pleasure seems to have been suggested by the pleasures and pains of eating; for there we really do first run short, suffering pain, and then find pleasure in being filled again. But it is not so with all pleasures. The pleasures of the mathematician involve no antecedent pain; nor, among the pleasures of sense, do those of smell; nor do many sights and sounds; nor memories and hopes. Of what, then, are these the coming into being? Nothing has run short here that could be replenished.

To those who point to the disgraceful pleasures as proof that pleasure is not good, we may reply that these things are not pleasant. Granted they are pleasant to ill-conditioned people, we should no more conclude that they are pleasant — except to those people — than we conclude that what is wholesome or sweet or bitter to a sick man is really so, or that a thing is white because it looks white to a man with diseased eyes.

Or we might reply this way: the pleasures are desirable, but not from these sources — just as wealth is desirable, but not at the price of treachery, and health is desirable, but not at the price of eating any abominable food.

Or we might say that pleasures differ in kind. The pleasures that come from noble sources are different from those that come from base ones; you cannot feel the just man's pleasure without being just, or the musical man's pleasure without being musical, and so on through the rest.

The distinction between the true friend and the flatterer seems to show either that pleasure is not good, or else that pleasures differ in kind. The friend, in his company, is thought to have our good in view; the flatterer, our pleasure. And we blame the flatterer, while we praise the friend for having a different aim in keeping our company.

Again, no one would choose to live his whole life with the intellect of a child, even on condition of enjoying a child's pleasures to the fullest possible degree; and no one would choose to get enjoyment by doing some utterly disgraceful act, even if he were never to feel a moment's pain for it.

And there are many things we should care about even if they brought no pleasure — sight, memory, knowledge, the moral and intellectual virtues. Even if we grant that pleasure necessarily accompanies them, it changes nothing; for we should choose them even if no pleasure came of them.

It seems plain, then, that pleasure is not the good, and that not every pleasure is desirable, but that some pleasures are desirable — differing in kind, or in their sources, from those that are not. Let this stand, then, as a sufficient account of the current opinions about pleasure and pain.

Chapter 4

What pleasure is, and what sort of thing it is, we shall discover more readily if we make a fresh start, as follows.

Seeing seems to be complete at any moment: it lacks nothing that could come along afterward to complete its nature. And pleasure seems to resemble seeing in this respect. It is something whole and entire, and at no moment could you find a pleasure that would be made complete by lasting longer.

Therefore pleasure is not a motion. Every motion takes time and aims at an end — the motion of building, for instance — and is complete when it has produced what it was aiming at: complete, that is, in the whole time, or at the final moment of it. While the work is in progress, all the motions are incomplete, and different in kind from the whole motion and from one another. Fitting the stones together is a different thing from fluting the column, and both are different from building the temple. The building of the temple is complete — nothing more is needed to carry out the plan; but the building of the foundation, or of the triglyph, is incomplete, for each is the building of a part only. These motions, then, differ in kind, and you cannot find a motion that is complete in its nature at any given moment — it is complete, if at all, only in the whole time.

The same goes for walking and the other forms of locomotion. All locomotion is motion from one place to another, but there are distinct kinds of it — flying, walking, leaping, and so on. More than that: within walking itself there are differences. The starting point and the destination are not the same for the whole course as for a stretch of it, nor for this stretch as for that; and crossing this line is not the same as crossing that one — for you do not cross a line in the abstract, but a line that lies in a particular place, and this line lies in a different place from that one. For a detailed discussion of motion I must refer you to my other works; but the upshot seems to be that motion is not complete at any moment — its several parts are incomplete and different in kind from one another, the starting point and destination being what makes the difference in kind.

Pleasure, on the other hand, is complete in its nature at any moment. Plainly, then, pleasure and motion must be distinct from each other, and pleasure must belong to the class of things whole and complete. The same conclusion follows from this too: duration is necessary for motion, but not for pleasure — a moment's pleasure is something whole and entire.

From all this it is clear that people are wrong to call pleasure a motion or a coming into being. Those terms do not apply to everything, but only to things that are divisible into parts and are not wholes. There is no coming into being of seeing, or of a mathematical point, or of a unit; none of these is a motion or a coming into being. And the terms are just as inapplicable to pleasure; for pleasure is a whole.

Every sense is active upon its proper object, and its activity is complete when the sense is in good condition and its object is the noblest that falls within its range — for this seems to be what complete activity means. (And let us assume it makes no difference whether we speak of the sense being active or of the person who has it.) For each sense, then, the activity is best when the faculty on the one side is in the finest condition and the object on the other is the finest that falls within that faculty's range.

And this activity will be not only the most complete but also the pleasantest. Every sense has its pleasure in being active, and so do thought and contemplation; and the activity is pleasantest when it is most complete, and most complete when the faculty is in good condition and the object is the worthiest of those that fall within its scope.

Further, the pleasure completes the activity. But it does not complete it in the way the object and the faculty complete it, when both are as they should be — just as health and the doctor are not in the same way the cause of a man's being healthy.

(That every sense has its pleasure is evident: we speak of pleasant sights and pleasant sounds. And it is evident too that the pleasure is greatest when both the faculty and the object it works upon are as good as they can be; when that is true of object and perceiver alike, there will always be pleasure — so long, at least, as there is something to act and something to be acted upon.)

Now, the pleasure completes the activity not in the way the trained habit does, by being present in a man already, but as a sort of completeness laid on top — like the bloom of youth on those in their prime.

So long, then, as the object of thought or of sense is what it should be, and likewise the subject that perceives or contemplates it, there will be pleasure in the activity. For while the thing acted upon and the thing acting remain the same and stand in the same relation to each other, the same result naturally follows.

Why is it, then, that no one can feel pleasure continuously? Probably because we tire. No human faculty is capable of continuous activity; so pleasure cannot be continuous either, since it accompanies the activity. For the same reason some things please us when they are new, and please us less afterward. At first the mind is stimulated and works away at them intensely — just as we strain our eyes to look hard at something; later the effort slackens and relaxes, and the pleasure loses its keenness along with it.

We should expect the desire for pleasure to be shared by all men, seeing that all men desire to live.

For life is a kind of activity, and each man is active with the faculties he loves best upon the things he loves best: the musical man is active with his hearing upon melodies, the lover of learning with his mind upon the objects of study, and so on with the rest.

But pleasure completes these activities, and therefore completes life — which is what men desire.

Naturally, then, men desire pleasure too; for each man finds in it the completion of his life, and life is desirable.

Whether we desire life for the sake of pleasure or pleasure for the sake of life is a question we may set aside for the present. For the two seem to be yoked together and to admit of no separation: without activity there is no pleasure, and pleasure completes every activity.

Chapter 5

From this it seems to follow that pleasures differ in kind. For things that differ in kind are, we believe, completed by things that differ in kind. That is how it stands with the products of nature and of art — animals and trees, paintings, sculptures, houses, furniture. In the same way, then, activities that differ in kind are completed by things that differ in kind.

Now the activities of the intellect differ in kind from the activities of the senses, and the several kinds of each differ from one another; therefore the pleasures that complete them differ too.

The same conclusion follows from the close bond between each pleasure and the activity it completes. For an activity is heightened by its proper pleasure. People who enjoy a thing understand it better and press deeper into it: those who delight in geometry become geometricians, and grasp its propositions better than anyone else; and likewise lovers of music, or of architecture, or of anything else, make progress in their own line of work because they delight in it. The pleasures, then, help the activities grow. But what helps a thing grow must belong closely to it; and where things differ in kind, whatever belongs closely to them must differ in kind as well.

The point comes out still more clearly, perhaps, from the fact that the activity of one faculty is blocked by the pleasure proper to another. A lover of the flute cannot attend to an argument if he hears someone playing nearby; he takes more delight in the flute-playing than in the business at hand, and so the flute-player's pleasure ruins the activity of his reason.

The same thing happens in other cases too, whenever a man is active on two fronts at once: the pleasanter business crowds out the other, and the greater the gap in pleasantness, the harder it crowds — even to the point of shutting the other down altogether. That is why, when something delights us intensely, we cannot do anything else at all; and when we turn to a second thing, it is because we no longer care much about the first. People who eat sweets in the theater do it most when the actors are bad.

Since its proper pleasure sharpens an activity — makes it last longer and go better — while an alien pleasure spoils it, plainly these two pleasures stand far apart. An alien pleasure, in fact, does almost exactly what the activity's own pain does. For an activity is ruined by the pain that arises from it: if a man finds it disagreeable and painful to write or to calculate, he stops writing in the one case and calculating in the other, because the activity hurts. An activity, then, is affected in opposite ways by its proper pleasure and its proper pain — and by "proper" I mean what arises from the activity itself. An alien pleasure, as we have already said, does nearly what the proper pain does: it wrecks the activity, though not to the same degree.

Again, activities differ in goodness and badness — some are to be sought, some shunned, some are neither — and their pleasures differ in just the same way; for each activity has its proper pleasure. The pleasure proper to a good activity is good, and the pleasure proper to a bad one is bad: desire for noble things is praised, desire for base things blamed. And the pleasures that ride along with our activities belong to them even more intimately than the desires do; for the desires are separate from the activity both in time and in nature, while the pleasures come at almost the same moment, and are so hard to pull apart from the activity that men debate whether the activity is not simply identical with the pleasure.

It seems, though, that pleasure is not the same thing as thinking or perceiving — that is impossible; it is because the two cannot be separated that some people fancy they are identical.

As the activities vary, then, so do their pleasures. Sight is purer than touch, hearing and smell purer than taste; and their pleasures differ accordingly. The pleasures of the intellect are purer than these pleasures of sense, and within each class some are purer than others.

Each kind of being, moreover, seems to have its proper pleasure, just as it has its proper function — namely, the pleasure that accompanies the activity of its faculties, the realization of its nature. Look at the animals one by one and you will see it: the pleasures of a horse, a dog, and a man are all different. As Heraclitus says, a donkey would rather have hay than gold; fodder gives a donkey more pleasure than gold does.

The pleasures of beings that differ in kind, then, differ in kind; and we might naturally expect the pleasures of beings of the same kind not to differ. Yet among men, at least, they vary widely. The same things delight one man and pain another; what is grievous and hateful to one is pleasant and lovable to another. It happens even with sweet things: a man in a fever has a different notion of what is sweet from a man in health, and a feeble man a different notion of what is hot from a robust man. And so it goes in other matters too.

But in all matters of this kind we hold that things are what they appear to be to the good man.

If this is right, as we hold it to be — if, that is, the measure of everything is virtue, or the good man as such — then what appears to him to be pleasure will be pleasure, and what he delights in will be pleasant.

If what he finds disagreeable strikes someone else as pleasant, there is nothing astonishing in that; men are corrupted and warped in many ways. Such things are not pleasant — they are pleasant only to these men, in this condition. Plainly, then, the pleasures that everyone admits are base should not be called pleasures at all, except for the corrupt.

But among the pleasures that are considered good, which, or what kind, are to be called the proper pleasures of man? The answer follows from his proper activities, for the pleasures ride along with these. Whether, then, the activities of the complete and happy man are one or many, the pleasures that complete those activities will be the pleasures of man in the full sense of the words; the rest will be so only in a secondary sense and with a fraction of the meaning — just like the activities themselves.

Conclusion

Chapter 6

Now that we have discussed the kinds of virtue and friendship and pleasure, it remains to give a summary account of happiness, since we set that down as the end of everything man does. And it will shorten the work if we first recapitulate what we said above.

We said that happiness is not a habit — not a settled state. If it were, it could belong to a man who slept through all his days and lived the life of a vegetable, or to a man crushed by the greatest misfortunes. Since we cannot accept that, we must place happiness among activities, as we said before. And since some activities are necessary — that is, desirable for the sake of something else — while others are desirable in themselves, plainly happiness must be placed among the activities desirable in themselves, not those desirable for the sake of something else. For happiness lacks nothing; it is self-sufficient.

Now, an activity is desirable in itself when nothing is looked for from it beyond the activity itself.

Two kinds of activity are held to be of this sort: first, virtuous actions, for doing what is noble and excellent must count as desirable for its own sake; and second, the amusements that please us, for these are not chosen for the sake of anything else — indeed men are more often harmed than helped by them, neglecting their health and their fortunes.

Now most of the people whom the world calls happy fill their days with pastimes of this sort. That is why the man with a ready wit at such pastimes is a favorite at a tyrant's court: he makes himself pleasant in exactly what the tyrant wants, and what the tyrant wants is amusement. So amusements pass for ingredients of happiness, because princes spend their leisure on them. But princes, we may venture to say, prove nothing. Princely rank does not carry virtue or reason with it, and virtue and reason are the springs of all excellent activity. And if these men, never having tasted pure and refined pleasure, run to the pleasures of the body, that is no reason to think those pleasures the more desirable; children, too, are sure that what they prize is better than anything else. It is only natural, then, that as children and men differ in their estimate of what is valuable, so do bad men and good.

Therefore — as we have said many times — what is truly valuable and truly pleasant is what is valuable and pleasant to the good man. Now what each man finds most desirable is the activity that fits his own trained character; and so what the good man finds most desirable is activity in accordance with virtue.

Happiness, therefore, does not consist in amusement. Indeed, it would be absurd if the end were amusement — if we toiled and labored our whole life long for the sake of amusing ourselves. For we choose everything, one may say, for the sake of something else — everything except happiness, for happiness is the end. To be serious and to work hard for the sake of amusement seems silly, downright childish; but to amuse ourselves so that we can get back to serious work — that, as Anacharsis put it, seems right. For amusement is a kind of rest, and we need rest because we cannot work continuously.

Rest, then, cannot be the end; for we take it as a means to activity.

Again, the happy life is thought to be the life in accordance with virtue; and such a life is a serious business, not a matter of amusement.

Again, we call serious things better than funny and amusing things, and the better the part or the man, the more serious the function; but we have already said that the activity of the better is higher, and more of the nature of happiness.

Again, bodily pleasures are within anyone's reach — a slave's no less than the best man's; but no one supposes that a slave shares in happiness, seeing that he does not share in the life proper to a man. For happiness does not lie in pastimes of this sort, but in activities in accordance with virtue, as we have already said.

Chapter 7

But if happiness is an activity of the soul in accordance with virtue — or, if there is more than one virtue, in accordance with the best and most complete of them — then it is reasonable to expect that it will be the activity of the highest virtue; and that will be the virtue of the best part of us.

Now, that part or faculty — call it reason or what you will — which seems by nature to rule and to lead, and to grasp things noble and divine, whether it is itself divine or only the most divine element in us: the activity of this part, in accordance with its proper virtue, will be perfect happiness.

That this activity is contemplation we have already said.

And this conclusion would seem to agree both with what we said above and with the truth.

For this activity is the highest possible: reason is the highest thing in us, and of all knowable things, those that reason deals with are the highest.

Again, it is the most continuous; for we can contemplate more continuously than we can do anything else whatever.

We think, too, that pleasure must be one of the ingredients of happiness; and by common consent the pleasantest of all virtuous activities is the activity of wisdom. Philosophy, at any rate, is held to offer pleasures wonderful in their purity and steadiness; and it stands to reason that those who possess knowledge pass their time more pleasantly than those who are still hunting for it.

Again, what we call self-sufficiency will belong most of all to the contemplative life. The necessities of life, true enough, the wise man needs as much as the just man and the rest; but once these are adequately supplied, the just man still needs people toward whom, and along with whom, he can act justly — and so do the self-controlled man and the courageous man and each of the others — while the wise man can contemplate all by himself, and the wiser he is, the more he can do it. He would do it better, we may grant, with fellow workers; but for all that, he is the most self-sufficient of men.

Again, this life alone seems to be desired purely for its own sake. It yields nothing beyond the contemplating itself, while from the practical activities we look to gain something, more or less, beyond the action.

Again, happiness is thought to require leisure; for we work in order to have leisure, just as we make war in order to have peace. Now the practical virtues get their exercise in politics or in war, and these are anything but leisurely occupations.

War, indeed, is the very opposite of leisurely. No one chooses to fight for fighting's sake, or stirs up a war for that purpose; a man who turned friends into enemies just to bring on battles and slaughter would be counted a bloodthirsty monster.

But the politician's life is not leisurely either; and beyond the political work itself, it aims at power and honors — or at least at happiness for the man and his fellow citizens, which is something different from the political activity; for we, in asking what happiness is, also ask what politics is, plainly treating the two as different things.

If, then, among actions in accordance with virtue, the statesman's and the soldier's stand first in nobility and grandeur, and yet they are unleisurely, aim at some further end, and are not desired simply for themselves; while the activity of reason seems to surpass them in seriousness, since it is contemplation of truth, and to aim at no end beyond itself, and to have its proper pleasure — which itself heightens the activity — and to be self-sufficient, and leisurely, and unwearying so far as anything human can be, and to have every other mark that we assign to happiness: then this, the activity of reason, will be the complete happiness of man — provided it is granted a complete span of life; for nothing incomplete has a place in happiness.

But a life of this kind would be something more than human. A man will live it not insofar as he is a man, but insofar as there is something divine in him; and as far as this divine element surpasses our compound human nature, so far does its activity surpass the activity of the other kind of virtue — the practical, moral kind.

If, then, reason is divine compared with man, the life of reason is divine compared with human life. And we should not listen to those who advise us, being men, to think human thoughts, and, being mortal, mortal thoughts. We ought, so far as in us lies, to put on immortality, and strain every nerve to live by the best thing in us; for small though it may be in bulk, in power and in worth it far surpasses all the rest.

Indeed, this part would even seem to be each man's true self, since it is the sovereign and better part of him. It would be strange, then, if a man chose to live not his own life, but the life of something else.

And here what we said before applies again: what is proper to each being by nature is best and pleasantest for it. For man, then, the life of reason is best and pleasantest, since reason more than anything else is the man. And this life, therefore, is also the happiest.

Chapter 8

The life that consists in the activity of the other kind of virtue — moral virtue — is happy only in a secondary sense; for the activities of moral virtue are emphatically human, not divine. Justice, I mean, and courage and the other moral virtues show themselves in our dealings with one another: in contracts and services and outward acts of every sort, where we observe what is due in each case, and in our inward feelings too. And all of this is emphatically human business.

Again, moral virtue seems in some respects to be actually a product of our physical constitution, and in many respects to be closely bound up with the passions.

Again, practical wisdom is inseparably yoked to moral virtue, and moral virtue to practical wisdom, since the moral virtues fix the principles from which practical wisdom starts, while practical wisdom fixes what is right in morals.

But the moral virtues, being bound up with the passions, must belong to our compound nature — the union of soul and body — and the virtues of the compound nature are emphatically human. Therefore the life that expresses them, and the happiness that consists in that life, must be emphatically human too.

The happiness that consists in the activity of reason, on the other hand, stands apart from our lower nature. (So much we may be allowed to assert about it; a detailed discussion would take us beyond our present purpose.)

Further, this happiness would seem to need only a small supply of external goods — certainly less than the moral life needs. Both need the necessaries of life to the same extent, let us say; for though the statesman does in fact take more care of his body than the philosopher does, the difference will be trifling. But in what they need for their activities the difference will be great. The generous man will need money, if he is to act generously; the just man will need it to pay what he owes — for mere wishes cannot be tested: even unjust men pretend they wish to act justly. The courageous man will need strength, if he is to perform any act of courage; and the man of self-control will need the liberty to indulge — for how else can he, or the possessor of any other virtue, show what he is?

Again, people dispute whether the purpose or the action is more essential to virtue, since virtue implies both. Plainly, completeness requires both. But action needs many things, and the greater and nobler the action, the more it needs.

The man engaged in contemplation, by contrast, needs none of these things for his work; indeed, one may even say they get in the way of his contemplating. But insofar as he is a man living among men, he chooses to act virtuously; and so he will need such things in order to live a human life.

That perfect happiness is some kind of contemplative activity may also be shown in the following way.

We always suppose the gods to be the most blessed and happy of all beings; but what kind of actions shall we ascribe to them? Acts of justice? Surely it is ridiculous to picture the gods making contracts and returning deposits and so on. Acts of courage, then — enduring terrors and facing danger because it is noble to do so? Or acts of generosity? But to whom are they to give? And is it not absurd to imagine that they have money, or anything of that kind? And what could acts of self-control possibly mean for them? Surely it would be an insult to praise the gods for having no evil desires. Run through the whole list, and you will find that everything about action is petty and unworthy of gods.

And yet everyone supposes that the gods live, and therefore that they are active; we can hardly suppose they lie asleep like Endymion.

Now, if a being lives, and action cannot be ascribed to it, still less production, what is left but contemplation? It follows, then, that the divine life, which surpasses all others in blessedness, is a life of contemplation.

Of all human activities, therefore, the one most akin to this will be capable of the greatest happiness.

And this is further confirmed by the fact that the other animals have no share in happiness, being wholly incapable of this kind of activity. The life of the gods is blessed through and through; the life of man is blessed just so far as it attains some likeness of this activity; but none of the other animals is happy, since none of them can contemplate at all.

Happiness, then, extends exactly as far as contemplation does: the more contemplation in a life, the more happiness — not by accident, but because happiness necessarily accompanies contemplation; for contemplation is precious in itself.

Our conclusion, then, is that happiness is a kind of contemplation.

But since we are men, we shall need external good fortune as well; for our nature does not by itself supply everything contemplation requires. The body must be healthy, and fed, and otherwise cared for. Still, we must not suppose that because happiness is impossible without external goods, the man who is to be happy will need many of them, or much. Independence does not depend on a superabundance of good things, and neither does action; a man can do noble deeds without being ruler of land and sea. A moderate equipment of fortune is enough to give a man scope for virtuous action — as we can easily see, for private citizens seem to do what is right not less than princes, but rather more — and that much is all he needs; for that man's life will be happy who has virtue and exercises it.

Solon too, I think, described the happy man well when he said that, in his view, it was a man moderately supplied with the gifts of fortune who had done the noblest deeds and lived with self-control; for a man of modest means can do his duty.

Anaxagoras also seems to have held that the happy man is neither a rich man nor a prince; he said he would not be surprised if the happy man struck the masses as a strange choice, for they judge by externals, which are all they can appreciate.

The opinions of the wise, then, seem to agree with our theory. Such opinions carry some weight; but in practical matters the test of truth lies in the facts of life, for it is there that the supreme authority resides. So we should test everything we have said against the facts of life: if it agrees with the facts, we may accept it; if it clashes with them, we must set it down as mere theory.

But, once more, the man who exercises his reason and cultivates it, and keeps it in the best condition, seems also to be the most beloved of heaven. For if the gods take any care for human affairs, as they are thought to do, it is reasonable to suppose that they delight in what is best in us and most akin to themselves — that is, in reason — and that they reward those who love and honor it most, as men who care for what is dear to the gods and act rightly and nobly. And plainly all this belongs, above all, to the wise man. The wise man, therefore, is the most beloved of heaven — and therefore, we may conclude, the happiest.

In this way too, then, the wise man will be happier than anyone else.

Chapter 9

Now that we have said enough — in summary form, at least — about these matters, and about the virtues, and about friendship and pleasure too, are we to suppose that we have reached the end we proposed? Surely not. The old saying holds good: in practical matters the end is not to know what should be done, but to do it. It is not enough to know about virtue, then; we must try to possess it and to use it, or take whatever other road may make us good.

Now, if theories had power by themselves to make us good, then, in Theognis' words, "many and great would be the rewards they deserved," and we ought to provide them. But in fact, though theories seem strong enough to guide and stimulate generous-minded young men — though a well-bred character with a sincere love of the noble may be opened by them to the influence of virtue — they are powerless to turn the mass of men toward goodness. For most men are naturally governed by fear rather than by reverence: they abstain from evil not because it is foul but because of the punishment it brings. Living under the sway of their passions, they pursue the pleasures that suit their nature and the means to those pleasures, and avoid the opposite pains; while of the noble and the truly pleasant they have not so much as a notion, since they have never tasted it.

What theory or argument, then, could bring men like these to order? It is impossible, or at least very difficult, to argue away what has long been ingrained in the character. For my part, I think we must be well content if, when everything that seems to make men good is present, we get some modicum of virtue.

Now, what makes men good is held by some to be nature, by others habit — that is, training — by others instruction.

As for the goodness that comes by nature, it is plainly not in our power; it is bestowed by some divine agency on those who truly deserve to be called fortunate.

As for theory and instruction, I fear they cannot avail in every case. The hearer's soul must first have been prepared by training — taught to feel delight and aversion on the right occasions — just as soil must be prepared if the seed is to thrive. A man who lives at the mercy of his passions will not listen to the arguments meant to turn him back, and will not even understand them. When he is in that state, how is argument to change his mind? To put it bluntly, passion seems to yield not to reason but only to force. The character, then, must already be there, somehow akin to virtue — loving what is noble and hating what is base.

But it is hard to get right guidance toward virtue from our youth up unless we are brought up under suitable laws; for a life of self-control and steadiness is not pleasant to most men, least of all to the young. Our upbringing, then, should be fixed by law, and our whole way of life with it; it will stop being painful as it becomes habitual. And I venture to think it is not enough to get the right nurture and training while we are young: since we must keep up the same way of life as grown men, and confirm the habits, we need the intervention of the law in these matters too — and indeed, to put it bluntly, over the whole of life. For most men obey compulsion more readily than reason, and fear of punishment more readily than desire for the noble.

That is why some hold that the legislator should first invite men to virtue, exhorting them for the nobility of it — those who have been well trained will listen; but that when men will not listen, or are of baser nature, he should apply correction and punishment, and banish the incorrigible altogether. The good man, they say, whose guide is the noble, will obey reason; but the man who is not good, whose desires are set on pleasure, must be corrected by pain, like a beast of burden. And for the same reason, they say, the pains applied must be those most contrary to the pleasures the offender loves.

As we have said, then, the man who is to be good must be well nurtured and trained, and must afterward continue in the same excellent way of life, doing nothing vile either willingly or unwillingly; and this can be brought about only if men live subject to some kind of reason and right order — backed by force.

Now, a father's rule has neither the force nor the power of compulsion required, nor does the rule of any individual, unless he is a king or something like one. But law has compulsory power, and at the same time it is a rational ordinance proceeding from a kind of practical wisdom, from reason. And whereas we resent individuals who oppose our inclinations, even when they are right to oppose them, we do not feel aggrieved when the law bids us do what is right.

Yet Sparta is the only state, or nearly the only one, where the legislator seems to have attended to the nurture and way of life of the citizens. In most states these matters are wholly neglected, and every man lives as he pleases, laying down the law for wife and children like the Cyclops.

The best thing, then, would be for the state to take these matters in hand and manage them rightly. But since the state neglects them, it would seem to fall to each of us individually to help our own children and friends along the road to virtue — and we should have the power to do it, or at least the will.

From what has been said, it would seem that our best plan for this is to learn the art of legislation. Training on the public scale is plainly carried on by means of laws, and it is good where the laws are good; and it would seem to make no difference whether the laws are written or unwritten, or whether they regulate the education of one person or of many — no more than it matters in music or gymnastics or any other course of training. For just as law and custom prevail in the state, so in the household the father's word and the family's ways prevail — no less, indeed even more, because of the ties of kinship and of benefits received; for affection and obedience are implanted in the members of a family by nature from the start.

Moreover — despite what has just been said — individual treatment is better than treatment in the mass, in education no less than in medicine. As a general rule, rest and fasting are good for a fever patient; in a particular case they may not be. A boxing master, I suppose, does not prescribe the same style to every pupil. It would seem, then, that individuals are educated more precisely under private instruction; for then each gets exactly what he needs.

But you will treat an individual case best — whether as doctor, or trainer, or anything else — when you know the general rule: "such-and-such is good for all men," or "for all men of a certain temperament." For the sciences are said to deal, and do deal, with what is common to many individuals.

I do not deny that a particular person may be treated quite well by someone with no scientific knowledge at all, if experience has taught him precisely how particular causes affect that one man — just as some men seem to be their own best doctors, though quite unable to prescribe for anyone else.

Nevertheless, I venture to say that a man who wishes to master an art, or to grasp it scientifically, must go on to its general principles and get to know them by the proper method; for, as we have said, it is with universals that the sciences deal.

And so the man who wants to make men better by training — whether many men or few — should try to acquire the art or science of legislation, assuming that men can be made good through law. To mold rightly the character of whoever presents himself is not a thing that just anybody can do; if anyone can, it is the man who knows — just as in medicine, and in every other profession that calls for careful treatment and practical wisdom.

Our next business, then, is to ask: from whom, and by what means, are we to learn the art or science of legislation?

"As we learn the other arts," someone will say — "from the men who practice it, the politicians; for we found that legislation is a branch of statesmanship."

But I think the case of statesmanship is not quite like that of the other sciences and arts. In the others, the same men plainly teach the art and practice it — physicians and painters, for instance. But with statesmanship, the sophists profess to teach it, while it is never they who practice it, but the statesmen. And the statesmen would seem to act by some instinctive gift, proceeding by experience rather than by reasoning. For plainly they never write or speak about these matters (though that might be a better use of their time than composing speeches for the courts and the assembly), and they have never made statesmen of their own sons or of any of their friends. Yet we should expect them to have done so if they could; for they could have left no better legacy to their country, and there is nothing they would sooner have chosen for themselves — and therefore for those dearest to them — than this power.

Experience, however, we must allow, does seem to be of great service here; otherwise men would never become statesmen by mere familiarity with politics. Those who want a knowledge of statesmanship, then, seem to need experience as well as theory.

But those sophists who profess to teach statesmanship turn out to be ludicrously far from doing it. To put it bluntly, they do not even know what statesmanship is or what it deals with. If they knew, they would not treat it as identical with rhetoric, or as something lower than rhetoric; nor would they think it easy to frame a code of laws by collecting the laws that are most admired. "It is only a matter of picking out the best," they say — ignoring the fact that the selection itself requires understanding, and that to judge correctly is here, as in music, a matter of the greatest difficulty. In every field it is the men of special experience who judge the products rightly, who understand how and by what means each is brought about, and which combinations are harmonious; the inexperienced must be content if they can tell whether the result is good or bad — as with painting. Now, laws are, so to speak, the products of statesmanship. How, then, should a collection of laws make a man able to legislate — or even to pick out the best of them?

Even medicine, it seems, cannot be taught from handbooks. And yet the medical handbooks try to tell you not only the remedies but how to apply them, and how to treat each class of patient, sorting them by temperament. All this may be serviceable to men of experience; to those who know nothing of medicine it would seem to be useless.

So too, I think we may say, collections of laws and constitutions may be very serviceable to those who are able to examine them with a discriminating eye — to judge which ordinances are good and which bad, and which fit together with which. But those who go through such compendia without the trained faculty cannot judge them properly (unless a correct judgment comes of itself), though they may perhaps sharpen their wits on these matters.

Since our predecessors, then, have left the subject of legislation unexamined, it will perhaps be better to look into it ourselves — and indeed into the whole question of the management of a state — so that our philosophy of human life may be completed as far as it lies in our power to complete it.

Let us try, then, first of all, to review whatever sound observations our predecessors have made on this or that branch of the subject; and then, with our collection of constitutions before us, let us ask what things preserve and what things destroy states, what preserves and destroys each kind of constitution, and why some states are governed well and others badly. When we have gained an insight into all this, we shall perhaps be better able to see what the best kind of constitution is, and how each kind should be arranged, and what system of laws and customs suits each best.

Let us begin, then.
